<Platform 'Amiga'>
Amiga
1985-07-23 00:00:00
Commodore Electronics Ltd.
1285.00

<h2>Overview</h2><p>The first Amiga computer model, the A1000, was released in 1985 by <a href="/commodore/3010-2000/" data-ref-id="3010-2000">Commodore Inc.</a></p><p>The design team was headed up by Jay Miner and development of the system began in 1982 under the Amiga Corporation company. Commodore acquired Amiga Corporation in the summer of 1984, snatching the Amiga chipset from under the nose of <a href="/atari/3010-182/" data-ref-id="3010-182">Atari</a>, who originally funded the project. Commodore filed for bankruptcy in 1994, despite the success of the A1200 and CD32.</p><h2>Hardware</h2><p>The Amiga range was based on the Motorola 68000 chipset, but made use of proprietary graphics and sound co-processors (codenamed Denise and Paula respectively), which took the load off of the CPU and allowed the Amiga to process more than just gaming graphics extremely efficiently. The Amiga revolutionized video production, providing an affordable CG solution with the <a href="http://www.newtek.com" rel="nofollow" data-ref-id="false">Newtek</a> Video Toaster (which later evolved into the Newtek TriCaster, the core of Giant Bomb's video production). It was the first consumer grade computer to be able to sample and record stereo audio in 44.1khz (CD quality) at the time. Over the lifetime of the computer, three graphics and sound chipsets were used: OCS (Original ChipSet), ECS (Enhanced ChipSet) and AGA (Advanced Graphics Architecture).</p><h2>Models</h2><table><thead><tr><th scope="col">Released</th><th scope="col">Model</th><th scope="col">Codename</th><th scope="col">Chipset</th><th scope="col">Memory</th><th scope="col">Kickstarter</th><th scope="col">Variants</th><th scope="col">Colours</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td><p>July 23 1985</p></td><td><p><strong>Amiga 1000</strong></p></td><td>Lorraine</td><td><p>OCS</p></td><td><p>256k onboard, upgradable to 512k</p></td><td><p>v1.0 - included 20MB or 40MB hard drive</p></td><td><p>64 on screen,</p><p>4096 palette</p></td></tr><tr><td><p>September 1992</p></td><td><p><strong>Amiga 4000</strong></p></td><td><p>Pandora</p></td><td><p>AGA</p></td><td><p>2MB onboard, upgradable to 16MB</p></td><td><p>v3.0, in ROM</p></td><td><p><strong>A4000T</strong> - Tower case, 68040 CPU</p></td><td><p>4096 on screen,</p><p>262,144 palette</p></td></tr><tr><td><p>October 1992</p></td><td><p><strong>Amiga 1200</strong></p></td><td><p>Channel Z</p></td><td><p>AGA</p></td><td><p>2MB onboard, upgradable to 8MB</p></td><td><p>v3.0, in ROM</p></td><td><p><strong>CD32</strong> - Console variant. CD-ROM, no keyboard, no floppy disk drive, no upgradable RAM (codename "Spellbound")</p></td><td><p><span>4096 on screen,</span></p><p>262,144 palette</p></td></tr></tbody></table><h2>Operating System</h2><p><b>AmigaOS </b></p><p>The AmigaOS originally was split into two parts, the <em>Kickstarter</em> and the <em>Workbench</em>.</p><p>The Amiga 1000 required a Kickstarter disc to boot before any other disc could be loaded. The benefits of this were easy OS upgrades (v1.0-1.3), however it also provided an easy vector for the first Amiga viruses to spread. The Amiga 500 onwards stored the Kickstarter (v1.2+) in ROM, thereby eliminating the need for an initial disc boot.</p><p>The Workbench was an optional windowing system and file manager, similar to Mac OS or Windows. Unlike those systems, games could launch at boot instead of the Workbench in order to give them larger access to system resources.</p>

<h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942953-database_hardware_gameboy01.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-942953" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.1285714285714" data-width="350" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 350px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:112.9%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942953-database_hardware_gameboy01.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-942953"><img alt="The Game Boy" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942953-database_hardware_gameboy01.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942953-database_hardware_gameboy01.jpg 350w" sizes="(max-width: 567px) 100vw, 567px" data-width="567"></a><figcaption>The Game Boy</figcaption></figure><p>The <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a> Game Boy was one of the first commercially viable handheld game systems on the market being released in 1989. The Game Boy was released in <a href="/north-america/3035-1664/" data-ref-id="3035-1664">North America</a> in August, 1989, in <a href="/japan/3035-37/" data-ref-id="3035-37">Japan</a> on April 21, 1989, and in <a href="/europe/3035-289/" data-ref-id="3035-289">Europe</a> in September 1990. The Game Boy was the first in the Game Boy line, a line that lasted over fifteen years until the release of the <a href="/nintendo-ds/3045-52/" data-ref-id="3045-52">Nintendo DS</a>. It was created by the brilliant <a href="/gunpei-yokoi/3040-45151/" data-ref-id="3040-45151">Gunpei Yokoi</a>, as well as the same team that had developed Nintendo's <a href="/game-watch/3025-1183/" data-ref-id="3025-1183">Game &amp; Watch</a> series. The Game Boy pack-in game was <a href="/tetris/3030-1896/" data-ref-id="3030-1896">Tetris</a>, a little known puzzle game from <a href="/russia/3035-38/" data-ref-id="3035-38">Russia</a> that would help lead the Game Boy to its massive success. <a href="/minoru-arakawa/3040-19267/" data-ref-id="3040-19267">Minoru Arakawa</a> and several employees from Nintendo of America went to Russia to sign a deal to get the game on their system, they saw it as the equivalent to packing in <a href="/super-mario-bros/3030-15544/" data-ref-id="3030-15544">Super Mario Bros</a> with the <a href="/nes/3045-21/" data-ref-id="3045-21">NES</a>. Despite the Game Boy's handicap of being able to only display two colors, something that was technically inferior to what other handhelds was offering at the time it was massively more successful. Part of this was due to the fact that the batteries for Game Boy lasted a significantly longer times than those used for other full color handhelds, due to the fact that it used four AA batteries. 1998 saw Nintendo introduce the Game Boy's successor the <a href="/game-boy-color/3045-57/" data-ref-id="3045-57">Game Boy Color</a>; the two systems games were actually compatible with each other, with the entire Game Boy line up playable on the Game Boy Color and a select few Game Boy Color titles playable on the original Game Boy (although not in color). Backwards compatibility became a stable of Nintendo handhelds, Game Boy classic games were playable up until the release of the Nintendo DS.</p><p>In 1994 Nintendo released an adapter for the <a href="/snes/3045-9/" data-ref-id="3045-9">Super Nintendo</a> entitled the <a href="/super-game-boy-enhancement/3015-4804/" data-ref-id="3015-4804">Super Game Boy</a>, which allowed Super Nintendo players to play Game Boy games on their TV with a limited amount of colors. The adapter did not require the owner to own the original Game Boy system, just the adapter; despite this Game Boy remained as popular as ever. The first variation of the Game Boy, the Game Boy Pocket arrived in 1996. It was slimmer, more compact and the first in what would become a Nintendo tradition of releasing multiple variations of their handhelds. The Game Boy managed to sell its shipment of a million units within weeks in the <a href="/the-united-states-of-america/3035-396/" data-ref-id="3035-396">United States</a> and its initial shipment of 300,000 units in Japan in two weeks and reached 500,000 sales in Japan by July. The Game Boy and the color has sold 118.69 million combined worldwide and the Game Boy had sold 66.42 million alone before the release of the color. Twenty years after its introduction, in 2009 the Game Boy was inducted into the Nintendo toy hall of fame.</p><h2>Competition</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/130768/1859791-nintendoad29.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1859791" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.3" data-width="800" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 800px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:130.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/130768/1859791-nintendoad29.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1859791"><img alt="An ad for the Atari Lynx that portrays the Gameboy as underpowered." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/130768/1859791-nintendoad29.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/130768/1859791-nintendoad29.jpg 800w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/13/130768/1859791-nintendoad29.jpg 738w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/130768/1859791-nintendoad29.jpg 492w" sizes="(max-width: 492px) 100vw, 492px" data-width="492"></a><figcaption>An ad for the Atari Lynx that portrays the Gameboy as underpowered.</figcaption></figure><p>When it launched in 1989, the Game Boy entered a handheld battle against the <a href="/lynx/3045-7/" data-ref-id="3045-7">Atari Lynx</a> and <a href="/game-gear/3045-5/" data-ref-id="3045-5">Sega Game Gear</a>. Both the Lynx and Game Gear were technologically impressive for the time. They featured full color backlit screens and, in the case of the Lynx, significantly more processing power. Atari and Sega attempted to capitalize on their products' capabilites in advertisements that portrayed the Game Boy as weak and childish. A memorable Sega ad from the time proclaimed that "The Sega Game Gear separates the men from the boys". Despite their marketing efforts, the Lynx and Game Gear could not stop the Game Boy's dominance. Their weak battery life (a Game Gear or Lynx could deplete six AA batteries in 4-6 hours), combined with lackluster software libraries and higher prices, proved fatal. </p><h2>Hardware Revisions</h2><p>There was three different versions of the Game Boy that was released during its life. The Game Boy Light however was only released in Japan.</p><h3>Game Boy</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-392624" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.99148936170213" data-width="470" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 470px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:99.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-392624"><img alt="Game Boy Classic" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg 470w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Game Boy Classic" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg 470w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/392624-123950_gadget26_gameboy_b.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Game Boy Classic</figcaption></figure><p>The Game Boy was released on April 21, 1989 in Japan and then later in August for North America. The classic Game Boy ran on 4 AA batteries which powered the system for around 35 hours. Despite its size and less than perfect monochromatic screen the Game Boy lasted seven years until it received its first update. The Game Boy was only available in grey, in 1995 a "Play it Loud" line of the handheld was available in red, green, black, yellow, blue, white and transparent though this line had the exact same specifications. This set a trend of Nintendo handheld's being released in multiple colours. The link cable port is larger than the rest of the Game Boy line which makes it unable to link with all future models of the Game Boy.</p><p>RAM: 8 kB internal S-RAM</p><ul><li>Video RAM: 8 kB internal</li><li>ROM: 8 Mb cartridges</li><li>Sound: 2 Square Waves, 1 programmable 32-sample 4-bit PCM Wave, 1 White noise, and one audio input from the cartridge.</li><li>Display: Reflective LCD 160 x 144 pixels</li><li>Screen Size: 66 mm (2.6 in) diagonal</li><li>Power: 4 AA Batteries</li><li>Dimensions: 148 x 89 x 32 mm</li><li>Weight: 394g (with batteries)</li></ul><h3>Game Boy Pocket</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/api/image/original/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1777455" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="1600" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 1600px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/api/image/original/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1777455"><img alt="Game Boy Pocket" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 1600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Game Boy Pocket" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 1600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777455-gameboy_pocket.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Game Boy Pocket</figcaption></figure><p>The Game Boy Pocket was originally released July 21, 1996 in Japan and then later released in North America in September 3, 1996. The Game Boy Pocket was smaller, lighter version of the original Game Boy designed to be able to easily fit in your pocket. An additional plus point of the system was that it only needed 2 AAA batteries, though this only powered the system for 10 hours. The Pocket featured a re-designed smaller link port, this version of the port was later used for all later models in the Game Boy line. Another improvement was an improved monochrome screen which did not blur up like on the original. The Game Boy Pocket like the original had no blacklight for darkened area and the first run did not feature a LED to indicate power levels, though due to public outcry a LED was added. The Pocket has been released in silver/chrome, red, yellow, green, black, transparent, pink and the original grey. Some limited editions of the handheld were also released.</p><ul><li>CPU: Custom 8-bit Sharp LR35902 core at 4.19 MHz</li><li>RAM: 8 kB internal S-RAM</li><li>Video RAM: 8 kB internal</li><li>ROM: 8 Mb cartridges</li><li>Sound: 2 Square Waves, 1 programmable 32-sample 4-bit PCM Wave, 1 White noise, and one audio input from the cartridge.</li><li>Display: Reflective LCD 160 x 144 pixels</li><li>Screen Size: 66 mm (2.6 in) diagonal</li><li>Power Supply: 2 AAA Batteries</li><li>Dimensions: 124 x 76 x 23 mm</li><li>Weight: 148g (with batteries)</li></ul><h3>Game Boy Light</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/api/image/original/1777456-gbl.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1777456" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.99277456647399" data-width="692" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 692px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:99.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/api/image/original/1777456-gbl.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1777456"><img alt="Game Boy Light" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg 692w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Game Boy Light" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg 692w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777456-gbl.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Game Boy Light</figcaption></figure><p>The Game Boy Light was a Japan exclusive variation of the handheld that was released on April 14, 1998, due to its Japan only release it is one of the rarest Game Boy models. The main selling point was it's indigo coloured backlight that allowed for play in dark environments. The Light ran on two AA batteries and lasted for 20 hours with the light off and 12 with it on. Amazingly none of the consoles that followed the Game Boy Light featured a back lit screen until the 2003 release of the <a href="/game-boy-advance/3045-4/" data-ref-id="3045-4">Game Boy Advance SP</a>. The Game Boy Light was only available in Gold and Silver though numerous special editions were released. The Game Boy Light was never released outside of Japan due to the imminent release of the Game Boy Color and Japanese thinking that western gamers wanted color not light.</p><ul><li>CPU: Custom 8-bit Sharp LR35902 core at 4.19 MHz</li><li>RAM: 8 kB internal S-RAM</li><li>Video RAM: 8 kB internal</li><li>ROM: 8 Mb cartridges</li><li>Sound: Mono</li><li>Display: LCD (Passive, monochrome, backlit) 160 x 144 pixels</li><li>Screen Size: 2.56 inches</li><li>Power Supply: 2 AA Batteries</li></ul><h2>Launch Titles</h2><h3>Japan</h3><ul><li><a href="/alleyway/3030-4381/" data-ref-id="3030-4381">Alleyway </a></li><li><a href="/baseball/3030-8145/" data-ref-id="3030-8145">Baseball</a></li><li><a href="/super-mario-land/3030-19834/" data-ref-id="3030-19834">Super Mario Land</a></li><li><a href="/yakuman/3030-29144/" data-ref-id="3030-29144">Yakuman</a></li></ul><h3>North America</h3><ul><li><a href="/alleyway/3030-4381/" data-ref-id="3030-4381">Alleyway</a></li><li><a href="/baseball/3030-8145/" data-ref-id="3030-8145">Baseball</a></li><li><a href="/super-mario-land/3030-19834/" data-ref-id="3030-19834">Super Mario Land</a></li><li><a href="/tennis/3030-20780/" data-ref-id="3030-20780">Tennis</a></li><li><a href="/tetris/3030-1896/" data-ref-id="3030-1896">Tetris</a></li></ul><h3>Europe</h3><ul><li><a href="/tetris/3030-1896/" data-ref-id="3030-1896">Tetris</a></li><li><a href="/super-mario-land/3030-19834/" data-ref-id="3030-19834">Super Mario Land</a></li><li><a href="/alleyway/3030-4381/" data-ref-id="3030-4381">Alleyway</a></li><li><a href="/baseball/3030-8145/" data-ref-id="3030-8145">Baseball</a></li><li><a href="/tennis/3030-20780/" data-ref-id="3030-20780">Tennis</a></li></ul><h2>Features</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942954-picture_6.png" data-ref-id="1300-942954" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.1351351351351" data-width="148" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 148px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:113.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942954-picture_6.png" data-ref-id="1300-942954"><img alt="A Game Boy cartridge" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 148px) 100vw, 148px" data-width="148" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942954-picture_6.png" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942954-picture_6.png 148w"><noscript><img alt="A Game Boy cartridge" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942954-picture_6.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/942954-picture_6.png 148w" sizes="(max-width: 148px) 100vw, 148px" data-width="148"></noscript></a><figcaption>A Game Boy cartridge</figcaption></figure><p>The Game Boy's control was very similar to that of the original Nintendo Entertainment System. It had a D-pad, A and B buttons, and a Select and Start button. On the side of the Game Boy was a volume control, a contrast control, a link cable port, and a jack for rechargeable batteries (instead of just the regular batteries). There was a sliding on-off switch on the top of the handheld a slot for the cartridges, the classic Game Boy cartridges are usable in the entire Game Boy line. The link cable port could also be used for the Game Boy Printer. The link cable was originally designed to allow for head to head multiplayer though <a href="/satoshi-tajiri/3040-46953/" data-ref-id="3040-46953">Satoshi Tajiri's</a> wildly successful <a href="/pokemon/3025-452/" data-ref-id="3025-452">pokemon</a> series revolutionised multiplayer handheld gaming with its focus on trading rather than competitive multiplayer.</p><h2>Top Ten Best Selling Games</h2><ol><li><a href="/tetris/3030-1896/" data-ref-id="3030-1896">Tetris</a> (35 Million) *Bundled with the system.</li><li><a href="/pokemon-redblue/3030-3966/" data-ref-id="3030-3966">Pokemon Red/Blue/Green</a> (20.68 Million Approx)</li><li><a href="/super-mario-land/3030-19834/" data-ref-id="3030-19834">Super Mario Land</a> (18.06 Million)</li><li><a href="/super-mario-land-2-6-golden-coins/3030-14425/" data-ref-id="3030-14425">Super Mario Land 2: 6 Golden Coins</a> (11.09 Million)</li><li><a href="/pokemon-yellow-special-pikachu-edition/3030-18673/" data-ref-id="3030-18673">Pokemon Yellow</a> (8.86 Million Approx)</li><li><a href="/the-legend-of-zelda-links-awakening/3030-3645/" data-ref-id="3030-3645">The Legend of Zelda: Link's Awakening</a> (6.05 Million Approx)</li><li><a href="/dr-mario/3030-17641/" data-ref-id="3030-17641">Dr Mario</a> (2.08 Million)</li><li><a href="/wario-land-super-mario-land-3/3030-19225/" data-ref-id="3030-19225">Super Mario Land 3: Wario Land</a> (1.59 Million Approx)</li><li><a href="/kirbys-dream-land-2/3030-4474/" data-ref-id="3030-4474">Kirby's Dream Land 2</a> (1.49 Million Approx)</li><li><a href="/kirbys-dream-land/3030-19788/" data-ref-id="3030-19788">Kirby's Dream Land</a> (1.45 Approx)</li></ol><h2>Accessories</h2><h3>Game Link Cable</h3><p>The game link cable allows for interaction between multiple Game Boy's in certain games. Originally designed for the multiplayer battle mode in Tetris, its most popular use was the transferring of data in <a href="/pokemon-redblue/3030-3966/" data-ref-id="3030-3966">pokemon</a>. The game link cable for the original Game Boy was not compatible with any future variations of the Game Boy without the use of the adapter.</p><h3>Game Boy Printer</h3><p>The Game Boy printer was released in 1998, it was compatible with the Game Boy, Pocket, Color and Advance. It is designed to work in conjunction with the Game Boy Camera, though some Game Boy and GBC games were also usable. It needs 6 AA batteries to run and uses special 3.8 cm Game Boy printer paper.</p><h3>Game Boy Camera</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1777605" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.77777777777778" data-width="450" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 450px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:77.8%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1777605"><img alt="Game Boy Camera" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg 450w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Game Boy Camera" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg 450w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/11/112517/1777605-gameboycamera.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Game Boy Camera</figcaption></figure><p>In 1998 Nintendo released the Game Boy camera, it was compatible with the Game Boy, Color, Pocket and Advance. The camera could only take black and white pictures and when used in conjunction with the game boy printer you could print your pictures out. The Game Boy camera was aimed at children who could not afford their own camera, the Camera also had some special features and mini-games to play. The Game Boy Camera was launched in five different colors blue, green, red, yellow and clear purple. There was also some limited editions released such as The Legend of Zelda and Pokemon editions.</p><h3>Super Game Boy</h3><p>Nintendo released the Super Game Boy accessory in 1994 for $60. The Super Game Boy allows Game Boy games to be played on your TV screen by using it with your Super Nintendo. It was also compatible with Game Boy Color cartridges, any original Game Boy cartridges you played with your Super Game Boy would still be in black and white, though some later games were programmed to show up in color and display a border around the game.</p>

<h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705634-810156_gba_logo_super.png" data-ref-id="1300-1705634" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.40042826552463" data-width="467" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 467px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:40.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705634-810156_gba_logo_super.png" data-ref-id="1300-1705634"><img alt="Game Boy Advance Logo" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1705634-810156_gba_logo_super.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705634-810156_gba_logo_super.png 467w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1705634-810156_gba_logo_super.png 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>Game Boy Advance Logo</figcaption></figure><p>The Game Boy Advance was originally launched in <a href="/japan/3035-37/" data-ref-id="3035-37">Japan</a> on March 21, 2001 and then in <a href="/north-america/3035-1664/" data-ref-id="3035-1664">North America</a> on June 11. It is the third platform in the <a href="/game-boy/3045-3/" data-ref-id="3045-3">Game Boy</a> hand-held line and like it's predecessors, the Game Boy Advance established itself as the leader in the portable market. The Game Boy Advance continued the <a href="/game-boy-color/3045-57/" data-ref-id="3045-57">Game Boy Color</a> trend of offering different colors and special branded limited editions. It also established the trend of a periodic hardware redesign (see below), which was carried on with the <a href="/nintendo-ds/3045-52/" data-ref-id="3045-52">Nintendo DS</a> and DS Lite. The Game Boy Advance series has had sales of over 81 million worldwide, approx 43 million of which are Game Boy Advance SP units.</p><h2>Launch Titles</h2><h3>Japan</h3><ul><li><a href="/gt-advance-championship-racing/3030-14360/" data-ref-id="3030-14360">Advance GTA</a></li><li><a href="/castlevania-circle-of-the-moon/3030-13197/" data-ref-id="3030-13197">Akumajo Dracula: Circle of the Moon</a></li><li><a href="/super-dodge-ball-advance/3030-13484/" data-ref-id="3030-13484">Bakunetsu Dodgeball Fighters</a></li><li><a href="/boku-wa-koukuu-kansei-an/3030-13321/" data-ref-id="3030-13197">Castlevania: Circle of the Moon</a></li></ul><h2>Games</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873717-6a00d83452033569e200e54f27386f8834_800wi.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-873717" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75333333333333" data-width="300" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 300px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873717-6a00d83452033569e200e54f27386f8834_800wi.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-873717"><img alt="A typical Game Boy Advance cartridge." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873717-6a00d83452033569e200e54f27386f8834_800wi.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873717-6a00d83452033569e200e54f27386f8834_800wi.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300"></a><figcaption>A typical Game Boy Advance cartridge.</figcaption></figure><p>The Game Boy Advance's hardware was very similar to the <a href="/snes/3045-9/" data-ref-id="3045-9">SNES</a>'s, which made it perfect for sprite-based games. It had many ports of SNES titles, including a large number of RPGs. As well as being known for its SNES remakes and great original content a lot of original <a href="/nes/3045-21/" data-ref-id="3045-21">NES</a> games were made available to play on the move thanks to the <a href="/classic-nes-series/3015-3653/" data-ref-id="3015-3653">NES Classic's Series</a>. The Game Boy Advance is regarded as an incredibly popular console among old school gamers and many call it <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo's</a> best handheld in terms of games. The last game to be release for the Game Boy Advance was <a href="/samurai-deeper-kyo/3030-28897/" data-ref-id="3030-28897">Samurai Deeper Kyo</a> which was released on February 12, 2008 in North America. As of March, 2009 there has been over 377 million GBA games sold worldwide.</p><h3>Best Selling Games Worldwide</h3><ol><li><a href="/pokemon-rubysapphire/3030-10122/" data-ref-id="3030-10122"><i>Pokmon Ruby</i>/<i>Sapphire</i></a> (13 million)</li><li><a href="/pokemon-fireredleafgreen/3030-14522/" data-ref-id="3030-14522"><i>Pokmon FireRed</i>/<i>LeafGreen</i></a> (11.82 million)</li><li><i>Pokmon Emerald</i> (6.32 million)</li><li><i>Super Mario World: Super Mario Advance 2</i> (4.179 million approx)</li><li><i>Super Mario Advance</i> (3.938 million approx)</li><li><a href="/mario-kart-super-circuit/3030-16769/" data-ref-id="3030-16769"><i>Mario Kart: Super Circuit</i></a> (3.768 million approx)</li><li><a href="/super-mario-bros-3-super-mario-advance-4/3030-20748/" data-ref-id="3030-20748"><i>Super Mario Advance 4: Super Mario Bros. 3</i></a> (3.698 million approx)</li><li><i>Namco Museum</i> (2.96 million in US)</li><li><i>Pokmon Pinball: Ruby &amp; Sapphire</i> (2.5 million)</li><li><i>Pokmon Mystery Dungeon: Red Rescue Team</i> (2.2 million)</li></ol><h2>Hardware Revisions</h2><p>There were three different models offered during the lifespan of the Game Boy Advance.</p><table><tbody><tr><th>Game Boy Advance (GBA)</th></tr><tr><td>The original Game Boy Advance was released with a horizontal design. This model is backwards compatible with games for the Game Boy and Game Boy Color. Many gamers complained that the screen was not bright enough on this model and many third party lighting accessories were developed, this issue was fixed in the next iteration.</td><td><figure Reflective TFT Color LCD</li><li>Display Size: 1.6" by 2.4" (40.8 by 61.2 mm)</li><li>Resolution: 240 x 160 pixels</li><li>Colors: 512 simultaneous colors from a palette of 32,768</li><li>Size: 3.2 by Advance SP not only saw a major change with its flip design, but also added a built-in (but removeable, with tools) rechargeable battery and front mounted light. Like the original GBA, the SP is backwards compatible with all Game Boy and Game Boy Color games. This version did not have a standard headphone jack, and users dissatisfied by the small built-in speaker needed either proprietary Nintendo headphones or an adapter. In September of 2005, a back-lit model of the SP was released. This version of the hardware is recognizable by the model number AGS-101.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8272/1352103-gbasp_famicom.jpg" 99.99</li></ul></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>Game Boy Micro</th></tr><tr><td>Tiny in size, the third design borrowed from the then-popular cell phone trend of having interchangeable face-plates. Featuring a brighter screen than the SP (with 5 levels of adjustable brightness) and a standard headphone jack (found at the bottom of the unit), but with an external port unique to the Micro which did not accept a standard GBA link cable (though a cable exclusive to the micro was released) and able to play Game Boy Advance games only, this version of the platform never achieved the success of the previous two versions.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9983/680978-micro.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-680978" data-size="small" $99.99</li></ul></td></tr></tbody></table><h2>Official Nintendo Accessories</h2><p>Many accessories were created for the Game Boy Advance line.</p><table data-max-width="true"><thead><tr><th scope="col">Game Link Cable</th><th scope="col"> </th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>This 4-port cable allowed between 2 and 4 Game Boy Advance units to communicate for multiplayer games or to share or trade data. The game that is best known for its use of this accessory is <a href="/the-legend-of-zelda-a-link-to-the-past-four-swords/3030-35064/" data-ref-id="3030-35064">The Legend of Zelda: A Link to the Past &amp; Four Swords</a>, which is the GBA remake of the SNES classic.</td><td><figure data-align="right" data-size="medium" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/16/164924/2486361-gba_link_cable_en.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2486361" data-ratio="0.8" data-width="155" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 155px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:80.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/16/164924/2486361-gba_link_cable_en.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2486361"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 155px) 100vw, 155px" data-width="155" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/16/164924/2486361-gba_link_cable_en.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/16/164924/2486361-gba_link_cable_en.jpg 155w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/16/164924/2486361-gba_link_cable_en.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/16/164924/2486361-gba_link_cable_en.jpg 155w" sizes="(max-width: 155px) 100vw, 155px" data-width="155"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table data-max-width="true"><thead><tr><th scope="col">Nintendo Gamecube Game Boy Advance Link Cable</th><th scope="col"> </th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>This cable allowed you to use your GBA with certain Gamecube games, such as <a href="/the-legend-of-zelda-four-swords-adventures/3030-7856/" data-ref-id="3030-7856">The Legend of Zelda: Four Swords Adventures</a>, <a href="/pac-man-vs/3030-12338/" data-ref-id="3030-12338">Pac-Man VS</a> and <a href="///">Final Fantasy: Crystal Chronicles</a>, as a controller (with screen!) for your Gamecube. Some other games also used the accessory for side featrures, for example being able to travel to</td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table><table data-max-width="true"><thead><tr><th scope="col">Game Boy Wireless Adapter</th><th scope="col"> </th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>The wireless adapter was a great alternative to the wired Game Link cable, but only worked with certain games designed specifically for it. As it was released relatively late in the handhelds life less than 20 games supported it the most pominent tat offered support was <a href="/pokemon-fireredleafgreen/3030-14522/" data-ref-id="3030-14522">Pokemon FireRed/LeafGreen</a> which had the adapter included with the game.</td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table><table data-max-width="true"><thead><tr><th scope="col"><a href="/e-reader/3015-3782/" data-ref-id="3015-3782">e-Reader</a></th><th scope="col"> </th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>This optical scanner was released in 2001 and co-created with Olympus Optical, Ltd. It worked with specially printed dot-patterned cards which could be used to unlock bonus content in games, most notably <a href="/super-mario-bros-3-super-mario-advance-4/3030-20748/" data-ref-id="3030-20748">Super Mario Bros. 3: Super Mario Advance 4</a> (which had the most ambitious e-Reader release of all, with multiple packs of cards adding items, levels, and even "professional" replays), or to play entire games, especially retro titles like the <a href="/game-watch/3025-1183/" data-ref-id="3025-1183">Game and Watch</a> series, or Nintendo Arcade Classics, like the original <a href="/donkey-kong/3030-311/" data-ref-id="3030-311">Donkey Kong</a>. The device was inserted into the cartridge slot of the Game Boy Advance, (shown here to the right), and featured a pass-through port to allow connection of multiple Game Boy Advance units using the standard Game Link Cable. The e-Reader served as a bootable cartridge, and had onboard memory to store one game at a time, but for most other features more than one GBA was required, since there was no other way for data to be transferred to a game cartridge, severely limiting its usefulness.</td><td><figure data-align="right" data-size="small" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1124594" data-ratio="0.7" data-width="400" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 400px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:70.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1124594"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg 400w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg 400w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1124594-gba_e_reader.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a></figure><p> </p></td></tr></tbody></table><table data-max-width="true"><thead><tr><th scope="col">Game Boy Advance SP Headphone Jack Adapter</th><th scope="col"> </th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>This short cable connected to the charging port of the SP and allowed the user to connect a standard 3.5mm headphone jack instead of the charger. Many third parties improved on this design by allowing both the headphone jack and the charger to be used simultaneously.</td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table><table data-max-width="true"><thead><tr><th scope="col">Game Boy Player</th><th scope="col"> </th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>This device allowed you to play GBA games on your television by connecting it to your <a href="/gamecube/3045-23/" data-ref-id="3045-23">GameCube</a>. The device installed semi-permanently to the bottom of the Gamecube and booted with a standard Gamecube disc. The Game Boy Advance cartridges were inserted in the front of the device, visible in the center of the bottom left-hand side of the picture (right).</td><td><figure data-align="right" data-size="medium" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9983/680979-player.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-680979" data-ratio="0.75641025641026" data-width="546" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 546px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.6%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9983/680979-player.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-680979"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9983/680979-player.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9983/680979-player.jpg 546w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9983/680979-player.jpg 480w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9983/680979-player.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9983/680979-player.jpg 546w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9983/680979-player.jpg 480w" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480"></noscript></a></figure><p> </p></td></tr></tbody></table>

Game Gear

<h2>Overview</h2><p><a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62"> Sega</a> Game Gear came out in the U.S. in 1991. Work originally began on the console in 1989 under the codename "Project Mercury", following Sega's policy at the time of codenaming their systems after planets. Like the <a href="/sega-master-system/3045-8/" data-ref-id="3045-8"> Sega Master System</a>, it was an <a href="/8-bit/3015-5951/" data-ref-id="3015-5951"> 8-bit</a> system. It was only the third commercially available color handheld console, after the <a href="/lynx/3045-7/" data-ref-id="3045-7"> Atari Lynx</a> and the TurboExpress. .It could run on 6-AA batteries or an AC adapter could be used to plug the GG into the wall or outlet. It came packaged with <em><a href="/columns/3030-5487/" data-ref-id="3030-5487"> <i>Columns</i></a></em> a "<a href="/tetris/3030-1896/" data-ref-id="3030-1896"> Tetris</a> type" game, in which you have to match the colored blocks as opposed to making lines.</p><p>The GG had many accessories. One of them was a TV tuner, which no longer works as of February 2000. Rechargable battery packs to help make the GG portable and not make you keep buying AA batteries every 2 hours, master gear converter to play Master System cartridges on the GG, a car adapter for playing in the car without batteries, and much more.</p><p>Originally only available in black, the GG was available in other colors. A Japan only release of an ultra-limited (10,000 units) white console in a special metal game case and TV Tuner was available soon after launch. Later a special blue sports themed version and a Coca-cola Red version were available in the US.</p><p>The Game Gear was built as a "landscape" hand-held. This meant that the D-pad and buttons were to the sides of the screen as opposed to being at the bottom like the <a href="/game-boy/3045-3/" data-ref-id="3045-3"> Game Boy</a>. This made the GG a lot more comfortable to hold because you hands were not so close and crammed together. The Game Gear ultimately played second fiddle to the Game Boy and despite being more popular in Europe it still became the first Sega product to be beaten in sales by a competing <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90"> Nintendo</a> product in the region.</p><h2>Launch</h2><h3>Japan - October 6, 1990</h3><ul><li><a href="/columns/3030-5487/" data-ref-id="3030-5487"> Columns</a></li><li><a href="/pengo/3030-4196/" data-ref-id="3030-4196"> Pengo</a></li><li><a href="/super-monaco-gp/3030-532/" data-ref-id="3030-532"> Super Monaco GP</a></li></ul><h3>North America - April 26, 1991</h3><ul><li><a href="/castle-of-illusion-starring-mickey-mouse/3030-9513/" data-ref-id="3030-9513"> Castle of Illusion starring Mickey Mouse</a></li><li><a href="/columns/3030-5487/" data-ref-id="3030-5487"> Columns</a></li><li><a href="/g-loc-air-battle/3030-13742/" data-ref-id="3030-13742"> G-Loc</a></li><li><a href="/psychic-world/3030-10932/" data-ref-id="3030-10932"> Psychic World</a></li><li><a href="/wonder-boy/3030-5220/" data-ref-id="3030-5220"> Revenge of Drancon</a></li><li><a href="/super-monaco-gp/3030-532/" data-ref-id="3030-532"> Super Monaco GP</a></li></ul>

<Platform 'Genesis'>
Genesis
1988-10-29 00:00:00
Sega
189.00
40000000
<h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084124-800px_sega_genesis_original_model.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084124" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.72875" data-width="800" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 800px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:72.9%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084124-800px_sega_genesis_original_model.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084124"><img alt="The Sega Genesis Console." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084124-800px_sega_genesis_original_model.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084124-800px_sega_genesis_original_model.jpg 800w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/8153/1084124-800px_sega_genesis_original_model.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084124-800px_sega_genesis_original_model.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>The Sega Genesis Console.</figcaption></figure><p>Released as the Sega Mega Drive in <a href="../../japan/95-37/" rel="nofollow">Japan</a>, <a href="../../europe/95-289/" rel="nofollow">Europe</a>, <a href="/south-america/3035-2906/" data-ref-id="3035-2906">South America</a>, and <a href="/oceania/3035-2907/" data-ref-id="3035-2907">Oceania</a>, and as the Sega Genesis in <a href="/north-america/3035-1664/" data-ref-id="3035-1664">North America</a>, it is <a href="../../sega/65-62/" rel="nofollow">Sega</a>'s 16-bit console. It was the successor of the 8-bit <a href="../../sega-master-system/60-8/" rel="nofollow">Sega Master System</a> and predecessor to the 32-bit <a href="../../saturn/60-42/" rel="nofollow">Sega Saturn</a>.</p><p>The console hardware had both a 16-bit Motorola 86000 for the main console CPU, and 8-bit Zilog Z80 CPU for the sound chip. Both CPU chips were commonly used together in Sega<a href="/arcade/3045-84/" data-ref-id="3045-84"> arcade</a>  cabinets at the time--as well as arcade cabinets in general during the late 80s and early 90s. Sega's goal was to give home consumers a taste of arcade quality gameplay, made popular by their System-16 arcade cabinets, where hit games like <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/golden-axe/3030-14351/" data-ref-id="3030-14351">Golden Axe</a>, <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/altered-beast/3030-2536/" data-ref-id="3030-2536">Altered Beast</a>, and <a href="/alien-syndrome/3030-22009/" data-ref-id="3030-22009">Alien Syndrome </a>were frequently played in the arcades. An image from the video game Altered Beast was even used as the console box art, to help market it as a 16-bit arcade console. The console was not as powerful as their System-16 and future 16-bit arcade cabinets, however. The console used a 9-bit color palette, compared to a 16-bit color palette used in their early 16-bit arcade cabinets. The console could only display 80 sprites on screen, compared to the 128 sprites from their System 16 cabinets. Sega also used a lower grade version of the sound chips used in most arcade cabinets at the time. The basic structure, however, allowed for many good (but not perfect) arcade ports of their games, as well as games from other developers.</p><p>It had a production run lasting from 1988, when it was first released in Japan, to 1997, when it was finally discontinued. It sold <a href="http://vgsales.wikia.com/wiki/Fourth_generation_of_video_games" rel="nofollow">over 40 million units</a> worldwide, over half of which were sold in <a href="/north-america/3035-1664/" data-ref-id="3035-1664">North America</a> alone. While its performance in Japan was underwhelming, coming third place behind both the <a href="/snes/3055-2795/" data-ref-id="3055-2795">SNES</a> and <a href="/turbografx-16/3045-55/" data-ref-id="3045-55">PC Engine</a>, the Mega Drive was Sega's most successful console worldwide, outselling the SNES in the overseas territories of North America, Europe, South America, Oceania, and <a href="/south-korea/3035-2410/" data-ref-id="3035-2410">South Korea</a>.</p><p>The console's best-selling stand-alone game was <a href="../../sonic-the-hedgehog-2/61-1662/" rel="nofollow"><i>Sonic the Hedgehog 2</i>,</a> with over 6 million cartridges sold, while its best-selling bundled game was the original <em><a href="/sonic-the-hedgehog/3030-2465/" data-ref-id="3030-2465">Sonic the Hedgehog</a></em>, with over 15 million units sold.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084009-sonic_the_hedgehog_2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084009" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.7" data-width="640" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 640px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:70.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084009-sonic_the_hedgehog_2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084009"><img alt="Sonic the Hedgehog proved Blast Processing was real." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084009-sonic_the_hedgehog_2.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084009-sonic_the_hedgehog_2.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/8153/1084009-sonic_the_hedgehog_2.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084009-sonic_the_hedgehog_2.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>Sonic the Hedgehog proved Blast Processing was real.</figcaption></figure><p>To make it seem cooler than its main rival, the <a href="../../snes/60-9/" rel="nofollow">Super Nintendo Entertainment System</a> (SNES), Sega's Marketing Department came up with the term "Blast Processing" to imply that the Genesis/Mega Drive had more power and was more capable of rendering sprites on screen faster than the SNES. While "Blast Processing" was purely a marketing term, the Genesis/Mega Drive did in actuality run at a higher clock speed than the SNES, enabling many games to feature fast scrolling (often with many background layers in parallax) and a large number of sprites on screen.</p><p>During its nine-year production run, it was expanded upon numerous times. These add-ons included the <a href="../../power-base-converter/59-23/" rel="nofollow">Power Base Converter</a>, allowing it to play 8-bit Master System games, as well as the <a href="../../sega-cd/60-29/" rel="nofollow">Sega CD</a> and the <a href="../../sega-32x/60-31/" rel="nofollow">32X</a>. Additionally, two later models of the Genesis were released.</p><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084017" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.66666666666667" data-width="450" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 450px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:66.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084017"><img alt="Six Button Controller." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg 450w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Six Button Controller." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg 450w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084017-genesisb.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Six Button Controller.</figcaption></figure><p>The Genesis had two main versions of its controller. The first was slightly larger than the other, with three face buttons and a slightly "sharp," hard-plastic thumb pad. Although this controller was initially well-received, as the popularity of games like <a href="../../street-fighter-ii/61-243/" rel="nofollow">Street Fighter II</a> rose, there was demand to produce a six-button controller.</p><p>Sega finally did release a six-button controller with a slightly streamlined look and feel, a softer thumb pad, and naturally three more face buttons. Many later Genesis games recommended the use of this secondary controller (many of them fighting games), as games that were played with the original controller and designed for the new six-button one would need to tap the start button to switch the three face buttons to the function of the other three.</p><h2>Accessories</h2><figure data-align="right" data-size="small" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2720463" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="600" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 600px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2720463"><img alt="XE-1AP Controller" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg 600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="XE-1AP Controller" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg 600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2720463-4113433581-XE1AP.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>XE-1AP Controller</figcaption></figure><p>The Sega Mega Drive/Genesis supported the following accessories:</p><ul><li><strong><a href="/x-e-1-a-p/3000-80/" data-ref-id="3000-80">XE-1AP</a></strong>, released by <a href="/dempa-micomsoft/3010-4743/" data-ref-id="3010-4743">Dempa Micomsoft</a> only for Japan in 1989. It featured an analog thumbstick, analog slider, four shoulder buttons, and two grip handles. It was supported by <a href="http://www.giantbomb.com/after-burner-ii/3030-2139/" data-ref-id="3030-2139">After Burner II</a> (1990), <a href="http://www.giantbomb.com/ayrton-sennas-super-monaco-gp-ii/3030-8935/" data-ref-id="3030-8935">Ayrton Senna's Super Monaco GP II</a> (1992), Fastest 1 (1991), <a href="http://www.giantbomb.com/galaxy-force-ii/3030-8320/" data-ref-id="3030-8320">Galaxy Force II</a> (1991), and <a href="http://www.giantbomb.com/musha/3030-7928/" data-ref-id="3030-7928">Musha Aleste</a> (1990).</li><li><strong><a href="/sega-activator/3000-8/" data-ref-id="3000-8">Sega Activator</a></strong>, released by Sega in 1993. It allowed full-body <a href="/motion-control/3015-474/" data-ref-id="3015-474">motion control</a>, but had limited motion detection.</li><li><strong><a href="/sega-vr/3000-17/" data-ref-id="3000-17">Sega VR</a></strong>, planned for release in 1994 but eventually cancelled. It was a <a href="/virtual-reality/3015-421/" data-ref-id="3015-421">virtual reality</a> headset with <a href="/head-tracking/3015-606/" data-ref-id="3015-606">head-tracking</a> capabilities.</li></ul><h2>Network and Cable Support</h2><p>In Japan, the Sega Mega Drive had network service available beginning in 1991. In order to use the service, players had to attach a Mega modem (which had a connection speed ranging from 1,600 to 2,400 bits/s) to the DE-9 port on the console. The few games available for download onto the Mega Drive were <a href="../../sonic-eraser/61-23076/" rel="nofollow">Sonic Eraser</a> and <a href="../../phantasy-star-ii-text-adventures/61-24979/" rel="nofollow">Phantasy Star II Text Adventures</a>. In 1995, the service reached Brazil, where two more games were available: <a href="../../mortal-kombat-ii/61-3571/" rel="nofollow">Mortal Kombat II</a> and <a href="../../fifa-soccer-95/61-12888/" rel="nofollow">Fifa Soccer '95</a>. The service was not successful, and was eventually discontinued.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/959869-sega_channel_1jpgjpg.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-959869" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.5862068965517" data-width="145" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 145px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:158.6%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/959869-sega_channel_1jpgjpg.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-959869"><img alt="The Sega Channel provided games directly to your Sega Genesis." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 145px) 100vw, 145px" data-width="145" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/959869-sega_channel_1jpgjpg.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/959869-sega_channel_1jpgjpg.jpg 145w"><noscript><img alt="The Sega Channel provided games directly to your Sega Genesis." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/959869-sega_channel_1jpgjpg.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/959869-sega_channel_1jpgjpg.jpg 145w" sizes="(max-width: 145px) 100vw, 145px" data-width="145"></noscript></a><figcaption>The Sega Channel provided games directly to your Sega Genesis.</figcaption></figure><p>Despite this, Sega teamed up with TCI and <a href="../../time-warner-interactive-inc/65-468/" rel="nofollow">Time Warner</a> in 1994 to release the Sega Channel in the <a href="../../the-united-states-of-america/95-396/" rel="nofollow">United States</a>. The Sega Channel was a hardware add-on meant to be placed on top of the Genesis cartridge slot. The add-on allowed players to download games directly to their Sega Genesis consoles and play the games for a monthly fee. They did this by connecting a coaxial cable (the same kind used on cable boxes) to the back of the cartridge add-on. One could then choose to browse games from a variety of menus. Categories like shooter, role playing, platforming, etc. contained many popular Genesis titles people could download and play. After 30 days the games available would change. Usually, the most popular games were kept in service while the games people hardly played were replaced with other games. The Sega Channel also kept track of new games that were released. The games were usually available a month after their official release dates. Japanese import games were also available to subscribers. The activation fee was $29.95, and the monthly fee was $14.95. The service lasted until 1998, when the 32-bit era was established with the Sega Saturn, and the <a href="../../playstation/60-22/" rel="nofollow">Sony PlayStation</a>. The Sega Channel was also the only way American gamers could play the Genesis last two best games, <a href="../../alien-soldier/61-17008/" rel="nofollow">Alien Soldier</a> and <a href="../../pulseman/61-3637/" rel="nofollow">Pulseman</a>.</p><h2>Sega Genesis Nomad</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1096731" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.70857142857143" data-width="350" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 350px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:70.9%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1096731"><img alt="The Sega Nomad, a portable version of the Genesis." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg 350w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="The Sega Nomad, a portable version of the Genesis." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg 350w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/18617/1096731-sega_nomad_2s.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>The Sega Nomad, a portable version of the Genesis.</figcaption></figure><p>The Sega Nomad was a failed handheld version of the Genesis console. Released in October of 1995 at a price of $180 USD, the Nomad featured a 3.25 inch color screen and ran on six AA batteries.</p><p>Interestingly, the Nomad was a fully functional Genesis home console in addition to being a handheld device. Television connectivity was provided through an A/V slot on top of the unit. For single player games, the Nomad itself functioned as the controller, but two player gaming was possible using a controller port on the bottom of the device. This port accepted standard Genesis controllers.</p><p>The Nomad suffered from problems that plagued Sega's other handheld, the 8-bit <a href="/game-gear/3045-5/" data-ref-id="3045-5">Game Gear</a>. Both machines were notoriously power-hungry thanks to their (relatively) powerful processors and high quality backlit LCD screens. The Nomad was capable of depleting its batteries in two hours, which lessened its appeal as a portable device. Additionally, despite being advertised as a wholly-capable Genesis console, the Nomad was functionally incompatible with the various Genesis add-ons Sega had released such as the <a href="/sega-cd/3045-29/" data-ref-id="3045-29">Sega CD</a> and <a href="/sega-32x/3045-31/" data-ref-id="3045-31">32X</a>. These compatibility problems were due to the shape of the Nomad, and the inability to mount these accessories on its portable form factor.</p><p>Faced with competition from Nintendo's hugely successful Game Boy, the Nomad faltered at market. By the time Sega ceased production, it had only managed to sell 1 million units.</p><h2>Launch Titles</h2><h3>United States</h3><p>The Sega Genesis launched along with the following 7 games in the US August 14th 1989.</p><ul><li><a href="../../space-harrier-ii/61-12420/" rel="nofollow">Space Harrier II</a></li><li><a href="../../thunder-force-ii/61-12471/" rel="nofollow">Thunder Force II</a></li><li><a href="../../tommy-lasorda-baseball/61-11264/" rel="nofollow">Tommy Lasorda Baseball</a></li><li><a href="../../last-battle/61-12187/" rel="nofollow">Last Battle</a></li><li><a href="../../altered-beast/61-2536/" rel="nofollow">Altered Beast</a></li><li><a href="../../alex-kidd-in-the-enchanted-castle/61-4968/" rel="nofollow">Alex Kidd in The Enchanted Castle</a></li><li><a href="../../ghouls-n-ghosts/61-12381/" rel="nofollow">Ghouls n' Ghosts</a></li></ul><h2>Hardware Specifications</h2><h3>CPU (Central Processing Unit)</h3><ul><li>Main Processor Unit: Motorola 68000 at 7.67 MHz</li><li>Secondary Processor Unit: Zilog Z80 at 3.58 MHz</li></ul><p><strong>Note: </strong>The Zilog Z80 is used to control the sound chip.</p><h3>Graphics</h3><ul><li>Maximum Color Palette: 512 (9-bit color)<br /><ul><li>64 colors on screen at once</li></ul></li><li>Maximum Sprites On Screen: 80</li><li>Screen Resolution: 256 x 192 - 320 x 448</li><li>Possible AV Outputs <ul><li>RF (Radio Frequency Unit with Coaxial Cable)</li><li>Composite (RCA Cable)</li><li>S-Video (Separate Video Cable for Model 3 and Custom Units),</li><li>Component (RGB Cable)</li></ul></li></ul><h3>Sound</h3><ul><li>Yamaha 2612 Sound Chip <ul><li>6 Channel FM (Frequency Modulation) at 7.67 MHz</li></ul></li><li>Texas Instrument Programmable Sound Generator (version Ti-SN76489) <ul><li>4 Channels at 3.58 MHz</li></ul></li><li>Sample Rate 22 KHz</li></ul><p><b>Note:</b> The sixth FM channel on the YM-2612 can be used as a 8-bit PCM. Doing so disables the FM channel.</p><p><strong>Note: </strong>The original sample rate of the YM-2612 was 52KHz, however the sound chip was compressed down to 22KHz on the original Model 1 Genesis. Model 2 had both compression and a filter added to mask the distortion caused by lowering the original sample rate. Unfortunately, this didn't work. This is why you hear little distortion on the Model 1 Genesis, and a lot of distortion on the Model 2 version, which eliminated the direct headphone link to the sound chip, and relied on stereo RCA cables instead. Later revisions fixed some of the sound issues.</p><p><b>Note:</b> The PSG Ti-SN76489 was the same sound chip used in the Sega Master System. This allowed backwards compatibility with the Genesis. This option, however, was abandoned by the time Model 2 was released and became the norm.</p><h3>Memory</h3><ul><li>Main Memory: 64KB</li><li>Video Memory: 64KB</li><li>Sound Memory: 8KB (from Z80)</li></ul><h3>Nomad Specifications:</h3><table><tbody><tr><td>Processor:</td><td>Motorola 68000 16 bit processor running at 7.67 MHz</td></tr><tr><td>Co-processor (Sound Controller):</td><td>Zilog Z80 8-bit at 3.58 MHz</td></tr><tr><td>Memory:</td><td>156KB total - 64 KB Main RAM, 64KB VRAM, 8KB Sound RAM. 20 Kb ROM</td></tr><tr><td>Display Palette:</td><td>512</td></tr><tr><td>Onscreen colors:</td><td>64</td></tr><tr><td>Maximum onscreen sprites:</td><td>80</td></tr><tr><td>Resolution:</td><td>320  224</td></tr><tr><td>Sound:</td><td>Yamaha YM2612 6 channel FM, additional 4 channel PSG. Stereo sound. Also Texas Instruments SN76489 PSG ( Programmable Sound Generator)</td></tr><tr><td>Display:</td><td>Integrated CSTN LCD at 320 x 224</td></tr><tr><td>Power Rating:</td><td>9V 850mA (same as Genesis/Mega Drive model 2)</td></tr></tbody></table><h2>Model Versions</h2><h3>Model 1</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084011" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.61551155115512" data-width="606" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 606px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:61.6%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084011"><img alt="Model 1" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg 606w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Model 1" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg 606w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084011-segagenesis1.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Model 1</figcaption></figure><p>Model 1 is the original design of the Sega Genesis. It has the logo High Definition Graphics above the Sega Genesis label. It has a headphone jack input for stereo sound. Model 1 only supported RF and Mono Composite outputs like the original <a href="/nes/3045-21/" data-ref-id="3045-21">Nintendo</a>. It has an expansion port for hardware add-ons for things like the <a href="../../sega-cd/60-29/" rel="nofollow">Sega CD</a>.</p><h3>Model 2</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084012" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75806451612903" data-width="372" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 372px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.8%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084012"><img alt="Model 2" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg 372w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Model 2" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg 372w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084012-sega_genesis2_1.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Model 2</figcaption></figure><p>Model 2 was the redesign of the Sega Genesis, shortly after the Super Nintendo became popular. It got rid of the headphone inputs, as this model supported full stereo composite and component outputs. Sadly, the redesign of the circuits caused a sound distortion that made the quality poor. Later, the sound issues were <a href="http://www.sega-16.com/forum/showthread.php?7796-GUIDE-Telling-apart-good-Genesis-1s-and-Genesis-2s-from-bad-ones" rel="nofollow" data-ref-id="false">improved somewhat</a> with the VA3 &amp; VA4 motherboards. The Sega CD was also redesigned for Model 2 to fit the new look.</p><h3>Model 3</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084013" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.78693181818182" data-width="1056" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 1056px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:78.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1084013"><img alt="Model 3" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 1056w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Model 3" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 1056w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/8153/1084013-genesis3led.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Model 3</figcaption></figure><p>Model 3 came out during the 32-bit era. It's a compact version of the Sega Genesis. It has no expansion slot for add-ons. However, it does have output support for S-Video, which separates the color signal from the contrast making the picture quality much better. S-Video inputs can be found on all modern television sets.</p><h3>Model 4</h3><p>Sega Genesis 4. also known as Firecore and Blaze Mega Drive, released in 2008, is an officially licensed console using an <a href="/emulation/3015-1587/" data-ref-id="3015-1587">software emulator</a> rather than actual hardware, and comes with a selection of games preinstalled (the number varies by region - the US release has 20 games, the European Blaze console has 15), as well as a slot to play Genesis cartridges. It does not support the <a href="/sega-cd/3045-29/" data-ref-id="3045-29">Sega CD</a> or <a href="/sega-32x/3045-31/" data-ref-id="3045-31">32X</a>. The emulator has a number of compatibility problems, such as being unable to play the music for some cartridge based games, most notably Sonic the Hedgehog 1 and 2.</p><p>In <a href="/brazil/3035-720/" data-ref-id="3035-720">Brazil</a> another console using model number 4 exists, <a href="/tec-toy-industria-de-brinquedos-sa/3010-96/" data-ref-id="3010-96">TecToy</a> Mega Drive 4, which like the Firecore/Blaze uses emulation rather than original hardware, but lacks a cartridge slot, and instead has 87 games pre-installed, some of which are ports of more recent titles, like <a href="/the-sims-2/3030-2473/" data-ref-id="3030-2473">The Sims 2</a> and <a href="/fifa-soccer-08/3030-19350/" data-ref-id="3030-19350">FIFA Soccer 08</a>, as well as an <a href="/guitar-hero/3025-289/" data-ref-id="3025-289">Guitar Hero</a> clone titled Guitar Idol. It does support SD cards for MP3 playback and Rom files.</p>

<Platform 'Atari Lynx'>
Atari Lynx
1989-09-30 00:00:00
Atari SA

<h2><b>Overview</b></h2><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The Atari Lynx was released in 1989 by <a href="/atari-sa/3010-548/" data-ref-id="3010-548">Atari Corporation, </a>based upon an <a href="/epyx-inc/3010-901/" data-ref-id="3010-901">Epyx</a> design. The design was completed two years earlier, but Epyx did not have the capital to produce the system themselves. Atari had considered entering the portable market in 1981 with of a portable console called the Atari 2200; a smaller version of the <a href="/atari-2600/3045-40/" data-ref-id="3045-40">Atari 2600</a>, with an internal screen. This never came to fruition as it was not deemed cost-effective.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The Lynx was highly advanced for the time, and was the first handheld console with a fully backlit screen and 16-bit technology. Further, a Lynx is ambidextrous with a simple flip. In 1991, Atari released the updated Lynx II. Slightly smaller than the original and with improved aesthetics, it is still quite large and bulky compared to other contemporary consoles. Over 100 games were released for the system over its life span, and it had an estimated 500,000 user install base worldwide.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Criticisms of the console are generally regarding the relatively large size, short battery life, and lack of third-party support. The <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a> <a href="/game-boy/3045-3/" data-ref-id="3045-3">Game Boy</a> and the <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> <a href="/game-gear/3045-5/" data-ref-id="3045-5">Game Gear</a> both dominated the market into the early nineties, though the Lynx continued to do well, particularly in Europe. By 1996, Atari ceased production of the system.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Today the Atari Lynx still has a dedicated cult following, with some developers and private individuals still making and producing games for the platform. In 2008 the system, and Atari were honored by the 59th annual technology and engineering Emmy awards, for pioneering the handheld gaming platform.</p><h2>Hardware</h2><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">CPU - Dual 16-bit CMOS, Mikey &amp; Suzy (16MHZ)</p><figure style="width: 800px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3104/417046-atari_lynx_1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-417046" data-size="medium" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="800" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3104/417046-atari_lynx_1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-417046"><img alt="Atari Lynx console" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/3104/417046-atari_lynx_1.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3104/417046-atari_lynx_1.jpg 800w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/3104/417046-atari_lynx_1.jpg 480w" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480"></a><figcaption>Atari Lynx console</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">RAM - 64KB DRAM.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">On Screen Colors - 4096 (16 simultaneous per scan line).</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Screen Resolution - 160x102 Pixels.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Screen - LCD 82.55mm x 47.75mm and (88.9mm/3.5" diagonal).</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Sound - 8-bit 4 channel (mono for Lynx I and Stereo sound for Lynx II).</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Game Cartridge - 2MB (16Mbit).</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Power Supply - AC adapter or batteries.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Battery Life - 4 hours for the Lynx I and 5 hours for Lynx II (6 AA batteries Alkaline).</p><h3>Launch titles in North America</h3><ul><li><a href="/blue-lightning/3030-3142/" data-ref-id="3030-3142">Blue Lightning</a></li><li><a href="/california-games/3030-3193/" data-ref-id="3030-3193">California Games</a></li><li><a href="/electrocop/3030-11150/" data-ref-id="3030-11150">Electrocop</a></li><li><a href="/gates-of-zendocon/3030-6909/" data-ref-id="3030-6909">Gates of Zendocon</a></li></ul>
