<h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/500/434974-sega_master_pad.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-434974" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.675" data-width="400" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 400px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:67.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/500/434974-sega_master_pad.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-434974"><img alt="The gamepad." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/500/434974-sega_master_pad.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/500/434974-sega_master_pad.jpg 400w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/500/434974-sega_master_pad.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>The gamepad.</figcaption></figure><p>The <a href="/sega-corporation/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> Master System is an 8-bit cartridge and card based console released in 1985 for <a href="/japan/3035-37/" data-ref-id="3035-37">Japan</a>, 1986 for <a href="/north-america/3035-1664/" data-ref-id="3035-1664">North America</a>, 1987 for <a href="/europe/3035-289/" data-ref-id="3035-289">Europe</a>, and 1989 for <a href="/south-america/3035-2906/" data-ref-id="3035-2906">South America</a>. It is based off the Mark III release of Sega's first entry into the Japanese console market, the <a href="/sega-sg-1000/3045-141/" data-ref-id="3045-141">SG-1000</a>. It competed directly alongside the <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a> <a href="/nes/3045-21/" data-ref-id="3045-21">Entertainment System</a> during its heyday, and it was supported by Sega well into the 1990's. The console usually featured a built-in game, such as <a href="/hang-on/3030-11570/" data-ref-id="3030-11570">Hang-On</a>, <a href="/space-harrier/3030-6036/" data-ref-id="3030-6036">Space Harrier</a>, a timed <a href="/snail-maze/3030-21555/" data-ref-id="3030-21555">maze game</a> featuring a snail, <a href="/alex-kidd-in-miracle-world/3030-9101/" data-ref-id="3030-9101">Alex Kidd in Miracle World</a>, <a href="/sonic-the-hedgehog/3030-2465/" data-ref-id="3030-2465">Sonic the Hedgehog</a>, or <a href="/sonic-the-hedgehog-2/3030-1662/" data-ref-id="3030-1662">Sonic the Hedgehog 2</a>.</p><p>The system has 2 9-pin controller ports, with each controller having 2 buttons (labeled 1 and 2) and an 8-way directional pad, created largely due to Nintendo's copyright of their cross bar pad, which caused the SMS controller to have more pronounced diagonals than their competition, but smaller N, S, E, and W controls. The pause button is located directly on the console.</p><p>While the Master System didn't challenge Nintendo much in the <a href="/the-united-states-of-america/3035-396/" data-ref-id="3035-396">US </a>and Japan, it was a major success in Europe and South America, outselling the NES in those territories. This gave Sega a good foothold in the home console market, which they capitalized on with their more successful follow up, the <a href="/genesis/3045-6/" data-ref-id="3045-6">Sega Genesis</a>. The Master System was still selling in Europe into the late 1990s, and is still selling through to the present day in <a href="/brazil/3035-720/" data-ref-id="3035-720">Brazil</a>, where it remains the best-selling console to this day. The Master System sold <a href="http://gaming.wikia.com/wiki/Third-Generation_Consoles" rel="nofollow">16.39 million</a> units worldwide, including 1.72 million in Japan, 2 million in the <a href="/the-united-states-of-america/3035-396/" data-ref-id="3035-396">United States</a>, 5 million in <a href="/brazil/3035-720/" data-ref-id="3035-720">Brazil</a>, 6.95 million in Western Europe, and 720,000 in <a href="/south-korea/3035-2410/" data-ref-id="3035-2410">South Korea</a>.</p><p>The Master System's software library was limited in North America and Japan due to Nintendo's restrictive third-party licensing policies, for which the console has been criticized by the North American press. However, the system's reception was far more positive in Europe and South America, regions where Nintendo's restrictive third-party licensing policies did not apply. As a result, the Master System's software library in Europe and South America was significantly larger and more diverse, whereas it was the NES which had a more limited software library in those regions.</p><h2>Launch</h2><h3>Japan - October 20, 1985</h3><ul><li><a href="/hang-on/3030-11570/" data-ref-id="3030-11570">Hang-On</a></li><li>Teddy Boy Blues</li></ul><h3>North America - June 1986</h3><ul><li><a href="/hang-on/3030-11570/" data-ref-id="3030-11570">Hang-On</a></li><li><a href="/safari-hunt/3030-13798/" data-ref-id="3030-13798">Safari Hunt</a></li></ul><h3>Europe - Fall srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/91/1075409-800px_sega_master_system_lightphaser.jpg 800w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/91/1075409-800px_sega_master_system_lightphaser.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>The Light Phaser.</figcaption></figure><p>Early models of the console shipped with a <a href="/safari-hunt/3030-13798/" data-ref-id="3030-13798">Safari Hunt</a>/ <a href="/hang-on/3030-11570/" data-ref-id="3030-11570">Hang On</a> card, one game pad and one light phaser. <a href="/missile-defense-3-d/3030-19870/" data-ref-id="3030-19870">Missile Defense 3D</a> also made use of the console's <a href="/segascope-3-d-glasses/3000-76/" data-ref-id="3000-76">SegaScope</a> <a href="/3-d-glasses/3055-1919/" data-ref-id="3055-1919">3D glasses</a>, which plugged into a card slot device and saw only six games made for it since release. The glasses plugged into a card slot device and used shutters to simulate <a href="/stereoscopic-3d/3015-1204/" data-ref-id="3015-1204">3D depth</a> from flickering images on screen. An <a href="/arcade/3045-84/" data-ref-id="3045-84">arcade</a> stick was also released as a peripheral, and a six-button version of the game pad appeared later in the console's life.</p><p>Sega released an add-on for the Genesis called the <a href="/power-base-converter/3000-23/" data-ref-id="3000-23">Power Base Converter</a> that sat on top of the cartridge slot and allowed for Master System games to be played on the 16-bit console. It has all of the exterior features of the original Master System including a pause button on the device and support for both cartridges and cards.</p><p>The Light Phaser was was a <a href="/light-gun/3015-2354/" data-ref-id="3015-2354">light gun</a> required to play Safari Hunt and would go on to be featured in games such as <a href="/shooting-gallery/3030-39454/" data-ref-id="3030-39454">Shooting Gallery</a> and <a href="/missile-defense-3-d/3030-19870/" data-ref-id="3030-19870">Missile Defense 3D</a>.</p><h2>Technical Specifications</h2><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2092986" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="640" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 640px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2092986"><img alt="Sega Control Stick" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Sega Control Stick" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092986-sega_control_stick.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Sega Control Stick</figcaption></figure><ul><li>CPU: 8-Bit Zilog processor running at 3.579545 MHz</li><li>Memory: 64 Kb RAM</li><li>Video: Texas Instruments VDP, 256 x 192 or 256 x 224 resolution, 32 colors on screen (16 for sprites/background, 16 for background only) from a palette of 64</li><li>Sound: Texas Instruments 4 channel processor (3 Square Wave, 1 White Noise)</li></ul><h2>Built-in Games</h2><p>The <a href="/sega-corporation/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> Master System (depending on the model) came with one, two, or three games built into its hardware. For the model 1 Sega Master System, the machine pre-programmed with three games. The actual retail quality games were <a href="/hang-on/3030-11570/" data-ref-id="3030-11570">Hang On</a> (Action) and <a href="/safari-hunt/3030-13798/" data-ref-id="3030-13798">Safari Hunt</a> (Light Phaser Series). However, there is a special exclusively programmed meta-mini-game hidden in the Master System hardware. It is a <a href="/snail-maze/3030-21555/" data-ref-id="3030-21555">Snail Maze</a> game that can be accessed through holding both the one and two buttons as well as holding the up potion of the directional square.</p><h2>Software Formats</h2><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2092994" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.97" data-width="500" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 500px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:97.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2092994"><img alt="Sega Handle Controller" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg 500w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Sega Handle Controller" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg 500w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/122766/2092994-sega_handle_controller.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Sega Handle Controller</figcaption></figure><p>The <a href="/sega-corporation/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> Master System had multiple forms of solid state software technology that was made compatible for the system. The machine allowed for cartridges in the Mega-Cartridge series. For example, the game <a href="/alex-kidd-in-miracle-world/3030-9101/" data-ref-id="3030-9101">Alex Kidd in Miracle World</a> was a Mega-Cartridge game, but later and more advanced games like <a href="/after-burner/3030-3629/" data-ref-id="3030-3629">After Burner</a> used the Four Mega-Cartridge technology. It is unknown at this point if the Sega Master System cartridge technology surpassed the Four-Mega Cartridge. However, there is another variation on this form of software; the implementation of a lithium battery (exactly the same as batteries used for computer motherboards). This addition is indicated with the cartridge suffix descriptor of "Plus" or plus battery aided rewritable memory storage. For example, <a href="/phantasy-star/3030-7400/" data-ref-id="3030-7400">Phantasy Star</a> is classified as a Four-Mega Plus Cartridge. The Plus feature allowed for players to record scores and game progress.</p><p>Another form of game media for the Sega Master System also had support for game card or "Sega Cards." These were credit card sized solid state form of media that were fed into their own dedicated input slot on the system and tended to be ports of games from Sega's older Japanese console titles. The appeal of this software format was that they held less (only 34KB of space), game cards ended up being cheaper than Sega's Mega Cartridge games. One of the best titles on the Sega Card media is <a href="/transbot/3030-15686/" data-ref-id="3030-15686">Transbot</a>. It is important to note that the two later model designs of the Sega Master system omit a game card input slot.</p><p> </p>

9
<Platform 'Super Nintendo Entertainment System'>
Super Nintendo Entertainment System
1990-11-21 00:00:00
Nintendo
199.00
49100000
<h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813477-snes_logo2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-813477" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.28138528138528" data-width="231" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 231px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:28.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813477-snes_logo2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-813477"><img alt="Super Nintendo Entertainment System logo" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813477-snes_logo2.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813477-snes_logo2.jpg 231w" sizes="(max-width: 231px) 100vw, 231px" data-width="231"></a><figcaption>Super Nintendo Entertainment System logo</figcaption></figure><p>After the worldwide success of the <a href="/nes/3045-21/" data-ref-id="3045-21">NES</a>, and competitors' introduction of more powerful consoles such as the <a href="/genesis/3045-6/" data-ref-id="3045-6">Genesis</a> and <a href="/turbografx-16/3045-55/" data-ref-id="3045-55">Turbografx-16</a>, <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a> decided it was time to introduce a new home console: the SNES (Super Nintendo Entertainment System). The machine debuted in Japan on November 21, 1990, and arrived in North America on August 23, 1991. The release in South America, Europe, and Australia happened in 1992, starting at April 11 for UK/Ireland, June 6 for the rest of Europe, July 3 for Australia and Brazil in September.</p><p>The SNES was a 16-bit console and a direct competitor to <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a>'s Genesis. As with the NES, the SNES was the most successful console of its generation in terms of sales. The console triumphed through the support of both Nintendo and third-party developers. The console saw releases from high profile developers such as <a href="/capcom/3010-367/" data-ref-id="3010-367">Capcom</a>, <a href="/squaresoft/3010-6323/" data-ref-id="3010-6323">Squaresoft</a>, and <a href="/konami-corporation/3010-87/" data-ref-id="3010-87">Konami</a>. The SNES era is looked back on by many as "the golden era of video games."</p><p>Nintendo introduced an arcade system called the <a href="/nintendo-super-system/3015-3204/" data-ref-id="3015-3204">Nintendo Super System</a> in the early nineties that could be used to preview upcoming Super Nintendo games. Games that appeared on it included <a href="/super-mario-world/3030-1334/" data-ref-id="3030-1334">Super Mario World</a>, <a href="/actraiser/3030-7274/" data-ref-id="3030-7274">ActRaiser</a>, <a href="/contra-iii-the-alien-wars/3030-3016/" data-ref-id="3030-3016">Contra III: The Alien Wars</a>, and <a href="/f-zero/3030-15242/" data-ref-id="3030-15242">F-Zero</a>.</p><h3>Rivalry with Sega</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873617-large.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-873617" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.65" data-width="500" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 500px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:65.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873617-large.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-873617"><img alt="Genesis does what Nintendon't." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/873617-large.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873617-large.jpg 500w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/7465/873617-large.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/873617-large.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>Genesis does what Nintendon't.</figcaption></figure><p>Nintendo and <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> maintained a fierce rivalry during the 16-bit era. Sega's Genesis was released before the SNES, and this head start allowed the Genesis to gain a substantial sales lead by the time the SNES came to market. Simultaneously, Sega ran an aggressive marketing campaign that portrayed the SNES and its players as slow and childlike when compared to the speedy and "cool" Genesis. One of the most notorious of these ads touted the Genesis' (non-existant) "<a href="/blast-processing/3015-963/" data-ref-id="3015-963">Blast Processing</a>," which ostensibly allowed the Genesis to better render high-speed games such as <a href="/sonic-the-hedgehog/3030-2465/" data-ref-id="3030-2465">Sonic the Hedgehog</a> . While the Genesis led the SNES in sales throughout most of the 16-bit generation, the SNES eventually overtook it as the generation ended.</p><h2>History</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813478-snes_logo.png" data-ref-id="1300-813478" data-size="small" data-ratio="1" data-width="600" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 600px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:100.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813478-snes_logo.png" data-ref-id="1300-813478"><img alt="The Super Famicom logo in Japan and PAL regions" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 640px) 100vw, 640px" data-width="640" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813478-snes_logo.png" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813478-snes_logo.png 600w"><noscript><img alt="The Super Famicom logo in Japan and PAL regions" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813478-snes_logo.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/813478-snes_logo.png 600w" sizes="(max-width: 640px) 100vw, 640px" data-width="640"></noscript></a><figcaption>The Super Famicom logo in Japan and PAL regions</figcaption></figure><p><a href="/nec-technologies-inc/3010-1741/" data-ref-id="3010-1741">NEC</a> released the <a href="/turbografx-16/3045-55/" data-ref-id="3045-55">TurboGrafx-16</a> in 1987 to compete with the incredibly popular NES. <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> released its <a href="/genesis/3045-6/" data-ref-id="3045-6">Genesis</a> the next year. Both of these consoles were based on 16-bit hardware, and were large improvements in sound and graphics over the 8-bit based NES. As Nintendo's dominance in the market began slipping, Nintendo executives began to design a new system (albeit reluctantly at first) as early as 1988.</p><p>The SNES was designed by <a href="/masayuki-uemura/3040-69066/" data-ref-id="3040-69066">Masayuki Uemura</a>, who also designed the original Famicom. The Super Famicom was originally released in Japan on November 21, 1990 for 25,000, or about $210. It was a huge hit, with the initial units shipped selling very quickly. With the Super Famicom being such a success, Nintendo dominated over their rivals. This was partially due to the support of third-party developers, such as <a href="/capcom/3010-367/" data-ref-id="3010-367">Capcom</a>, <a href="/konami-corporation/3010-87/" data-ref-id="3010-87">Konami</a>, <a href="/square-co-ltd/3010-6323/" data-ref-id="3010-6323">Square</a>, <a href="/enix-corporation/3010-6337/" data-ref-id="3010-6337">Enix</a>, and <a href="/tecmo-inc/3010-485/" data-ref-id="3010-485">Tecmo</a>.</p><p>The Super Nintendo Entertainment System was released in North America in September 1991. It was a mildly redesigned version of the Super Famicom that originally sold for $199. One of the main selling points for the console was the fact that it was packaged with Super Mario World. Originally, very few titles were shipped upon release, such as <a href="/pilotwings/3030-14727/" data-ref-id="3030-14727">Pilotwings</a>, <a href="/f-zero/3030-15242/" data-ref-id="3030-15242">F-Zero</a>, <a href="/simcity/3030-15035/" data-ref-id="3030-15035">SimCity</a>, and <a href="/gradius-iii/3030-7617/" data-ref-id="3030-7617">Gradius III</a>, but these titles were all very well-received and showcased the SNES's Mode 7 abilities.</p><h3>Policies</h3><p>Nintendo's policies during the NES era were infamous: Nintendo had to approve every game, third-party developers could release only five games per year (and these games couldn't be released on a rival console within two years), and Nintendo controlled the manufacturing and supply of NES cartridges. When Sega began competing with Nintendo, however, these practices ended. Acclaim was the first developer to release their games for both consoles, and many other developers followed suit.</p><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873638-1112203563_04.png" data-ref-id="1300-873638" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.87109375" data-width="256" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 256px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:87.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873638-1112203563_04.png" data-ref-id="1300-873638"><img alt="The censored SNES version." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 256px) 100vw, 256px" data-width="256" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873638-1112203563_04.png" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873638-1112203563_04.png 256w"><noscript><img alt="The censored SNES version." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873638-1112203563_04.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/873638-1112203563_04.png 256w" sizes="(max-width: 256px) 100vw, 256px" data-width="256"></noscript></a><figcaption>The censored SNES version.</figcaption></figure><p>Nintendo, however, maintained their strict censorship policy. This was one of the key reasons why the Genesis version of <a href="/mortal-kombat/3030-15743/" data-ref-id="3030-15743">Mortal Kombat</a> sold much better than the SNES version. Nintendo removed all of the gore from the game, including fatalities, while the Genesis version kept these things.</p><p>Mortal Kombat's gore also sparked US Senators Herb Kohl and Joe Lieberman to call a Congressional hearing to discuss the effect of violent games on children. This meeting caused the founding of the Interactive Digital Software Association (now known as the Entertainment Software Association) and the <a href="/entertainment-software-ratings-board/3010-6476/" data-ref-id="3010-6476">Entertainment Software Rating Board</a> (which applies ratings to all games). Because of these companies being founded, Nintendo decided their censorship policies were no longer needed, and <a href="/mortal-kombat-ii/3030-3571/" data-ref-id="3030-3571">Mortal Kombat 2</a> was released in an uncensored version on the SNES, which caused it to sell better than the Genesis version.</p><h2>Technical Specifications</h2><ul><li><b>CPU</b>: Custom 65c816 based Ricoh 5A22 running at a selectable 3.58 MHz, 2.66 Mhz or 1.77 Mhz.</li><li><b>Memory</b>: 128 Kb main RAM, 64 Kb video RAM, 64 Kb audio RAM</li><li><b>Video</b>: 256 x 224 or 512 x 224 or 256 x 239 or 512 x 239 progressive resolutions, 512 x 448 or 512 x 478 interlaced resolutions, 32,768 colors with 256 colors maximum on screen. (128 sprites max on screen)</li><li><b>Audio</b>: 2 Sony audio processors (1 8-bit, and 1 16-bit DSP) 8 channels, Sample Rate 32KHz, 16-bit stereo output</li><li>The SNES can also take advantage of custom chips provided by the individual game cartridges, like the "Super FX" co-processor chip that ran at either 10.5Mhz or 21Mhz depending on the game. Examples include <a href="/star-fox/3030-3984/" data-ref-id="3030-3984">Star Fox</a>, <a href="/super-mario-world-2-yoshis-island/3030-2866/" data-ref-id="3030-2866">Super Mario World 2: Yoshi's Island</a> and <a href="/stunt-race-fx/3030-19991/" data-ref-id="3030-19991">Stunt Race FX</a>.</li></ul><h3>Regional Differences</h3><p>The main difference between the SNES and the Super Famicom is the console's color scheme. While purple and blue were used on the North American SNES, the Super Famicom as well as the European and Australian SNES had a logo consisting of rounded green, blue, yellow, and red shapes matching the Super Famicom's and PAL SNES' colored A, B, X, and Y buttons. Additionally, each version of the console has its own version of region lockout, with differently shaped cartridges, rendering a Japanese or PAL version of a game useless on a North American console and vice versa.</p><h3>Hardware Revision</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-232259" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.63092979127135" data-width="1054" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 1054px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:63.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-232259"><img alt="The SNS-101" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 1054w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="The SNS-101" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 1054w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/245/232259-img_0078.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>The SNS-101</figcaption></figure><p>On October 20, 1997 the Super Nintendo received lighter and more streamlined design in the US. Officially called the SNS-101 it was Nintendo's way of selling more hardware at a cheaper $99.99 USD price point. It was targeted at gamers who where not ready to buy expensive next generation hardware and wanted something with a large library of cheaper games.</p><p>Pack in games included:</p><ul><li><a href="/the-legend-of-zelda-a-link-to-the-past/3030-10276/" data-ref-id="3030-10276">The Legend of Zelda: a Link to the Past</a></li><li><a href="/super-mario-world-2-yoshis-island/3030-2866/" data-ref-id="3030-2866">Super Mario World 2 : Yoshi's Island</a></li><li><a href="/super-mario-all-stars-super-mario-world/3030-544/" data-ref-id="3030-544">Super Mario All Stars &amp; Super Mario World</a></li></ul><h3><a href="/mode-7/3015-184/" data-ref-id="3015-184">Mode 7</a></h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-254065" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.87761194029851" data-width="335" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 335px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:87.8%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-254065"><img alt="F-Zero extensively uses Mode 7." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg 335w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="F-Zero extensively uses Mode 7." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg 335w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4231/254065-f_zero_snes_screenshot_white_land.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>F-Zero extensively uses Mode 7.</figcaption></figure><p>Mode 7 is one of the SNES's seven different graphics modes, and the most famous of the seven. It was famous for allowing SNES games to display pseudo-3D graphics, giving the illusion of depth. The mode was able to warp the background layer of a game, changing the perspective to make the background appear to be moving. This was used prominently in a variety of games, to various effects. <a href="/final-fantasy-iv/3030-20829/" data-ref-id="3030-20829">Final Fantasy IV</a> and <a href="/final-fantasy-vi/3030-6336/" data-ref-id="3030-6336">Final Fantasy VI</a> used it to give the overworld an illusion of movement and size; when the player goes into an airship, the ground below shrinks away as the player gets higher and higher.</p><p>One limitation of Mode 7 was that it only allowed the background layer to be scaled and rotated. Individual sprites could not have this effect applied to them. So games like <a href="/super-castlevania-iv/3030-11443/" data-ref-id="3030-11443">Super Castlevania IV</a> and <a href="/super-metroid/3030-8292/" data-ref-id="3030-8292">Super Metroid</a>, where characters appear to have that effect applied to them are actually part of the background layer. In <a href="/contra-iii-the-alien-wars/3030-3016/" data-ref-id="3030-3016">Contra III: The Alien Wars</a>, where massive bosses appear to rotate and twist, they actually are the "background," and the background is actually a sprite that changes with the player's movement.</p><h3>Enhancement Chips</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8572/477089-star_fox__v1.2___u_____002.png" data-ref-id="1300-477089" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.875" data-width="256" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 256px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:87.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8572/477089-star_fox__v1.2___u_____002.png" data-ref-id="1300-477089"><img alt="Star Fox's 3D graphics are powered by the Super FX chip." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 256px) 100vw, 256px" data-width="256" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8572/477089-star_fox__v1.2___u_____002.png" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8572/477089-star_fox__v1.2___u_____002.png 256w"><noscript><img alt="Star Fox's 3D graphics are powered by the Super FX chip." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8572/477089-star_fox__v1.2___u_____002.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8572/477089-star_fox__v1.2___u_____002.png 256w" sizes="(max-width: 256px) 100vw, 256px" data-width="256"></noscript></a><figcaption>Star Fox's 3D graphics are powered by the Super FX chip.</figcaption></figure><p>There were a variety of first and third party enhancement chips included in SNES games. The most famous of these chips is the Super FX chip, used in games such as <a href="/star-fox/3030-3984/" data-ref-id="3030-3984">Star Fox</a> and <a href="/stunt-race-fx/3030-19991/" data-ref-id="3030-19991">Stunt Race FX</a>. However, there are numerous other notable chips, including the GB-Z80, DSP, CX4, S-RTC. The Super FX chip actually works to accelerate the graphics, making the SNES capable of more detailed, and even 3D, graphics. Its effects are seen in many games, including <a href="/doom/3030-7326/" data-ref-id="3030-7326">Doom</a> and <a href="/super-mario-world-2-yoshis-island/3030-2866/" data-ref-id="3030-2866">Super Mario World 2: Yoshi's Island</a>.</p><p>The GB-Z80 chip was used in the <a href="/super-game-boy-enhancement/3015-4804/" data-ref-id="3015-4804">Super Game Boy</a>, and it enabled the SNES to emulate <a href="/game-boy/3045-3/" data-ref-id="3045-3">Game Boy</a> hardware effectively. The SNES's processor was not powerful enough on its own. The DSP chip, of which there are several varieties, increases the capabilities of the SNES's Mode 7. The CX4 was a math processor used by <a href="/capcom/3010-367/" data-ref-id="3010-367">Capcom</a> in <a href="/mega-man-x2/3030-19801/" data-ref-id="3030-19801">Mega Man X2</a> and <a href="/mega-man-x3/3030-16948/" data-ref-id="3030-16948">Mega Man X3</a>, aiding in the formation of trigonometric graphics. The S-RTC was used in one game (Dai Kaiju Monogatari 2) to power a real-time in-game clock.</p><h2>Launch Titles</h2><h3>Japanese Launch Titles (November 21, 1990)</h3><ul><li><a href="/super-mario-world/3030-1334/" data-ref-id="3030-1334">Super Mario World</a></li><li><a href="/f-zero/3030-15242/" data-ref-id="3030-15242">F-Zero</a></li></ul><h3>U.S. Launch Titles (August 23, 1991)</h3><ul><li><a href="/super-mario-world/3030-1334/" data-ref-id="3030-1334">Super Mario World</a></li><li><a href="/f-zero/3030-15242/" data-ref-id="3030-15242">F-Zero</a></li><li><a href="/pilotwings/3030-14727/" data-ref-id="3030-14727">Pilotwings</a></li></ul><h3>European Launch Titles (April 11, 1992)</h3><ul><li><a href="/super-soccer/3030-11864/" data-ref-id="3030-11864">Super Soccer</a></li><li><a href="/super-tennis/3030-18664/" data-ref-id="3030-18664">Super Tennis</a></li><li><a href="/super-r-type/3030-8479/" data-ref-id="3030-8479">Super R-Type</a></li><li><a href="/f-zero/3030-15242/" data-ref-id="3030-15242">F-Zero</a></li></ul><h2>Planned CD Add-On and Other Peripherals</h2><p>Nintendo eventually planned to partner with Sony to develop a CD-based add-on for the console. Sony went on to develop a console referred to as "Play Station," which would play SNES CD releases as well as standard carts. After various disagreements between the two companies, the partnership was dissolved and Nintendo looked to Phillips to produce a similar CD-based add-on for the popular 16-bit console.</p><p>The partnership between Nintendo and Phillips wouldn't result in the release of a SNES CD drive, but the deal eventually resulted in Nintendo's characters appearing in a small number of Phillips-produced titles for its own multimedia console, the CD-i. Meanwhile, Sony would reuse the PlayStation name when developing its own CD-based console.</p><p><a href="/secret-of-mana/3030-19507/" data-ref-id="3030-19507">Secret of Mana</a> was said to be originally envisioned as a SNES CD release. Once the project was killed, the game was retooled to run on the standard cartridge format.</p><table><tbody><tr><th>Satellaview</th></tr><tr><td>Main Article: <a href="/satellaview/3045-98/" data-ref-id="3045-98">Satellaview</a><br />The SNES had online capabilities by way of the Satellaview add-on. It connected to satellite via a modem and was released in 1995 in Japan only. It allowed the user to download and play games in weekly or daily installments, and only broadcast at certain times. It was pioneering in both downloadable game media and episodic content on consoles and is viewed as a precursor to services such as the Wii's Shop Channel and Xbox Live Marketplace.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703465-satellaview__article_image.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1703465" data-size="small" data-ratio="1" data-width="418" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 418px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:100.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703465-satellaview__article_image.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1703465"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 640px) 100vw, 640px" data-width="640" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703465-satellaview__article_image.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703465-satellaview__article_image.jpg 418w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703465-satellaview__article_image.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703465-satellaview__article_image.jpg 418w" sizes="(max-width: 640px) 100vw, 640px" data-width="640"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>Super Game Boy</th></tr><tr><td>Nintendo released the Super Game Boy, a peripheral that allowed gamers to play Games that where compatible with the <a href="/game-boy/3045-3/" data-ref-id="3045-3">Game Boy</a> System in color through the SNES. The color was achieved by having the four shades of green mapped to different colors that the player can choose. The games where displayed at a smaller resolution than a standard SNES game, to make up for this a electronic bezel was placed around the screen. Some games such as <a href="/street-fighter-ii/3030-243/" data-ref-id="3030-243">Street Fighter II</a> for the Game Boy, had custom bezels for each stage.<br />The hardware inside the Super Game Boy is the that same as the Game Boy handheld. Inside the cartridge there is a CPU that runs the games. The Super Game Boy uses the SNES to output to the TV, as well as receive inputs from the controller or controllers.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427013-acc_gameboy.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1427013" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.9622641509434" data-width="318" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 318px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:96.2%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427013-acc_gameboy.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1427013"><img alt="US Super Game Boy" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 318px) 100vw, 318px" data-width="318" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427013-acc_gameboy.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427013-acc_gameboy.jpg 318w"><noscript><img alt="US Super Game Boy" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427013-acc_gameboy.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427013-acc_gameboy.jpg 318w" sizes="(max-width: 318px) 100vw, 318px" data-width="318"></noscript></a><figcaption>US Super Game Boy</figcaption></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>SNES Game Genie</th></tr><tr><td>Many third-party peripherals were released as well. A new version of the <a href="/game-genie/3000-21/" data-ref-id="3000-21">Game Genie</a> was released for use with the SNES. It allowed gamers to use codes to modify the way the game was played, for instance gaining infinite lives or ammo. Unlike the NES era, Nintendo was much more tolerant with unlicensed third-party peripherals.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1427014" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.94857142857143" data-width="350" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 350px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:94.9%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1427014"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg 350w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg 350w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1427014-acc_genie.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>Capcoms's Fighting Soldier</th></tr><tr><td>After a boom of 2D fighters to the SNES, such as Fatal Fury and Street Fighter 2, companies tried to cash in with add-ons to make a more authentic arcade experience. This stemmed from the SNES pad not having six buttons on the face. Capcom's solution was "Capcom's Fighting Soldier". The pad moved the the two shoulder buttons to the face, and spaced them to allow for use of fingers or thumbs. It was designed as an ergonomic fightpad, so as to not exclude console only players.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1677386-capcom_pad_md.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1677386" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.1505882352941" data-width="425" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 425px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:115.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1677386-capcom_pad_md.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1677386"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 556px) 100vw, 556px" data-width="556" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1677386-capcom_pad_md.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1677386-capcom_pad_md.jpg 425w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1677386-capcom_pad_md.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1677386-capcom_pad_md.jpg 425w" sizes="(max-width: 556px) 100vw, 556px" data-width="556"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>Championship Joystick</th></tr><tr><td>The Championship Joystick was a high-end joystick released in 199* for the SNES. Also released under the name Arcadia, it uses authentic 30mm Happ buttons and bat top joystick. The button layout is nearly identical to American Street Fighter II cabinets.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1703474" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.3283333333333" data-width="600" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 600px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:132.8%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1703474"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 481px) 100vw, 481px" data-width="481" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg 600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg 481w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg 600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1703474-championship_joystick_ad.jpg 481w" sizes="(max-width: 481px) 100vw, 481px" data-width="481"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>SNES Mouse</th></tr><tr><td>Main Article: <a href="/snes-mouse/3000-19/" data-ref-id="3000-19">SNES Mouse</a><br />The SNES Mouse was released for use in a few games, most prominently <a href="/mario-paint/3030-8791/" data-ref-id="3030-8791">Mario Paint</a>. The mouse was bundled with a Nintendo hard plastic mouse pad.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/32061/932707-snes_super_snes_mouse.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-932707" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.3333333333333" data-width="480" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 480px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:133.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/32061/932707-snes_super_snes_mouse.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-932707"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/32061/932707-snes_super_snes_mouse.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/32061/932707-snes_super_snes_mouse.jpg 480w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/32061/932707-snes_super_snes_mouse.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/32061/932707-snes_super_snes_mouse.jpg 480w" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>Super Multi Tap 2</th></tr><tr><td>Super Multi Tap 2 for the SNES was released in 1993. It allowed for three to four players to play on the same screen, for games that supported multi tap. It is a spiritual successor to the NES Advantage, with an updated look and feel. The Super Advantage was released in the 1990's and was geared toward fighting enthusiasts that where accustomed to an arcade setup. The Super Advantage also functions as a turbo controller with sliders controlling the rate. The controller features a unique button layout.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1703495-dscn1575u.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1703495" featured the option to use it (such as <a href="/super-scope-6/3030-2543/" data-ref-id="3030-2543">Super Scope 6</a> and <a href="/yoshis-safari/3030-3699/" data-ref-id="3030-3699">Yoshi's Safari</a>). In Europe and Australia it was known as the Nintendo scope. The Super Scope was not a light gun, but used a infrared signal. The infrared receiver plugged into the control ports.</td><td><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1704122" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.40125" data-width="800" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 800px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:40.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1704122"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg 800w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg 800w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1704122-800px_nintendo_scope.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><th>ASCII Turbo Controller</th></tr><tr><td>A Turbo Controller released for the SNES by ASCII. <h2>CPC 464</h2><p>The <a href="/amstrad/3010-3924/" data-ref-id="3010-3924">Amstrad</a> CPC 464 was the first of the CPC series released on the 21st June 1984. it quickly became one of the most successful home computers in Europe. More than two million CPC 464s were sold in total between 1984 and 1990.</p><p>Despite its lack of stand-out features from competitors such as <a href="/commodore/3010-2000/" data-ref-id="3010-2000">Commodore</a> and <a href="/sinclair-research-ltd/3010-938/" data-ref-id="3010-938">Sinclair</a>, it gained popularity from its low price and the fact that the computers were sold as a complete unit, comprising of CPU/keyboard, built-in tape recorder and monitor (optional monochrome green or colour). Competitor computers such as the <a href="/zx-spectrum/3045-16/" data-ref-id="3045-16">ZX Spectrum</a> came only with the CPU/keyboard, requiring a separate monitor.</p><h2>CPC 472</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815411-800px_cpc_472_es.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-815411" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.3075"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:30.8%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815411-800px_cpc_472_es.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-815411"><img alt="CPC 472" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1270/815411-800px_cpc_472_es.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815411-800px_cpc_472_es.jpg 800w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1270/815411-800px_cpc_472_es.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1270/815411-800px_cpc_472_es.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>CPC 472</figcaption></figure><p>The CPC 472 was a Spanish version of the CPC 464 with an additional, non-functional 8KB RAM. A Spanish law required that every computer with up to 64KB should have extra keys for the Spanish language, or an extra tax would be levied. So Amstrad soldered in an extra 8KB which was not, however, usable by the machine since it was not connected to anything else. Later on Amstrad released a 472 with a proper Spanish keyboard. After the rule changed there was also a CPC 472 with non-Spanish keys available for a very short time. Today, the CPC 472 is very rare.</p><h2>CPC 664</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815412-cpc664.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-815412" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.67428571428571"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:67.4%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815412-cpc664.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-815412"><img alt="CPC 664" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1270/815412-cpc664.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815412-cpc664.jpg 350w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1270/815412-cpc664.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>CPC 664</figcaption></figure><p>Released in In June 1985, the CPC 664 is essentialy the same hardware as the previous CPC 464 with a floppy disc drive taking the place of the cassette drive and a new easier to type keyboard.</p><h2>CPC 6128</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815415-cpc6128.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-815415" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.7"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:70.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815415-cpc6128.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-815415"><img alt="CPC 6128" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 210px) 100vw, 210px" data-width="210" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815415-cpc6128.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815415-cpc6128.jpg 210w"><noscript><img alt="CPC 6128" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815415-cpc6128.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1270/815415-cpc6128.jpg 210w" sizes="(max-width: 210px) 100vw, 210px" data-width="210"></noscript></a><figcaption>CPC 6128</figcaption></figure><p>Amstrad launched the CPC 6128 shortly after the CPC 664 for the American market . The new machine had 128KB of memory and a sleeker appearance, but was otherwise identical to the 664.</p><p>The machine was launched in <a href="/europe/3035-289/" data-ref-id="3035-289">Europe</a> shortly afterwards, replacing the 664 for the same price.</p><h2>The Plus Series</h2><p>The last models in the Amstrad CPC range were the Amstrad 464 Plus, Amstrad 6128 Plus, and the short lived Amstrad GX4000, a standalone console aimed squarely at the burgeoning home console market.</p><p>All launched together in 1990. The CPC name is said to have been dropped because of a legal dispute with a French firm, though it is also likely that "CPC 6128 Plus" was considered too unwieldy a name and one redolent of the machine's mid-80s heritage.</p><p>Described as a solution of <em>"too little, too late"</em>, this was Amstrad's second effort to prolong the life of its 8-bit computer series in the face of fierce competition from new 16-bit machines (notably, the <a href="/atari-st/3045-13/" data-ref-id="3045-13">Atari ST</a> and the <a href="/amiga/3045-1/" data-ref-id="3045-1">Commodore Amiga</a>). The Plus series were mostly (but not quite 100%) compatible with the original CPC computers, and incorporated a list of new features, like a cartridge port for instant program loading, DMA for the AY soundchip, hardware scrolling, programmable interrupts, 16 hardware zoomable sprites (not vectorized) with an independent palette of 15 colours, and a choice of 4096 colours all in a new, sleek case which mimicked the keyboard-computer design that was <em>en vogue</em> in the 16-bit market (ST, Amiga etc). While the Amstrad Plus computers were arguably one of the best 8-bit computers ever built for the mass market, they died an early death as the 16-bit era had well and truly begun.</p>

.14019.14052.14068.14086.14087.14088.14103.14104.14175.14197.14200.14205.14220.14235.14243.14250.14276.14293.14312.14330.14339.14346.14349.14351.14401.14410.14417.14421.14435.14470.14474.14507.14509.14540.14545.14574.14586.14599.14610.14631.14674.14704.14740.14744.14750.14765.14797.14804.14849.14868.14869.14886.14903.14943.14954.14955.14962.14970.14991.15035.15042.15092.15107.15122.15123.15136.15139.15191.15237.15265.15283.15314.15361.15396.15401.15457.15480.15501.15508.15526.15531.15575.15607.15621.15637.15651.15654.15696.15705.15706.15735.15783.15794.15809.15839.15842.15852.15856.15871.15874.15894.15922.15923.15974.15995.15998.16005.16011.16034.16044.16046.16047.16072.16095.16098.16113.16133.16161.16194.16204.16205.16240.16242.16262.16267.16277.16291.16295.16359.16370.16394.16439.16440.16445.16455.16471.16483.16501.16555.16560.16580.16642.16649.16657.16709.16785.16802.16813.16819.16826.16828.16832.16839.16856.16877.16898.16899.16910.16920.16936.16939.16955.16964.16983.16991.17015.17022.17031.17098.17128.17139.17151.17224.17232.17286.17294.17307.17318.17325.17389.17403.17454.17459.17460.17478.17553.17563.17564.17574.17586.17619.17628.17642.17648.17679.17688.17694.17726.17790.17795.17812.17856.17866.17895.17906.17986.17997.17998.18028.18041.18096.18120.18129.18141.18142.18147.18160.18188.18203.18206.18219.18233.18235.18244.18266.18267.18273.18293.18329.18338.18352.18433.18449.18464.18489.18502.18544.18555.18597.18625.18660.18672.18692.18701.18729.18761.18765.18770.18783.18800.18852.18881.18884.18890.18897.18918.18931.18934.18944.18968.18995.19036.19079.19112.19147.19148.19266.19270.19276.19280.19283.19295.19302.19382.19386.19450.19482.19487.19492.19547.19578.19594.19605.19606.19611.19664.19666.19678.19682.19685.19688.19714.19726.19735.19736.19737.19756.19768.19793.19797.19821.19844.19862.19875.19876.19891.19894.19918.19928.19984.20008.20013.20020.20072.20097.20120.20152.20164.20170.20190.20201.20227.20296.20369.20373.20374.20459.20744.20752.20790.20814.20897.21323.2 <Platform 'Apple II'>
Apple II
1977-06-30 00:00:00
Apple Inc.
1300.00
6000000
<h2>Overview</h2><figure style="width: 1536px" data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/15606/1293607-wozniak_and_jobs.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1293607" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.66666666666667" data-width="1536" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:66.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/15606/1293607-wozniak_and_jobs.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1293607"><img alt="Jobs and Wozniak." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/15606/1293607-wozniak_and_jobs.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/15606/1293607-wozniak_and_jobs.jpg 1536w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/1/15606/1293607-wozniak_and_jobs.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/1/15606/1293607-wozniak_and_jobs.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/15606/1293607-wozniak_and_jobs.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>Jobs and Wozniak.</figcaption></figure><p>The "Apple ][" as it was initially branded was released in 1977 and was the follow-up effort to the Apple-1 which was born from the mind of one <a href="/steve-wozniak/3040-93519/" data-ref-id="3040-93519">Steve Wozniak</a>. Even before fully completing the Apple-1 though, Wozniak was already dreaming of bigger and better things. A major goal he set in creating his next machine--the Apple ][--was to have it display in color. As he worked with potential hardware changes he began to realize the potential of the Apple ][ more and more. His previous experience with the game <a href="/breakout/3030-13049/" data-ref-id="3030-13049">Breakout</a> on the <a href="/atari-2600/3045-40/" data-ref-id="3045-40">Atari 2600</a> motivated Wozniak to essentially ensure the Apple ][ could be a great gaming machine. After Wozniak set up the new computer to display in color, he naturally continued on to add sound and then paddle control to the Apple ][. The Apple ][ was now a full blown gaming machine.</p><p>Though the designs of the Apple-1 and later the Apple ][ were basically Steve Wozniak's efforts alone, <a href="/steve-jobs/3040-9951/" data-ref-id="3040-9951">Steve Jobs</a> played a very important role in establishing the visual appearance of the Apple ][. While Wozniak wouldn't think twice about extra wires hanging about on a computer that worked, Jobs knew how to make an appealing package for people outside the once very niche computer enthusiast group. The Apple ][ was made to look quite different from other similar machines on the market. It had a much more user-friendly aesthetic. It almost looked like a <a href="/typewriter/3055-524/" data-ref-id="3055-524">typewriter</a> but at the same time was "futuristic" enough to clearly be seen as a computer. The case was user-friendly as well. It was easily removable which was rare at the time. Most similar devices warned against any type of self-service and didn't welcome exploration.</p><h2>The Apple ][ and Gaming</h2><p>Since Steve Wozniak designed the Apple ][ to be a gaming machine, it ended up being really successful as one. There were</p><figure style="width: 281px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7622/643363-mystery_house___apple_ii_render_emulation___2.png" data-ref-id="1300-643363" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.68683274021352" data-width="281" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:68.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7622/643363-mystery_house___apple_ii_render_emulation___2.png" data-ref-id="1300-643363"><img alt="Mystery House." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7622/643363-mystery_house___apple_ii_render_emulation___2.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7622/643363-mystery_house___apple_ii_render_emulation___2.png 281w" sizes="(max-width: 281px) 100vw, 281px" data-width="281"></a><figcaption>Mystery House.</figcaption></figure><p>countless games either designed specifically for the Apple ][ or ported over from <a href="/arcade/3045-84/" data-ref-id="3045-84">arcade</a> versions or other systems versions over the years. One of the most important games to come from the Apple ][ early on though was a game called <a href="/mystery-house/3030-2744/" data-ref-id="3030-2744">Mystery House</a>. This was the first adventure style game to feature graphics of any kind. all previous games of this style were purely <a href="/text-based/3015-2199/" data-ref-id="3015-2199">text based</a>. A worthwhile side note is that Mystery House was created by <a href="/ken-williams/3040-928/" data-ref-id="3040-928">Ken</a> and <a href="/roberta-williams/3040-2073/" data-ref-id="3040-2073">Roberta Williams</a> who went on to start the company <a href="/sierra-entertainment-inc/3010-1030/" data-ref-id="3010-1030">Sierra Entertainment, Inc.</a> As previously mentioned, there were many ports of games that came to the Apple ][. Just a few of these titles include popular games such as:</p><ul><li><a href="/paperboy-rampage/3030-9961/" data-ref-id="3030-9961">Paper Boy</a></li><li><a href="/dig-dug/3030-2587/" data-ref-id="3030-2587">Dig Dug</a></li><li><a href="/donkey-kong/3030-311/" data-ref-id="3030-311">Donkey Kong</a></li><li><a href="/maniac-mansion/3030-2037/" data-ref-id="3030-2037">Maniac Mansion</a></li><li><a href="/bad-dudes/3030-10261/" data-ref-id="3030-10261">Bad Dudes</a></li><li><a href="/a-mind-forever-voyaging/3030-3250/" data-ref-id="3030-3250">A Mind Forever Voyaging</a></li><li><a href="/aliens-the-computer-game/3030-14797/" data-ref-id="3030-14797">Aliens:The computer game</a></li><li><a href="/arkanoid/3030-15107/" data-ref-id="3030-15107">Arkanoid</a></li><li>Arkanoid II</li><li>Dynasty</li><li>Depth Charge</li><li><a href="/f-15-strike-eagle/3030-10394/" data-ref-id="3030-10394">F-15 Strike Eagle</a></li><li><a href="/impossible-mission/3030-14849/" data-ref-id="3030-14849">Impossible Mission</a></li><li><a href="/impossible-mission-ii/3030-764/" data-ref-id="3030-764">Impossible Mission II</a></li><li><a href="/prince-of-persia/3030-2561/" data-ref-id="3030-2561">Prince of Persia</a></li><li><a href="/star-trek-the-kobayashi-alternative/3030-910/" data-ref-id="3030-910">Star Trek: The Kobayashi Alternative</a></li></ul><p>Most commercial software was provided on 5.25" floppy diskettes, but the earliest and cheapest disk drives of the era could only read one side at a time. This resulted in larger games being shipped on multiple, more expensive double-sided diskettes, and because there was no hard drive to install the software on to it, prompts would appear instructing the user when to swap or flip the diskettes to continue.</p><p>Even though the graphics were often pared down as the Apple ][ became older, it still provided Apple owners with roughly the same experience of these games. The Apple ][, along with machines like the <a href="/amiga/3045-1/" data-ref-id="3045-1">Amiga</a>, the <a href="/atari-8-bit/3045-24/" data-ref-id="3045-24">Atari 400 and 800</a>, and the <a href="/commodore-64/3045-14/" data-ref-id="3045-14">Commodore 64</a> (to an extent), single-handedly drove the microcomputer gaming era beginning in the late 70s.</p><h2>Hardware Details</h2><p>The Apple ][ was a very versatile computer in terms of hardware <i>and</i> software. The Apple ][ was actually the leader as a platform for launching new and unique hardware gadgets for quite some time after its initial launch in 1977. The popularity of the Apple ][ series kept it on the cutting edge of hardware/peripheral development until about 1991. That's nearly 15 years of hardware development for a single platform. Standard hardware components were included the Apple ][, such as keyboard, RAM, CPU, etc. The first Apple ][ models were built and shipped with 4K of memory, upgradable via expansion ports up to 48K. The Apple ]['s various expansion ports proved very useful for the many possible hardware upgrades to come in the future.</p><p>Hardware expansions were very common despite not always being very compatible or universal. Some of the general "upgrades" one could purchase and install included:</p><ul><li>Interface Cards</li><li>Video Cards</li><li>ROM/RAM expansion cards</li><li>Co-Processors</li><li>Accelerators</li><li>Printers</li><li>Alternate Input Devices (joysticks, paddles, etc.)</li></ul><p>The earliest Apple ][ keyboards are notable for not having a CAPS LOCK key, because the system typeface included in ROM had no lower-case letters. Graphical fonts could be used by applications to overcome this limitation, as the keyboard did have a Shift function for typing symbols placed above the number row.</p><p>The Apple logo on the case was emblazoned with a rainbow of stripes to signify the system's ability to produce color. Display monitors sold with the Apple ][ were generally monochrome, commonly in green or amber. The video capabilities of the system, however, were based on the NTSC video standard. Hooking up a normal household television to the computer would allow for compatible games and applications to display in a small but variable color palette, depending on the video mode used and the technique of signal modulation.</p><h2>Hardware Evolution and New Models</h2><h3>Apple ][ Plus</h3><figure style="width: 440px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1296890" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="440" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1296890"><img alt="The Apple II Plus" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg 440w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="The Apple II Plus" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg 440w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1296890-apple3.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>The Apple II Plus</figcaption></figure><p>The Apple ][ Plus hardware was introduced to market in June 1979. Upgraded features included a ROM-integrated version of the Applesoft II BASIC programming language (supplied by Microsoft), and a much larger 48K bank of RAM now expandable to 64K.</p><p>The inclusion of the BASIC programming language in every model sold, rather than having to purchase it separately, provided a greater opportunity for every end user to explore programming and naturally game development on the system. Computer enthusiast magazines of this era would often include several pages of BASIC code that a user would painstakingly type in by hand, to run various utility or entertainment programs, some even including primitive graphics. In the event of an error in the printed code, a user could attempt debugging, or simply wait for the next magazine issue to print a correction.</p>
