13
<Platform 'Atari ST'>
Atari ST
1985-04-01 00:00:00
Atari SA
1000.00
4000000
<h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/227982-atarist2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-227982" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.75"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/227982-atarist2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-227982"><img alt="Atari 1040STFM with Atari Mouse and a TAC-2 joystick." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9560/227982-atarist2.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/227982-atarist2.jpg 2048w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/0/9560/227982-atarist2.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9560/227982-atarist2.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9560/227982-atarist2.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>Atari 1040STFM with Atari Mouse and a TAC-2 joystick.</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The Atari ST line of computers were launched in 1985 with the Atari 520ST. It included an 8mhz Motorola 68000 CPU and 512kb RAM. Later models, called STFM, had a built-in floppy disk drive, RF-modulator and had the power supply built into the case. "ST" means Sixteen-Thirtytwo, because of the CPU's 16bit data bus and 32bit internals.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">It became popular in businesses because of the excellent office applications, in the homes of people because of the great games and amongst musicians because of its built-in MIDI ports and software like Cubase. The Atari ST was in direct competition with Commodore's <a href="/amiga/3045-1/" data-ref-id="3045-1">Amiga</a> and Apple's <a href="/mac/3045-17/" data-ref-id="3045-17">Macintosh</a> line of computers, and to some extent the <a href="/amstrad-cpc/3045-11/" data-ref-id="3045-11">Amstrad CPC</a> line of computers. The developement was lead by <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/jack-tramiel/3040-98032/" data-ref-id="3040-98032">Jack Tramiel</a>, who had recently left <a href="/commodore/3010-2000/" data-ref-id="3010-2000">Commodore </a>(Tramiel actually started the Commodore company and developed among other computers the <a href="/commodore-64/3045-14/" data-ref-id="3045-14">C64</a>.) to buy the rights to the Atari name. The Atari ST was intended to be an affordable computer with fast performance. But because it was supposed to be affordable, it is not as powerful in the audio and graphics department as the Commodore Amiga. Focus was instead put on including a fast CPU and lots of RAM.</p><h2>Hardware</h2><h3>Specifications</h3><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">These are the basic specifications for the Atari 520ST:</p><ul><li><b>CPU:</b> Motorola 68000 16/32bit @ 8mhz. 16 bit data bus/32 bit internal/24-bit address bus.</li><li><b>RAM:</b> 512kb (1mb for the 1040ST models)</li><li><b>Soundchip:</b> Yamaha YM2149F 3-voice squarewave plus 1-voice white noise mono PSG(plus 2 channels on the STE models).</li><li><b>Diskdrive:</b> Single-sided 3" floppy disk drive. 360kb capacity(1040ST had double-sided drives which had a capacity of 720kb).</li><li><b>Display:</b> 60 Hz NTSC, 50 Hz PAL, 71.2 Hz monochrome:</li></ul><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Low Resolution - 320200 (16 color), palette of 512 colors</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Medium resolution - 640200 (4 color), palette of 512 colors</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">High resolution - 640400 (mono), monochrome</p><ul><li><b>Ports:</b> TV out (on ST-M and ST-FM models, NTSC or PAL standard RF modulated), MIDI in/out (with 'out-thru'), RS-232 serial, Centronics parallel (printer), monitor (RGB or Composite Video colour and mono, 13-pin DIN), extra disk drive port (15-pin DIN), DMA port (ACSI port, Atari Computer System Interface) for hard disks and Atari Laser Printer (sharing RAM with computer system), joystick and mouse ports (9-pin MSX standard).</li><li><b>Operating System:</b> TOS (<b>T</b>he <b>O</b>perating <b>S</b>ystem) with the GEM user interface.</li></ul><h3>Models</h3><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">There are a couple of different models of the Atari ST. They are:</p><ul><li><b>Atari 130ST</b> - Never released. Was supposed to come with only 128kb ram. But the operating system alone took up almost all that amount, so they canceled it.</li></ul><ul><li><b>Atari 260ST</b> - Only released in Europe. It was first only a prototype just like the 130ST but was eventually released in Europe. It is identical to the 520ST and was released to differentiate it from the new 520ST+.</li><li><b>Atari 520ST</b> - The original model described in Specifications.</li><li><b>Atari 520STM</b> - Model with only RF modulation built in, without floppy drive. Not very common.</li><li><b>Atari 520STFM</b> - Built-in floppy drive, RF modulator and power supply.</li><li><b>Atari 520ST</b><strong>+</strong><b> </b> - 1mb RAM but no floppy drive.</li><li><b>Atari 1040STF</b> - A 520ST with built in double sided DD disk drive and 1mb ram. But without RF modulator.</li><li><b>Atari 1040STFM</b> - A 520ST with built in double sided DD disk drive, 1mb ram and RF modulator.</li><li><b>Mega ST</b> - Redesigned motherboard. Comes with 2 or 4mb of RAM. Includes a real-time clock and an internal connector for expansion. Most models also includes the BLiTTER graphics chip.</li><li><b>520STE and 1040STE</b> - Same as 520STFM/1040STFM but includes the BLiTTER graphics chip and has enhanced sound and color palettes. The sound chip has 2 extra channels that can be used for sampled sounds. The color palette is extended to 4096 colors. Unfortunately the STE specific features were rarely used in commercial software.</li><li><b>Mega STE</b> - Same hardware as the 1040STE has, but with a 16mhz CPU.</li><li><b>STacy</b> - A portable version of the ST.</li><li><b>ST BOOK </b>- Portable version of the STE. It was more portable than the STacy but sacrifice many features in order to achieve that.</li></ul><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Prototypes/Vaporware (Never Officially Released)</p><ul><li>1040 STE+ (STE 2080)  Similar to the 1040STE but with a additional, intern AMD 80286 CPU and a 40MB HD (2,5")</li></ul><h2>Operating System</h2><h3>Details</h3><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The operating system in the Atari ST is called TOS which means simply "The Operating System". It combines the GEM GUI and the underlying GEMDOS, a DOS like operating system, both by Digital Research. TOS is stored in onboard ROM chips, though early versions of the Atari ST came with TOS on floppy disks. Because it is stored on ROM chips it boots up instantly. Some people jokingly says that TOS means "Tremiel Operating System", since Jack Tremiel was the one leading the developement of the Atari ST.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">TOS is composed of the following parts:</p><ul><li><b>GEMDOS</b> - GEM Disk Operating System (A DOS-like operating system Digital Research had experimented with a little bit before Atari decided to use GEM.)</li><li><b>GEM</b> - Graphical Environment Manager (A graphical environment. GEM itself consists of AES, VDI and GEM Desktop.)</li></ul><ul><li><b>AES</b> - Application Environment Service (Provides the window system, window manager and other GUI elements.)</li></ul><ul><li><b>VDI</b> - Virtual Device Interface (The core graphics system of the GEM engine.)</li><li><b>GEM</b> Desktop (An application using AES to provide a file manager and application launcher.)</li><li><b>BIOS</b> - Basic Input/Output System</li><li><b>XBIOS</b> - Extended BIOS</li><li><b>Line-A</b> (Low-level high-speed graphics calls.)</li></ul><ul><li><b>GDOS</b> - Graphics Device Operation System (Sends commands from VDI to the proper device driver for rendering graphics.)</li><li><b>AHDI</b> - Atari Hard Disk Interface (Driver for Hard Drive. Has to be loaded seperately.)</li></ul><h3>Desktop</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/779987-220078_16_atarigem.gif" data-ref-id="1300-779987" data-size="medium" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.625"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:62.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/779987-220078_16_atarigem.gif" data-ref-id="1300-779987"><img alt="The GEM desktop in low resolution (320x200)" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9560/779987-220078_16_atarigem.gif" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/779987-220078_16_atarigem.gif 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9560/779987-220078_16_atarigem.gif 480w" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480"></a><figcaption>The GEM desktop in low resolution (320x200)</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The desktop looks like most other desktops in that it uses icons to represent devices and files, and has windows and dialog boxes. The standard desktop has a trashcan and two floppy disk icons, even if you only have one floppy drive. On the top part is a context sensitive panel. It changes it's contents depending on the application (much like on a Macintosh), though applications are not required to even use the panel in which case it is removed.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">TOS can execute a variety of different filetypes. They can be identified by their extensions:</p><ul><li><b>*.ACC</b> - Desktop accessory. Loaded automatically when the application loads.</li><li><b>*.APP</b> - Application. Was not used very often.</li><li><b>*.PRG</b> - Executable program. Often GEM programs.</li><li><b>*.TOS</b> - Programs that only uses GEMDOS and not the GEM system to function. (i.e. it doesn't use GEM to draw windows.)</li><li><b>*.TTP</b> - TOS Takes Parameters. Opens up a dialog box in which you can add arguments for the program.</li></ul><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">It is possible to autoboot TOS programs (but not GEM programs) on startup by placing the program inside a folder named "AUTO" on a floppy disk. If the floppy is inserted into the drive when the computer boots, it will bypass the GEM desktop and boot the TOS program instead. This is used by many demos and most games.</p><h3>Similarity to Macintosh UI</h3><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The look and feel of GEM for Atari ST is very much like the one in the original Macintosh. Apple sued Digital Research over this and Digital Reasearch had to redesign their GEM window manager for other architectures. But since it was mostly Atari who developed the Motorola 68000 version, they were given the rights to further develop the 68k version without Digital Research's consent. Because of this, Atari was not affected by the Apple-Digital Research lawsuit and were allowed to have a more Mac-like look and feel.</p><h2>Demo scene</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699568-virtual_escape.gif" data-ref-id="1300-699568" data-size="medium" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.625"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:62.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699568-virtual_escape.gif" data-ref-id="1300-699568"><img alt=""Virtual Escape" demo by Equinox" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px" data-width="400" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699568-virtual_escape.gif" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699568-virtual_escape.gif 400w"><noscript><img alt=""></noscript></a><figcaption>"Virtual Escape" demo by Equinox</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">As with most other early computers, there is a scene of people still active on the Atari ST platform. They use the ST to create productions called "demos", which is a program demonstrating the demo group's programming, graphics making and music composing abilities on limited hardware.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The groups came up with new programming techniques to push the limits of the computer to do things that people would've only dreamed about doing years ago. Some of these techniques were later used in games for the Atari ST. For example the technique to display graphics in the borders of the screen.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699564-left_border.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-699564" data-size="medium" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.5825"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:58.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699564-left_border.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-699564"><img alt=""Death of the left border" screen by TNT Crew." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px" data-width="400" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699564-left_border.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/699564-left_border.jpg 400w"><noscript><img alt=""></noscript></a><figcaption>"Death of the left border" screen by TNT Crew.</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Since the Atari ST was not powerful enough to display graphics in fullscreen, Atari put borders on the sides of the screen. But after many years of experimenting, people in the demo scene removed those borders one by one, starting with the upper border. The last border to be removed was the left one that was thought to be impossible to remove. But once again a demo group succeeded with removing the border and were finally able to display graphics in fullscreen. Of course, this had an impact on performance, so you couldn't do everything you could do otherwise.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The people in the demo scene on the Atari ST were also very involved in the game side of things. Some demo groups even started their own game companies and made some of the best games for the ST with the techniques they had invented, like the mentioned ability to put graphics where the borders should have been.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">One example of this is in the game <a href="/lethal-xcess-wings-of-death-ii/3030-11117/" data-ref-id="3030-11117">Lethal Xcess</a>. <a href="/thalion-software/3010-1964/" data-ref-id="3010-1964">Thalion Software</a> who made the game, put the HUD in the upper border of the screen so that the playing field wouldn't be smaller. Thalion Software was comprised of people from various demo groups and made some of the best games for the Atari ST, like <a href="/amberstar/3030-10464/" data-ref-id="3030-10464">Amberstar</a> and <a href="/enchanted-land/3030-23957/" data-ref-id="3030-23957">Enchanted Land</a>. Demo groups still release games, though not commercially like Thalion did.</p>
False

<Platform 'Commodore 64'>
Commodore 64
1982-08-31 00:00:00
Commodore

17000000
<h2>Summary</h2><p>The Commodore 64 (C64) is an 8-bit home computer released in August of 1982, and was the third system made by Commodore International. The starting price was $595 USD. The computer was named for its 64 kilobytes of RAM; superior to most mainstream personal computers at the time. The C64 is occasionally rendered: C=64 due to the original system's use of the "commodore symbol" which resembles a 'C' merged with an equal sign.</p><h2>History</h2><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/397494-home_c64_perip.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-397494" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.1633333333333" data-width="300" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 300px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:116.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/397494-home_c64_perip.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-397494"><img alt="Commodore 64" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/397494-home_c64_perip.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/397494-home_c64_perip.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300"></a><figcaption>Commodore 64</figcaption></figure><p>The Commodore 64 began development when Commodore Inc. bought MOS Technology. Unable to sell their technology in the mass market, MOS Tech approached Commodore in hopes of collaborating with their latest 6501 microprocessor. MOS had hoped to sell the microprocessor to console manufactures, but Commodore had other plans. MOS was also eager to sell their new SID sound chip in hopes that partnering with Commodore would give them an edge in the digital sound market. The iconic sound of the MOS SID chip was considered groundbreaking at the time, and video game sound composers used it to create entire in-game audio tracks.</p><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8273/1134666-clip_image001_0050.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1134666" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.0233333333333" data-width="300" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 300px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:102.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8273/1134666-clip_image001_0050.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1134666"><img alt="Jack Tramiel" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8273/1134666-clip_image001_0050.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8273/1134666-clip_image001_0050.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300"></a><figcaption>Jack Tramiel</figcaption></figure><p><a href="/jack-tramiel/3040-98032/" data-ref-id="3040-98032">Jack Tramiel</a>, founder of Commodore Business Machines, felt that the microprocessor was perfect for the new line of computers he had in mind. The chip would be the main microprocessor for the <a href="/vic-20/3045-30/" data-ref-id="3045-30">VIC-20</a>.</p><p>The project was given to Commodore Japan; an engineering team led by <a href="/y-terakura/3040-65272/" data-ref-id="3040-65272">Yash Terakura</a> created the VIC-1001 for the <a href="/japan/3035-37/" data-ref-id="3035-37">Japanese </a>market. The VIC-20 was marketed in Japan as VIC-1001 before VIC-20 was introduced to the US. The VIC-20 became a massive success.</p><p>Tramiel wanted to expand with the success of the VIC-20, but wanted a more powerful computer, while retaining a low price.</p><figure data-align="left" data-size="small" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2757760" data-ratio="0.5625" data-width="1920" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 1920px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:56.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2757760"><img alt="Yash Terakura" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 1920w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Yash Terakura" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 1920w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/13/139866/2757760-8729164817-maxre.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Yash Terakura</figcaption></figure><p>Commodore began a game console/computer hybrid project that would use the new chipscalled the <i>Ultimax, </i>or alternatively the <i><a title="Commodore MAX Machine" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodore_MAX_Machine" rel="nofollow">Commodore MAX Machine</a></i>, engineered by <a href="/y-terakura/3040-65272/" data-ref-id="3040-65272">Yash Terakura</a> from Commodore Japan. This project was eventually cancelled after just a few machines were manufactured for the Japanese market. The Commodore MAX hardware was the basis for the Commodore 64, and was the first computer of the C64 hardware family.</p><p>During the 1982 Consumer Electronics Show, the Commodore 64 was announced to enthusiasm. When the C64 was rolled out, it had an extremely low $595 price tag. Companies such as Intel and Atari had no idea how Commodore was making money on the computer. In reality, Commodore was using new and sometimes untested tech for their computer as opposed to licensing older tech from large competing corporations.</p><h2>Reception and Market Share</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/2238/379346-karateka_c64_8.png" data-ref-id="1300-379346" data-size="medium" data-ratio="0.625" data-width="320" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 320px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:62.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/2238/379346-karateka_c64_8.png" data-ref-id="1300-379346"><img alt="C64 Version of Karateka" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/2238/379346-karateka_c64_8.png" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/2238/379346-karateka_c64_8.png 320w"><noscript><img alt="C64 Version of Karateka" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/2238/379346-karateka_c64_8.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/2238/379346-karateka_c64_8.png 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>C64 Version of Karateka</figcaption></figure><p>Throughout its 12 year lifespan, the C64 sold over 17 million units. As such, it remains one of the best-selling personal computer models of all time. From 1983-1985, the Commodore 64 dominated the personal computer market with a 40% share. It then went on to outsell former market leaders Apple and Intel, and led Commodore to winning the personal computer market war. Its successor, the <a href="/commodore-128/3045-58/" data-ref-id="3045-58">Commodore 128</a>, was released in 1985 and was fully backwards compatible with most C64 software but was far less successful than the C64.</p><p>In all, about 10,000 commercial software were made for the C64, including development tools, office applications, games, and more. The C64 is currently used by hobbyists, and C64 games are available for purchase on the Wii Shop Channel in Europe and the United States.</p><h2>Hardware Specifications</h2><ul><li><b>CPU</b>: 8-bit MOS Technology 6510, 1.023 MHz</li><li><b>Memory</b>: 64KB RAM</li><li><b>Graphics</b>: MOS Technology VIC-II, 16 colors</li><li><b>Sound</b>: MOS Technology SID 6581/8580</li></ul><h3>SID Music</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/1303137-6581.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1303137" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="300" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 300px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/1303137-6581.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1303137"><img alt="SID 6581" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/1303137-6581.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/1303137-6581.jpg 300w"><noscript><img alt="SID 6581" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/1303137-6581.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9560/1303137-6581.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300"></noscript></a><figcaption>SID 6581</figcaption></figure><p>The Commodore 64's SID chip played a prominent role in the development of video game music, <a href="/chiptune/3015-3745/" data-ref-id="3015-3745">chiptunes</a>, and the demo, crack, and warez scenes. SID (itself an acronym for Sound Interface Device) was created by Robert Yannes, an engineer who later moved to the synthesizer industry. Yannes stated that he felt previous computer sound chips had been designed by people who did not understand music, and sought to design a chip that would be useful for musicians. Previously, there had been a single chip for both audio and video, so a dedicated chip for audio provided musicians with much more freedom and range.</p><p>The initial run of the SID chip, the SID 6581, was extremely popular, and instrumental in the success of the Commodore 64. It allowed for much more freedom comparative to previous chips, such as three oscillators, each of which could produce four separate waveforms. Additionally, many composers were able to find loopholes to make their music even more complex, such as a glitch that essentially created a fourth channel for 4-bit samples. Some games that took advantage of this were <a href="/impossible-mission/3030-14849/" data-ref-id="3030-14849">Impossible Mission</a> and <a href="/ghostbusters/3030-11874/" data-ref-id="3030-11874">Ghostbusters</a>, both of which used voice samples.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1843060" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="480" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 480px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1843060"><img alt="Many cracktros, such as this one, took advantage of the SID's capabilities" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Many cracktros, such as this one, took advantage of the SID's capabilities" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1843060-0.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Many cracktros, such as this one, took advantage of the SID's capabilities</figcaption></figure><p>Many well-known composers and developers had their start with the SID chip, such as <a href="/rob-hubbard/3040-2834/" data-ref-id="3040-2834">Rob Hubbard</a> and <a href="/martin-galway/3040-5357/" data-ref-id="3040-5357">Martin Galway</a>. These men have contributed to such games as <a href="/arkanoid/3030-15107/" data-ref-id="3030-15107">Arkanoid</a>, <a href="/wizball/3030-15894/" data-ref-id="3030-15894">Wizball</a>, <a href="/yie-ar-kung-fu/3030-15480/" data-ref-id="3030-15480">Yie Ar Kung-Fu</a>, <a href="/commando/3030-6850/" data-ref-id="3030-6850">Commando</a>, <a href="/monty-on-the-run/3030-105/" data-ref-id="3030-105">Monty on the Run</a>, and <a href="/skate-or-die/3030-8819/" data-ref-id="3030-8819">Skate or Die!</a>. The composition process was initially very rough; composers would have to input a sequence of hexadecimal bytes where each line would describe the note, pitch, length, etc. of the sound being played.</p><p>By the mid-1980s, SID musicians were improving to a point where they could effectively emulate "real" music. Games such as <a href="/times-of-lore/3030-6900/" data-ref-id="3030-6900">Times of Lore</a> were able to use mainstream songs as inspiration, and adapt that to a SID song. Also at this time, demo groups began to rise in popularity, with several Commodore 64 magazines releasing songs from their readers. The impact of SID music is evident as well, with songs still being developed to this day. While many musicians utilize emulation, some continue to use the original SID chips.</p>

<Platform 'MSX'>
MSX
1983-06-16 00:00:00
ASCII Corporation

<h2>History</h2><p>The MSX home computer architecture standard was conceived by <a href="/ascii-corporation/3010-805/" data-ref-id="3010-805">ASCII Microsoft</a> executive <a href="/kazuhiko-nishio/3040-92150/" data-ref-id="3040-92150">Kazuhiko Nishi</a>. Inspired by the success of VHS as a standard for video cassette recorders he had proposed the MSX standard as a single industry standard for home computers. This was many years before the <a href="/pc/3045-94/" data-ref-id="3045-94">PC</a> later became a standardized home computer architecture.</p><p>The MSX standard was first formally announced in 1983 and was followed by three other standards: MSX2 (1986), MSX2 (1988) and MSX turbo R (1990).</p><p>Over a hundred different companies have manufactured MSX computers. The list of companies includes: <a href="/sony-computer-entertainment-america/3010-313/" data-ref-id="3010-313">Sony</a>, <a href="/panasonic-interactive-media/3010-1546/" data-ref-id="3010-1546">Panasonic</a>, Sanyo, JVC, Spectravideo, <a href="/toshiba-corporation/3010-2638/" data-ref-id="3010-2638">Toshiba</a>, Canon, <a href="/casio-co-ltd/3010-2008/" data-ref-id="3010-2008">Casio</a>, Dragon, Dyndata, <a href="/fujitsu/3010-6596/" data-ref-id="3010-6596">Fujitsu</a>, Hitachi, Sharp/Epcom, <a href="/pioneer-productions/3010-845/" data-ref-id="3010-845">Pioneer</a>, Alalamia, Mitsubishi, NEC, <a href="/philips-interactive-media-inc/3010-48/" data-ref-id="3010-48">Philips</a>, Radiola, Schneider, Talent, Telematica, Yamaha, Yashica, Aster International, General, Samsung, Hyosung, Limco, Oric, Radofin.</p><p>Although it was developed by Microsoft's Japanese partner, Microsoft itself showed little support for the project beyond making the system software, and the MSX-based machines were seldom seen in the <a href="/the-united-states-of-america/3035-396/" data-ref-id="3035-396">United States</a>. Most of the Japanese companies decided to avoid the intensely competitive U.S. market, only Spectravideo and Yamaha decided otherwise. The MSX computers became popular in <a href="/japan/3035-37/" data-ref-id="3035-37">Japan</a>, <a href="/south-korea/3035-2410/" data-ref-id="3035-2410">South Korea</a>, <a href="/brazil/3035-720/" data-ref-id="3035-720">Brazil</a>, <a href="/the-netherlands/3035-1279/" data-ref-id="3035-1279">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/france/3035-197/" data-ref-id="3035-197">France</a>, <a href="/spain/3035-527/" data-ref-id="3035-527">Spain</a>, <a href="/finland/3035-1568/" data-ref-id="3035-1568">Finland</a>, Kuwait and former <a href="/soviet-union/3035-507/" data-ref-id="3035-507">Soviet Union</a>. A total of 5 million MSX-based units were sold world-wide.</p><h2><b>Standard definition</b></h2><p><b>MSX</b></p><ul><li>Processor: Zilog Z80A 3,579 MHz (8-bit)</li><li>ROM: 32 KB</li><li>RAM: 8 KB minimum (Most machines provided 64K.)</li><li>Video Display Processor: Texas Instruments TMS-9918/TMS-9928/TMS-9929</li><li>Video RAM: 16 KB (Maximum resolution: 256192 pixels with 16 colors)</li><li>Sound chip: General Instrument AY-3-8910 (PSG)</li><li>Connector for tape/data recorder.</li><li>A Centronics interface (interface for connecting printers and other parallel devices)</li><li>At least one joystick/mouse/paddle/trackball/graphic tablet connector. (Most computers have two.)</li><li>At least one expansion port.</li><li>A keyboard with At least 70 keys (including five function keys with ten programmable functions and four arrow keys).</li></ul><p><b>MSX2</b> (only changed to MSX listed)</p><ul><li>ROM: 48 KB</li><li>RAM: 64 KB minimum</li><li>Video Display Processor: Yamaha V9938</li><li>Video RAM: At least 64kB (usually 128kB)</li><li>Sound chip: Yamaha YM2149 (PSG)</li><li>Clock chip: Ricoh RP5C01 (or compatible)</li><li>A 3.5" Floppy disk drive was common</li></ul><p><b>MSX2+</b> (Only released in Japan)</p><ul><li>Processor: Zilog Z80 compatible running at 3.58 MHz or more (5.37 MHz versions were available)</li><li>ROM: 64 KB</li><li>RAM: At least 64 KB</li><li>Video Display Processor: Yamaha V9958</li><li>Sound chip: Yamaha YM2149 (PSG)</li><li>Optional sound chip: Yamaha YM2413 (OPLL) (MSX-Music)</li><li>Clock chip: RP5C01</li><li>3.5" Floppy disk drive was very common</li></ul><p><b>MSX turbo R</b> (Only two machines were made and they were only released in Japan)</p><ul><li>Processor: R800 (7.16 MHz) and Zilog Z80A compatible</li><li>ROM: 96 KB</li><li>RAM: 256 KB (FS-A1ST) or 512 KB (FS-A1GT)</li><li>Video Display Processor: Yamaha V9958</li><li>Sound chip: Yamaha YM2413 (OPLL) (MSX-Music)</li><li>Sound chip: PCM synthesizer 8-bit (sample rate 16kHz)</li><li>Sound chip: MIDI in/out (FS-A1GT only)</li><li>3.5" double sided double density (720kB) disk drive</li></ul>

16
<Platform 'ZX Spectrum'>
ZX Spectrum
1982-04-20 00:00:00
Sinclair Research Ltd.
249.00
5000000
<h2>Overview</h2><p>The <a data-ref-id="3010-938" href="/sinclair-research-ltd/3010-938/">Sinclair</a> ZX Spectrum was the successor to the ZX 81 released in the <a data-ref-id="3035-492" href="/united-kingdom/3035-492/">UK</a> by Sinclair Research Ltd. in 1982. Affectionately regarded as the "Speccy," the Spectrum was Sinclair's first computer capable of color output, and is widely recognized for its complex 40-key rubber keyboard. The ZX Spectrum was particularly successful in the UK, and was one of the most popular personal computer throughout Europe in the 1980s. The popularity of the Spectrum in Europe can be compared to that of the <a data-ref-id="3045-14" href="/commodore-64/3045-14/">Commodore 64</a> in <a data-ref-id="3035-1664" href="/north-america/3035-1664/">North America</a> as it was made to be the cheapest available color computer at the time. It was a well supported platform with a variety of companies developing software for it, including many games. There were eight models of the Spectrum produced between 1982 and 1987, and the recognizable computer spawned a good deal of clones and imitators.</p><h2>Technical Specifications</h2><ul><li><em>CPU</em> - Zilog Z80A 3.5 MHz processor</li><li><em>Memory</em> - 16 Kb / 48 Kb / 128 Kb</li><li><em>Video</em> - 256 x 192 resolution, 15 colors (7 shades bright mode for each black), each 8 x 8 pixel square limited to a max of 2 colors.</li><li><em>Audio</em> - 16K / 48K models have 1 channel with 1-bit sound using a 10-octave built-in beepe. 128K model has 3 channels of 4-bit sound using an AY-3-8912 sound chip. Some 48k models use the AY chip.</li><li><em>Media</em> - Cassette tapes via the cassette recorder peripheral</li><li><em>Built-in Language</em> - Sinclair Basic</li><li><em>Keyboard</em> - QWERTY layout with 40 rubber keys. Keys have up to 6 functions each.</li><li><em>Size/Weight</em> - 23 x 14.4 x 3 cm / 550g</li><li><em>IO Ports</em> - Expansion port, RF video out</li><li><em>Power Supply</em> - External PSU, 9v DC, 1.4A</li></ul><h2>Models</h2><h3>ZX Spectrum 16K</h3><p>The 16K model was the entry level model manufactured by <a data-ref-id="3010-938" href="/sinclair-research-ltd/3010-938/">Sinclair</a> in 1982. It launched alongside the 48K model and was very similar except for the memory difference. The original ZX Spectrum is very widely recognized for its signature rubber keyboard and rainbow flair in the corner of the device. It was small, easy to use and cheap. The 16K model launched at 125 and later dropped to 99. A 32K RAM upgrade was available for 16K users to upgrade their systems to 48K. Users could mail in their systems and they'd have their Spectrums fitted with a 32K daughterboard that made it essentially identical to the 48K model. An interesting note is that to save money on these RAM expansions, Sinclair used defective 64K chips with only 32K RAm actually working or available. Third parties released 32K RAM expansion packs that plugged into the expansion port as well.</p><p>The <a data-ref-id="3055-2361" href="/keyboard/3055-2361/">keyboard</a> of the computer is considered to be terrible for typists. The rubber keys are mushy, and the fact that keys can have up to 6 functions each means that even typing basic commands requires extensive use of the function keys. The 256x192 resolution of the computer was above average for its time, and the choice of 8 colors (15 including "BRIGHT" colors) set it apart from the competition. The downside to the Spectrum's color was that only two colors could be in a single 8x8px square. This caused a problem called <em>attribute clash (also known as color clash)</em> which required creative graphic design to circumvent.</p><h3>ZX Spectrum 48K</h3><p>The 48K model launched alongside the 16K model in 1982 and retailed for 175. The price was later reduced to 129. The 48K model is virtually the same as the 16K model, with the obvious exception being the upgraded RAM.</p><h3>ZX Spectrum+</h3><p>In 1984, two years after the release of the original Spectrum, Sinclair released the upgraded ZX Spectrum+. The ZX Spectrum+ was a 48K Spectrum in a Sinclair-QL style case (another computer launched in 1984). The most important change with the Spectrum+ was the introduction of a "professional keyboard" which had injection-molded plastic keys rather than rubber ones. The keys still rested on a rubber membrane and while it improved upon the original design, many users did not find it completely satisfactory.</p><p>The ZX Spectrum+ retailed for 179.95 and a do-it-yourself upgrade kit was released for 50, which allowed a user to upgrade any rubber-keyed Spectrum with plastic keys. The price was cut to 129.95 with the release of the Spectrum 128. The plastic keys on the Spectrum had a tendency to fall off, and retailers expressed failure rates ranging from 5% to as high as 30%.</p><h3><strong>ZX Spectrum 128</strong></h3><p>The Spectrum 128 was an evolution of the ZX Spectrum lineup, sporting 128K RAM, 3 channel audio, MIDI compatibility, a serial port, RGB video out, 32Kb ROM, an improved BASIC editor, and an external keypad. The Spectrum 128 also fixed the "dot crawl" problem that earlier units suffered from. It was developed by Sinclair in conjunction with their spanish distributer Investronica. It was unveiled and launched in Spain in 1985 for 44,250 pesatas; the English release was pushed back until 1986 because of unsold Spectrum inventory and retailed for 179.95. The English Spectrum 128 did not include an external keypad and the keypad port was renamed "AUX." The keypad was released separately for 19.95 and did not sell well.</p><p>The Spectrum 128 suffered from being released after the holiday season to an uninterested public. The new hardware had compatibility issues with old software and peripherals. Months after the Spectrum 128 was launched, Amstrad took over Sinclair's computing business and discontinued the Spectrum 128. It only retailed for about half a year.</p><h3><strong>ZX Spectrum +2</strong></h3><p>The <a data-ref-id="3010-3924" href="/amstrad/3010-3924/">Amstrad</a> ZX Spectrum +2 was the first Spectrum-branded computer released after Amstrad acquired the Sinclair brand and computing business. It was released in 1987 for 139149 and marked a huge turning point for the Spectrum brand. It was the first Spectrum to be released with a proper spring-loaded keyboard, two joystick ports, and a built in tape recorder. The case is dark gray and similar to the <a data-ref-id="3045-11" href="/amstrad-cpc/3045-11/">Amstrad CPC</a> 464.</p><p>The computer was also released in black as the <strong>ZX Spectrum +2A</strong> with the revised motherboard of the Spectrum 3. The internals were similar to those of the Spectrum 128, except the ROM was changed around a bit causing further incompatibility issues. A <strong>ZX Spectrum +2B</strong> followed in late 1987 when manufacturing of the machine moved from Hong Kong to Taiwan, but the internals remained identical. But before they made these in taiwan they made these in the uk</p><p>The Spectrum +2 was the first Spectrum marketed almost exclusive as a game machine, and as a result it sold very well. One of the Spectrum +2 bundles was the <a data-ref-id="3030-7365" href="/james-bond-007/3030-7365/">James Bond 007</a> Action Pack, which game with a <a data-ref-id="3015-2354" href="/light-gun/3015-2354/">light gun</a> and <a data-ref-id="3025-369" href="/james-bond/3025-369/">James Bond</a> games.</p><h3>ZX Spectrum +3</h3><p>The Spectrum +3 marks the final and most advanced Spectrum computer, and was released in 1987 for 249, with a price drop bringing it down to 199. It remained in production until 1990. It featured a 3" floppy disk drive, a parallel print port, and a new ROM. The Spectrum +3 also had a revised motherboard with drastically fewer chips than previous models. Continuing the trend of new Spectrum models, this more advanced computer was even more severely incompatible with old software and hardware. The 3" floppy drive used proprietary Amstrad disks and the computer as a whole was drastically overpriced compared to more advanced competing computers at the time such as the the <a data-ref-id="3045-1" href="/amiga/3045-1/">Amiga</a> 500.</p><p>Like the Spectrum +2, the Spectrum +3 was marketed as game device and sold in various "action packs" however it did not enjoy nearly the same success as its predecessor. The incompatibilities, 3" Amstrad floppy drive, and most importantly death of the 8-bit computer market all contributed to the computer's eventual failure and the end of the Spectrum line.</p><h3>Clones</h3><p>The ZX Spectrum is perhaps the most cloned computer line in all history. Sinclair licensed the Spectrum's design to Timex and a North American derivative named the Timex Sinclair 2068 was released. This "official" clone was largely incompatible with Sinclair software and within two years of the Timex-Sinclair partnership, Timex dropped out due to huge losses. Many more illegal clones were produced in countries such as the <a data-ref-id="3035-507" href="/soviet-union/3035-507/">USSR</a>, Czechoslovakia, Romania, <a data-ref-id="3035-1083" href="/hong-kong/3035-1083/">Hong Kong</a>, <a data-ref-id="3035-2867" href="/argentina/3035-2867/">Argentina</a>, <a data-ref-id="3035-720" href="/brazil/3035-720/">Brazil</a> and Spectrum clones continue to be produced in <a data-ref-id="3035-38" href="/russia/3035-38/">Russia</a>. The SAM Coupe is often considered a Spectrum clone because of its emulated Spectrum compatibility, as well as the fact it was produced by a Spectrum peripheral manufacturer. Dozens of clones and variants of the ZX Spectrum are known to exist.</p><h2>Software</h2><p>The ZX Spectrum had a very simple architechture but an extraordinarily diverse software library consisting of over 14,000 titles. Software applications included programming language implementations, Sinclair BASIC extensions, word processors, databases, office software, artist tools, music composition, and a great deal of games. Spectrum software was originally distributed on cassette tapes, read in with the tape recorder expansion. Load times were very long and could be anywhere from 3-12 minutes depending on the size of the program. While data on cassettes was reliable and widely used, the release of the Spectrum 3 with its build in floppy drive brought about around 700 floppy disk titles to the Spectrum's library throughout the decade after its release.</p><h3>Notable Games</h3><ul><li><a data-ref-id="3030-7959" href="/3d-deathchase/3030-7959/">3D Deathchase</a></li><li>All or Nothing</li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-16034" href="/batman-the-movie/3030-16034/">Batman: The Movie</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-2991" href="/boulder-dash/3030-2991/">Boulder Dash</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-13281" href="/chase-hq/3030-13281/">Chase H.Q.</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-8827" href="/dizzy-the-ultimate-cartoon-adventure/3030-8827/">Dizzy</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-7068" href="/head-over-heels/3030-7068/">Head Over Heels</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-5005" href="/jet-set-willy/3030-5005/">Jet Set Willy</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-3209" href="/magicland-dizzy/3030-3209/">Magicland Dizzy</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-17856" href="/manic-miner/3030-17856/">Manic Miner</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-7405" href="/midnight-resistance/3030-7405/">Midnight Resistance</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-14869" href="/operation-wolf/3030-14869/">Operation Wolf</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-13745" href="/r-type/3030-13745/">R-Type</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-10764" href="/rainbow-islands/3030-10764/">Rainbow Islands</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-18455" href="/rebelstar/3030-18455/">Rebelstar</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-21323" href="/robocop/3030-21323/">RoboCop</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-18433" href="/robocop-2/3030-18433/">RoboCop 2</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-13537" href="/stop-the-express/3030-13537/">Stop the Express</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-9769" href="/target-renegade/3030-9769/">Target: Renegade</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-4798" href="/the-sentinel/3030-4798/">The Sentinel</a></li><li><a data-ref-id="3030-7275" href="/tll-tornado-low-level/3030-7275/">Tornado Low Level</a></li></ul><h2>Peripherals</h2><p>A few first party peripherals were released for the ZX Spectrum including:</p><p><em>ZX Printer</em> - This was already on the market when the Spectrum was released. It was a ZX81 printer backwards compatible with the Spectrum.</p><p><em>ZX Interface 1</em> - An interface that added 8KB of ROM, a serial port, a LAN interface called ZX Net, and an interface for up to 8 ZX Microdrives, a propriety form of fast tape storage.</p><p><em>ZX Interface 2</em> - An interface that added 2 controller ports and a ROM cartridge slot which had instant loading. Only 10 games were commercially produced on ROM cartridges.</p><p><em>ZX Microdrive</em> - A small, fast, and somewhat unreliable tape-roll storage medium released by Sinclair.</p><p>A very popular third party peripheral was the <em>Kempston Joystick Interface</em>. It allowed the user of controllers complying with the Atari 2600 standard to be used in Spectrum games and was greatly supported. Many other peripherals were released such as disk drives, controller interfaces, serial interfaces, RAM upgrades, speech synthesizers, a drum kit and even a modem.</p>

1984-01-31 00:00:00
Apple Inc.

<h2>History</h2><p>The Macintosh was designed as part of a skunkworks operation originally headed by Jef Raskin. Raskin planned to create an easy to use and affordable appliance computer for use by ordinary people. The project was ongoing at the same time as <a href="/apple-inc/3010-5815/" data-ref-id="3010-5815" data-mce-href="/apple-inc/3010-5815/">Apple</a> founder <a href="/steve-jobs/3040-9951/" data-ref-id="3040-9951" data-mce-href="/steve-jobs/3040-9951/">Steve Jobs</a> was leading development of Lisa, Apple's intended flagship computer. Despite his heavy involvement in the inital stages of the Lisa project, Jobs eventually caught wind of the Macintosh project and gradually turned his full attention to it. In 1981, Raskin departed the team over a dispute with Jobs, making Steve the de facto leader.</p><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/66/521728-macintosh.gif" data-ref-id="1300-521728" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.145" data-width="200" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 200px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:114.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/66/521728-macintosh.gif" data-ref-id="1300-521728"><img alt="The Original Macintosh" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/66/521728-macintosh.gif" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/66/521728-macintosh.gif 200w" sizes="(max-width: 200px) 100vw, 200px" data-width="200"></a><figcaption>The Original Macintosh</figcaption></figure><p>It was under Jobs that the Mac became a GUI-oriented computer, influenced by a tour given to Apple staff of Xerox's Palo Alto Research Center which included looks at PARC's Alto computer and the related Smalltalk software. The computer eventually released in 1984 as a vertically-oriented all-in-one and was gradually revised over the course of the decade. Eventually, Apple began to offer Macs in different formats, ranging from standard desktops to early laptops and other portable designs.</p><p>The Mac's popularity was at first fairly high, but after Steve Jobs was stripped of power within Apple (eventually departing to form NeXT), executives who did not fully understand the platform began to devalue it (notably, Apple was criticized in the nineties for offering an absurd amount of Macs which were too similar and had generic names) and the platform struggled. To compound these problems, Apple's Mac System (later Mac OS), the OS powering the Macs, was hopelessly outdated and needed replacing.</p><p>Unfortunately, Apple's internal software R&amp;D had become bloated and ineffectual, stalling development on the next-generation Mac OS codenamed Copland. In 1996, Apple wrote it off as a sinking ship and decided to source the basis for their next-generation operating system from elsewhere. Several options were considered, such as licensing Microsoft's NT architecture and purchasing Be, Inc. for its BeOS. In the end though, it was Apple founder Steve Jobs and his NeXT outfit which Apple decided to buy, allowing them to base what would become OS X (as in the roman numeral for 10) on NeXT's powerful and robust OpenSTEP OS.</p><p>This also brought Jobs back into the company. He quickly assumed control and began to turn the Mac platform around. Jobs trimmed the bloat from the product line and steadily began to focus on releasing stylish computers like the iMac and iBook for consumers and powerhouses for the Mac's creative professional base like the PowerBook and PowerMac lines.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/66/428003-imac2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-428003" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.6" data-width="900" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 900px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:60.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/66/428003-imac2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-428003"><img alt="iMac, Apple's Flagship Mac" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/66/428003-imac2.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/66/428003-imac2.jpg 900w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/66/428003-imac2.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/66/428003-imac2.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>iMac, Apple's Flagship Mac</figcaption></figure><p>Since then, the Mac has become known as a stylish computer for the image conscious, as well as a great tool for artists, writers, film editors and so on. Since approximately halfway through the first decade of the 21st Century, Apple has begun to play up the Mac as a games platform, something for which it had developed a reputation for being poor at. This took several forms, most visibly the appearance of <a href="/electronic-arts/3010-1/" data-ref-id="3010-1" data-mce-href="/electronic-arts/3010-1/">EA</a> at an Apple event to promote the fact that they were bringing a number of games to the platform.</p><p>Despite the perception of the Mac as a second-rate games platform (by comparison to the dominant Windows PC), it has played host to a number of popular exclusives, most notably much of <a href="/bungie-studios/3010-476/" data-ref-id="3010-476" data-mce-href="/bungie-studios/3010-476/">Bungie's</a> earlier games (before the Microsoft buyout) catalog. In fact, Bungie's <a href="/halo-combat-evolved/3030-2600/" data-ref-id="3030-2600" data-mce-href="/halo-combat-evolved/3030-2600/">Halo</a>, a huge hit for the Xbox, was originally a Mac exclusive, built on <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/opengl/3015-5192/" data-ref-id="3015-5192" data-mce-href="//www.giantbomb.com/opengl/3015-5192/">OpenGL</a>. The game later found its way onto Mac as a port of the PC release.</p><h3>Windows Gaming on the Mac</h3><p>On April 5, 2006 Apple released a beta version of a new piece of software called Boot Camp. Due to the recent change in architecture - PowerPC to Intel - Mac and Windows computers were now more similar than ever. Boot Camp embraced this by allowing the user to install Windows on a Mac. Windows XP - and later Windows Vista and 7 - now ran natively on Macintosh computers, making gaming on Mac's easier than ever.</p><p>With the release of Mac OS 10.5 (Leopard), Boot Camp comes pre-installed on all Mac's and is thus officially supported by Apple. Although many Mac users are unwilling to install Windows on their computer, Boot Camp still offers Mac users something they've never had before - the ability to run any PC game 100% natively.</p><h3>Steam for Mac</h3><p>On March 8th, 2010, <a href="/valve-corporation/3010-1374/" data-ref-id="3010-1374" data-mce-href="/valve-corporation/3010-1374/">Valve</a> announced that it would begin a new <a href="/steam/3015-718/" data-ref-id="3015-718" data-mce-href="/steam/3015-718/">Steam</a> program for the Mac. Under this new system, Valve games would be able to run natively on the Mac, and 3rd parties would be able to release a Mac version of their game on Steam. Also announced is a program called Steam Play, where players who buy the Mac version of a game will also be able to download the PC version of the same game free of charge. Steam for Mac was released on May 12, 2010.</p><h3>Mac App Store</h3><p>Mimicking their strategy with the <a href="/iphone/3045-96/" data-ref-id="3045-96" data-mce-href="/iphone/3045-96/">iOS</a> platform, Apple has recently added an integrated App Store to the Mac OS. Users can purchase Applications in the same way as on iOS. Apps are then downloaded to a user's system and the App Store App itself keeps track of updates for these Apps. In addition, once an App is purchased, users are free to install it on any other Mac computer by logging into the Mac App Store with the same Apple Account.</p><p>This service, just like on iOS, extends to games and already a number of Publishers have made classic and more recent titles available for purchase on the Mac App Store. These games range from recent and current hits to old favourites, to popular indie games like the <a href="/penny-arcade-adventures-on-the-rain-slick-precipice-of-darkness-episode-one/3030-20696/" data-ref-id="3030-20696" data-mce-href="/penny-arcade-adventures-on-the-rain-slick-precipice-of-darkness-episode-one/3030-20696/">Penny Arcade Adventures</a> episodes and also ports of existing iOS games like <a href="/angry-birds/3025-1879/" data-ref-id="3025-1879" data-mce-href="/angry-birds/3025-1879/">Angry Birds</a>.</p>
True

<Platform 'PlayStation Portable'>
PlayStation Portable
2005-03-24 00:00:00

<h2>Overview</h2><figure style="width: 1363px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/2122742-psp.png" data-ref-id="1300-2122742" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.973587674248" data-width="1363" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:197.4%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/2122742-psp.png" data-ref-id="1300-2122742"><img alt="PSP revisions from top to bottom: The 1000, 2000, 3000, and Go models." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/2122742-psp.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/2122742-psp.png 1363w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/0/1992/2122742-psp.png 972w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1992/2122742-psp.png 486w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/2122742-psp.png 324w" sizes="(max-width: 324px) 100vw, 324px" data-width="324"></a><figcaption>PSP revisions from top to bottom: The 1000, 2000, 3000, and Go models.</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The PlayStation Portable (or PSP) was released March 24, 2005 and marked Sony's first entry into the handheld gaming market.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">In late 2007, the PSP was upgraded with a new hardware variation known by the model number PSP-2000.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">On August 20, 2008, at the Leipzig Games Convention, Sony announced a new revision of the PSP, the PSP-3000.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">On June 2, 2009, at <a href="/e3-2009/3015-3244/" data-ref-id="3015-3244">E3</a> in <a href="/los-angeles/3035-490/" data-ref-id="3035-490">Los Angeles</a>, Sony announced the PSPgo, which removed the UMD drive and instead was the first dedicated gaming handheld to be completely dependent on downloaded software.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">In August, 2011, at Gamescom in <a href="/cologne/3035-3196/" data-ref-id="3035-3196">Cologne</a>, Sony announced the budget priced PSP-E1000 for PAL regions, which retained a UMD drive but removed network support. This would be the last revision of the PSP.</p><h2>PSP-2000 (PSP Slim)</h2><figure style="width: 896px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3161/316751-picture_10.png" data-ref-id="1300-316751" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.53683035714286" data-width="896" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:53.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3161/316751-picture_10.png" data-ref-id="1300-316751"><img alt="PSP 1000 (bottom) vs. Slim (top)" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/3161/316751-picture_10.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3161/316751-picture_10.png 896w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/3161/316751-picture_10.png 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/3161/316751-picture_10.png 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>PSP 1000 (bottom) vs. Slim (top)</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">During the E3 conference of 2007, Sony released several details about the PSP's first hardware revision. The model number is PSP-2000, then commonly referred to as the PSP Slim. The Slim replaced the original (or ''phat'') PSP on the market and had significant hardware changes. The amount of RAM in the PSP-2000 was doubled from 32 megabytes to 64 megabytes. This added RAM was primarily used to decrease UMD load times, and to improve the memory limit when browsing web pages. The Slim's battery was manufactured to supply the same amount of playtime as the Phat battery while attaining a smaller form factor. The WLAN switch was relocated to the top of the device, and the UMD drive now required the user to open it manually, as opposed to the spring-loaded version in the PSP-1000 that was activated using a switch.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The PSP Slim was released in September of 2007.</p><h2>PSP-3000 (PSP Brite)</h2><figure style="width: 500px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-589004" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.75" data-width="500" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-589004"><img alt="PSP Slim vs. PSP-3000" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg 500w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="PSP Slim vs. PSP-3000" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg 500w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/4705/589004-pspshowdown2.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>PSP Slim vs. PSP-3000</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Sony officially announced the PSP-3000 at the 2008 Leipzig Games Convention. Improvements over the PSP-2000 included a built in mic and an improved LCD screen. The new screen features twice the color gamut, five times the contrast ratio, and a response time that is twice as fast as the PSP-2000's LCD screen. It also features a new anti-reflective technology for improved viewing of the LCD screen in outdoor areas.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The PSP-3000 was released in Japan, Europe and North America in October.</p><h2>PSPgo</h2><figure style="width: 1920px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1041137" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.5625" data-width="1920" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:56.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1041137"><img alt="PSP GO in the Flesh" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 1920w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="PSP GO in the Flesh" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 1920w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/17415/1041137-psp_go.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>PSP GO in the Flesh</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">During E3 2009, Sony revealed the PSPgo, (or PSP Go) the lightest and most physically compact PSP model. This portability is achieved using a slide-out form factor, a smaller screen (3.8 inch screen as opposed to the 4.3 inches on all other models), and the removal of the UMD drive, limiting the system to digitally downloaded games only. Under the hood, PSPgo is identical to other PSP models, with the same capabilities and OS, while also adding 16GB internal flash memory and PS3 controller support . However, the lack of UMD drive, which physically prevents it from playing disc-based games, resulted in the PSP Go being a complete commercial failure.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The 16GB internal storage space can be expanded by the use of Sony's proprietary M2 Memory Stick, unlike the other models of PSP, which use the Memory Stick ProDUO. The PSPgo existed alongside the PSP-3000 when it launched on October 1st, 2009, and unlike other PSP revisions was not intended by Sony to be a replacement for existing models.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The device proved unpopular with many gamers for various reasons, most notably the fact that PSP game discs could not be played on the PSPgo. Even if you own a game on disc, in order to play it you would have to repurchase and download the game from PSN, often at a marked up price. To make matters worse, certain PSP titles were never made available for digital download at all, making the PSPgo unattractive even to gamers who did not yet own a PSP.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Other issues include the fact that PSN games tend to be overpriced, the slow download speeds on PSP systems in general, new games being listed incorrectly in PSN store, the fact that some games suffering from glitches not found in their UMD counterparts, and poor customer service.</p><h2>PSP-E1000</h2><figure style="width: 565px" data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2137356" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.45486725663717" data-width="565" data-embed-type="image"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:45.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-2137356"><img alt="PSP-E1000" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg 565w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="PSP-E1000" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg 565w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/11512/2137356-pspe1000.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>PSP-E1000</figcaption></figure><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Announced at <a href="/gamescom-2011/3015-7065/" data-ref-id="3015-7065">Gamescom 2011</a>, the PSP-E1000 is a budget-priced PSP available only in PAL territories. Reversing course from the PSPgo, the PSP-E1000 includes a UMD drive, but does not include any network capabilities whatsoever. Slightly larger and heavier than the PSP-3000, the PSP-E1000 has a charcoal black plastic matte body similar to the slim PS3 redesign. The layout is fairly similar to the original PSP, however it moves the system buttons to a single row below the screen which is flush with the case itself. To save on costs, the PSP-E1000 removed WiFi and has only one speaker instead of two. Despite the lack of wireless connectivity, PSN content could still be accessed by connecting the PSP-E1000 to a Playstation 3, or to a PC via USB and using Sony's Media Go software.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The PSP-E100 launched at a cost of 99.99 or 85.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0"> </p><h2>Reception</h2><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Shown off at E3 2004, the portable was highly anticipated and thought of by many at the time to be superior to the <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/nintendo-ds/3045-52/" data-ref-id="3045-52">Nintendo DS </a>which was launching around the same time.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">However, leadIng up to launch whispers of long load times and a short battery life began appearing online. These criticisms were true and it was something that would haunt the system for a majority of it's life.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Another hamper to the system was a divided perspective of the handheld market. During its first few years, the PSP would often get ports or sequels of console games that many felt were ill-suited for the portable market. By 2006 and continuing into 2007, several of these PSP titles were ported to Playstation 2.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Rampant piracy was also an issue for third parties. The PSP was easily hackable and guides on how to bypass the system's security were easily available on the web. Sony attempted through several firmware updates to make hacking the system more difficult but the wheels were already in motion.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">By mid-2008 the once promising handheld's library looked barren with few games being released apart from ports. Sony would try in 2009 to give the system one last serious push with titles like <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/rock-band-unplugged/3030-25421/" data-ref-id="3030-25421">Rockband Unplugged </a>and <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/motorstorm-arctic-edge/3030-25424/" data-ref-id="3030-25424">Motorstorm Arctic Edge</a> but at that point much of the damage had already been done.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Despite this the PSP is still remembered for its best games and for being the most successful dedicated gaming handheld that wasn't made by Nintendo. The system is also known among gamers for its homebrew scene which Sony desperately tried to put a lid on.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">In Japan, the PSP continued to be phenomenally successful well past the launch date of the Playstation Vita, (the PSP's successor) and even outselling the Nintendo DS and 3DS for a period of time.</p><h2>Launch href="/wrc-world-rally-championship/3030-12503/" data-ref-id="3030-12503">World Rally Championship</a></li></ul><h2>UMD</h2><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">'Universal Media Discs' are used by the PSP for games and movies. They are small circular discs in a plastic casing. All PSP models except the PSPgo support this format, and various games include firmware updates for the PSP on their disc.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Some movies available on UMD are now included on Blu-Ray releases. The user can download a PSP version of the film from their PS3 to the PSP or PSPgo. Some examples are Godzilla and The Ugly Truth.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Copies of <a href="/lumines-puzzle-fusion/3030-5057/" data-ref-id="3030-5057">Lumines</a> featured an older firmware update which many users bought and installed to unlock their PSP. At the time this firmware update left the PSP easier to unlock. More recent firmware updates make this harder to do.</p><h2>Media Functionality</h2><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">The PSP plays most MP3 and AVC music files. The PSP's video playback options are far more limited, however. The PSP can play movie downloads and rentals from the Playstation Video store, but playing your own movies generally requires a file converter as the PSP does not support the vast majority of movie files.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Videos, games, and music can all be managed on the PSP by connecting it to your PC via a USB cable. Sony also distributes free software named Media Go, which allows users to download PSN content from their PC.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0">Each PSP requires a Memory Stick Pro Duo, which is a proprietary memory stick format created by Sony. The memory stick is typically sold separately.</p><p data-right-indent="0" data-left-indent="0"> </p><h2 style="margin-right:0px;margin-left:0px;">Custom Firmware and Homebrew</h2><p>The PSP was very well known for it's Custom Firmware scene. By opening up the battery and cutting a part of it, you could install Custom Firmware which allowed users to run unsigned software, which allowed ISOs of PSP games, emulators, and homebrew. It was later made even easier, because by some point you could just do it from files on the Memory Stick. Throughout the PSP's life, Sony tried to put a lid on this and kept updating the firmware, plugging up exploits each time, which made them infamous in the Homebrew scene. Now that the PSP has been discontinued, it's very easy to mod them, even on hardware revisions that were unmoddable during a good portion of their life, including the PSP go.</p><h2>Specifications</h2><table><tbody><tr><td>CPU</td><td>MIPS R4000-based; clocked from 1 to 333 MHz</td></tr><tr><td>Storage Capacity</td><td><b>PSP1000/2000/3000:</b> Memory Stick Duo and Memory Stick PRO Duo (1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32, 64 or 128 GB)<br /><b>PSPgo:</b> Memory Stick M2</td></tr><tr><td>Memory</td><td>32 MB (1000 model) or 64 MB (2000, 3000, and PSP Go models)</td></tr><tr><td>Display</td><td>480  272 pixels with 16.8 million colors, 16:9 widescreen TFT LCD, 3.8 in (97 mm) (PSP GO), 4.3 in (110 mm) (All other models)</td></tr><tr><td>Connectivity</td><td>Wi-Fi (802.11b),IrDA, USB</td></tr><tr><td>Dimensions</td><td><b>PSP1000:</b> 74 mm (2.9 in) (h) 170 mm (6.7 in) (w) 23 mm (0.91 in) (d)<br /><b>PSP2000/3000:</b> 71 mm (2.8 in) (h) 169 mm (6.7 in) (w) 19 mm (0.75 in) (d)<br /><b>PSPgo:</b> 69 mm (2.7 in) (h) 128 mm (5.0 in) (w) 16.5 mm (0.65 in) (d)</td></tr><tr><td>Weight</td><td><b>PSP1000:</b> 280 grams (9.9 oz)<br /><b>PSP2000/3000:</b> 189 grams (6.7 oz)<br /><b>PSPgo:</b> 158 grams (5.6 oz)</td></tr></tbody></table>

PlayStation 2

<h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/33529/1490403-playstation_2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1490403" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.1337868480726" data-width="441" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 441px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:113.4%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/33529/1490403-playstation_2.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1490403"><img alt="The PlayStation 2" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/33529/1490403-playstation_2.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/3/33529/1490403-playstation_2.jpg 441w" sizes="(max-width: 564px) 100vw, 564px" data-width="564"></a><figcaption>The PlayStation 2</figcaption></figure><p>The PlayStation 2 was developed by <a href="/sony-interactive-entertainment/3010-11938/" data-ref-id="3010-11938">Sony Interactive Entertainment</a> and was released on March 4th 2000 in <a href="/japan/3035-37/" data-ref-id="3035-37">Japan</a>, October 26th 2000 in <a href="/north-america/3035-1664/" data-ref-id="3035-1664">North America</a>, November 24th 2000 in <a href="/europe/3035-289/" data-ref-id="3035-289">Europe</a>, and November 30th 2000 in <a href="/australia/3035-292/" data-ref-id="3035-292">Australia</a>. The PlayStation 2 is powered by a 128-bit CPU that Sony co-developed with <a href="/toshiba-corporation/3010-2638/" data-ref-id="3010-2638">Toshiba</a>, known as the "Emotion Engine." It was also comprised of a "Graphics Synthesizer" chip (its GPU), a 24x speed CD-ROM (which was also a 4x DVD drive), 2 proprietary controller ports, 2 proprietary memory card slots, a DEV9 expansion bay for a network adapter, and 2 USB 1.1 ports.</p><p>The PS2 initially sold well, partly due to the strength of the PlayStation brand and the console's <a href="/backwards-compatibility/3015-1053/" data-ref-id="3015-1053">backward compatibility</a> with original PlayStation titles. It sold over 980,000 units by March 5th 2000, or one day after launch in Japan.</p><p>Its launch was not without setbacks. Manufacturing delays pushed back the original launch day of the PlayStation 2 to fall in North America. Also, a number of early PlayStation 2 units suffered from disk read errors, which subsequently prompted a class action lawsuit against Sony. Sony agreed to provide free repair services or replacement for the faulty consoles, and continued to do so until February 2005.</p><p>At the time, many people in the gaming industry predicted a close match up between the PlayStation 2, Microsoft's <a href="/xbox/3045-32/" data-ref-id="3045-32">Xbox</a>, and Nintendo's <a href="/gamecube/3045-23/" data-ref-id="3045-23">GameCube</a>. Several blockbuster games were released for the PlayStation 2 during the 2001 holiday season, which helped maintain sales momentum and hold off its rivals. <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a>'s <a href="/dreamcast/3045-37/" data-ref-id="3045-37">Dreamcast</a> was released a full 16 months before the PlayStation 2, but failed to gain momentum. Sega discontinued the Dreamcast in March of 2001, in order to focus on publishing software. </p><h4>Discontinuation</h4><p>On December 28th, 2012, Sony confirmed that the PlayStation 2 console had been discontinued in Japan. A week later, Sony confirmed that manufacture of the console had been discontinued worldwide, marking almost 13 years of production for the console. At 150 million units sold worldwide, it is the best-selling console ever.</p><h2>Launch Titles</h2><h3>Japan - March 4, 2000</h3><ul><li><a href="/a-train-6/3030-19375/"
