http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1843490-r.o.b.stack_up.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a></figure></td></tr></tbody></table><h2>Clones and Emulation</h2><h3>PlayChoice-10</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705473-pc10_mycab.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1705473" data-size="medium" data-ratio="1.3306233062331" data-width="369" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 369px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:133.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705473-pc10_mycab.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1705473"><img alt="PlayChoice-10" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 369px) 100vw, 369px" data-width="369" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705473-pc10_mycab.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705473-pc10_mycab.jpg 369w"><noscript><img alt="PlayChoice-10" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705473-pc10_mycab.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705473-pc10_mycab.jpg 369w" sizes="(max-width: 369px) 100vw, 369px" data-width="369"></noscript></a><figcaption>PlayChoice-10</figcaption></figure><p>Nintendo also released the <a href="/playchoice-10/3015-3263/" data-ref-id="3015-3263">PlayChoice-10</a>. The PlayChoice-10 was an arcade cabinet that contained full versions of ten NES games. This was to capitalize on the success of arcades at the time, as well as compete with Sega's arcade versions of the <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> <a href="/genesis/3045-6/" data-ref-id="3045-6">Genesis</a> and <a href="/sega-master-system/3045-8/" data-ref-id="3045-8">Sega Master System</a>. In addition to Nintendo's own games, many third party games also appeared on the PlayChoice-10.</p><h3>Emulators</h3><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-897595" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.66666666666667" data-width="3072" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 3072px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:66.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-897595"><img alt="Japanese Famulator" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 3072w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Japanese Famulator" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 3072w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/2/22299/897595-img_6496.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Japanese Famulator</figcaption></figure><p>After the release of the NES a lot of companies made emulators for retro gamers. One example is the new release of the NES Emulator</p><p>"Famulator" in Japan created by "Cyber Gadget". The Famulator is a slimmer, thinner, and lighter version of the original NES. Although the models look totally different the cartridges fit perfectly and run perfectly. The Famulator was priced 2,980 Japanese Yen, which adds up to roughly 30 American Dollars, creating a cheap and small NES for retro gamers. The Famulator comes with two controllers with the typical A &amp; B buttons but an add on with AA &amp; BB which are just double the speed of the regular A &amp; B buttons. The Famulator also includes the normal cables for connection to a display.</p><h3>Sharp Famicom Twin</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1705458" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.57666666666667" data-width="600" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 600px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:57.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1705458"><img alt="Sharp Famicom Twin" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg 600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="Sharp Famicom Twin" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg 600w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/12/121177/1705458-1554676_picture_006_super.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Sharp Famicom Twin</figcaption></figure><p>Sharp made their own version of the Famicom, which combines the Famicom and <a href="/famicom-disk-system/3045-91/" data-ref-id="3045-91">Famicom Disk System</a> in one unit. The system comes in two colors. Red with black highlights and black with red highlights. They later released a Turbo version in black with green highlights and red with blue/grey highlights. The Famicom Twin was licensed by Nintendo and also contains most of the original hardware with few changes.</p><h3>PolyStation</h3><p>The PolyStation was a unique clone released in China, designed to look like <a href="/sony-computer-entertainment-america/3010-313/" data-ref-id="3010-313">Sony</a>'s <a href="/playstation/3045-22/" data-ref-id="3045-22">PlayStation</a>, but emulate the NES's hardware. The PolyStation's packaging also falsely represented the product, showing screenshots from other consoles such as the <a href="/snes/3045-9/" data-ref-id="3045-9">SNES</a>, the Genesis, or the PlayStation, or describing advanced features that the NES is not capable of doing. The console has appeared under a variety of names, and sometimes even includes packaged pirated games.</p><h3>Dendy</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843377-dendy1.gif" data-ref-id="1300-1843377" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.1764705882353" data-width="170" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 170px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:117.6%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843377-dendy1.gif" data-ref-id="1300-1843377"><img alt="The Dendy logo" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 170px) 100vw, 170px" data-width="170" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843377-dendy1.gif" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843377-dendy1.gif 170w"><noscript><img alt="The Dendy logo" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843377-dendy1.gif" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1843377-dendy1.gif 170w" sizes="(max-width: 170px) 100vw, 170px" data-width="170"></noscript></a><figcaption>The Dendy logo</figcaption></figure><p>One of the more popular clones of the NES, the Dendy rose to popularity in Eastern Europe in particular due to the fact that an official NES never made it to that region, and importation was expensive. Over two million Dendy consoles sold in Russia by 1996, many for under the equivalent of $50 USD. The Dendy differs slightly from the NES in terms of hardware, containing a slightly different chipset. There were several variations of the Dendy, such as Dendy Classic and Dendy Junior. Cartridges were typically bootleg versions of official games, or even compilations, and were similar in appearance to the Famicom cartridges.</p>

<Platform 'PlayStation'>
PlayStation
1994-12-03 00:00:00
Sony Interactive Entertainment
299.00
102490000
<h2>Overview</h2><p>The Sony PlayStation (common abbreviations are PS, PS1, PSone, and PSX) is a video game console that was released by <a href="/sony-computer-entertainment-america/3010-313/" data-ref-id="3010-313">Sony Computer Entertainment</a>, in <a href="/japan/3035-37/" data-ref-id="3035-37">Japan</a> on December 3, 1994, and in <a href="/north-america/3035-1664/" data-ref-id="3035-1664">North America</a> on September 9, 1995. The PlayStation was the first of <a href="/sony-computer-entertainment-incorporated/3010-995/" data-ref-id="3010-995">Sony Computer Entertainment's</a> game consoles. The Playstation was the predecessor to the <a href="/playstation-2/3045-19/" data-ref-id="3045-19">PlayStation 2</a>, <a href="/psp/3045-18/" data-ref-id="3045-18">PlayStation Portable</a>, <a href="/playstation-3/3045-35/" data-ref-id="3045-35">PlayStation 3</a>, <a href="/playstation-vita/3045-129/" data-ref-id="3045-129">Playstation Vita, </a>and most recently the <a href="//www.giantbomb.com/playstation-4/3045-146/" data-ref-id="3045-146">PlayStation 4</a>, which was released in 2013.</p><h2>Development</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/13158/960638-gb.png" data-ref-id="1300-960638" data-size="medium" data-ratio="0.625" data-width="360" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 360px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:62.5%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/13158/960638-gb.png" data-ref-id="1300-960638"><img alt="No Caption Provided" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/13158/960638-gb.png" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/13158/960638-gb.png 360w" sizes="(max-width: 360px) 100vw, 360px" data-width="360"></a></figure><p>The development of the PlayStation actually began before the 16-bit era. At that time, <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a> wanted to experiment with the CD-ROM media for use on the <a href="/nintendo-entertainment-system/3045-21/" data-ref-id="3045-21">Famicom</a> (following the success of <a href="/nec-home-electronics-ltd/3010-2708/" data-ref-id="3010-2708">NEC</a>'s <a href="/turbografx-16/3045-55/" data-ref-id="3045-55">PC Engine</a> <a href="/turbografx-cd/3045-53/" data-ref-id="3045-53">CD-ROM</a> in Japan), but encountered a few problems. Nintendo finally decided to work with <a href="/sony-corporation/3010-3223/" data-ref-id="3010-3223">Sony</a>, one of the creators of the CD-ROM format (with Phillips) to produce a CD add-on to the <a href="/snes/3045-9/" data-ref-id="3045-9">Super Nintendo</a>, named the SNES-CD. A contract was signed in 1988, and Sony began work on the product. Initially, the partnership was supposed to be announced at CES 1991, but while reviewing the first contract, <a href="/hiroshi-yamauchi/3040-615/" data-ref-id="3040-615">Hiroshi Yamauchi</a> found out that Sony would entirely handle every title release on the console. <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a> then secretly cancelled the deal. At CES 1991, shortly after Sony announced their "<a href="http://news.google.com/newspapers?nid=1243&amp;dat=19910602&amp;id=MQIrAAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=joYDAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=7064,6792388" rel="nofollow">Play Station</a>" add-on for the SNES, Nintendo <a href="http://news.google.com/newspapers?nid=1499&amp;dat=19910625&amp;id=G6AaAAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=bSwEAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=2823,1014416" rel="nofollow">announced</a> its cancellation and that they were working to bring Nintendo franchises on Phillips own console, the <a href="/cd-i/3045-27/" data-ref-id="3045-27">CD-i</a>. This proved to be a bad decision on Nintendo's part as every game that used those properties were poorly received by both gamers and critics.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1238417-ken_kutaragi.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1238417" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.2313253012048" data-width="415" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 415px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:123.1%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1238417-ken_kutaragi.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1238417"><img alt="Ken Kutaragi" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1238417-ken_kutaragi.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1238417-ken_kutaragi.jpg 415w" sizes="(max-width: 519px) 100vw, 519px" data-width="519"></a><figcaption>Ken Kutaragi</figcaption></figure><p><a href="/ken-kutaragi/3040-48015/" data-ref-id="3040-48015">Ken Kutaragi</a>, president of Sony later dubbed the The Father of the PlayStation, was angered by the turn of events. He even declared that they would bury Nintendo. After this, Sony considered abandoning the whole project, which would have lost millions of dollars. Instead, they decided to take everything they learned while developing the console to create their own stand-alone video game system. In 1992, Sony signed a deal that would allow their new console to still be able to play SNES games, with Nintendo maintaining the rights to the game. However, Sony decided to abandon this idea and began reworking their concept for a video game system. They now were aiming for the next-generation of consoles, making the contract they signed with Nintendo irrelevant. Nintendo tried to prevent the release of the PlayStation with a lawsuit in 1991, claiming they essentially owned the name, but this tactic did not work.</p><p>The PlayStation was originally intended to be a mainly <a href="/2d/3015-1427/" data-ref-id="3015-1427">2D</a>-focused system. However, after Sony designers saw the huge success of <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a>'s <a href="/virtua-fighter/3030-15584/" data-ref-id="3030-15584">Virtua Fighter</a> (developed by <a href="/yu-suzuki/3040-15149/" data-ref-id="3040-15149">Yu Suzuki</a> and his Sega <a href="/sega-am2/3010-81/" data-ref-id="3010-81">AM2</a> team) at Japanese <a href="/arcade/3045-84/" data-ref-id="3045-84">arcades</a> in 1993, they decided to make the PlayStation a mainly <a href="/polygonal-3d/3015-1430/" data-ref-id="3015-1430">3D</a>-focused system (see <a href="http://www.wired.com/2012/09/how-virtua-fighter-saved-playstations-bacon/" rel="nofollow" data-ref-id="false">here</a> for more details).</p><p>The final version of the PlayStation took about 6 months to develop. Selling the console to publishers and developers was not an easy task for two reasons. One, Sony had to contend with the success of Nintendo and Sega at that time. Two, the PlayStation was a CD-based video game system, and so far none of them had been successful outside of Japan. Yet, when Sony showed them tech demos of the 3D capabilities of the system, gaming publishers and developers were sold on the console. At <a href="/e3-1995/3015-3482/" data-ref-id="3015-3482">E3 1995</a>, the first Electronic Entertainment Expo, Sony announced the price point of $299.</p><h2>Launch</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426394" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.0841487279843" data-width="511" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 511px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:108.4%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426394"><img alt="PS Launch Packaging" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 590px) 100vw, 590px" data-width="590" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg 511w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg 480w"><noscript><img alt="PS Launch Packaging" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg 511w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1992/1426394-system.jpg 480w" sizes="(max-width: 590px) 100vw, 590px" data-width="590"></noscript></a><figcaption>PS Launch Packaging</figcaption></figure><p>The console was first released in Japan, on December 3rd 1994, over 9 months before it would appear officially in the west. This fueled a massive import market in Europe and the US prior to it's official release there. The release date of the console in North America was September 9th 1995, in order to compete with the <a href="/saturn/3045-42/" data-ref-id="3045-42">Sega Saturn</a> and its supposed Saturnday September 2nd release. Its launch was accompanied by the advertising campaign U R Not E with the E printed in red, symbolizing the word ready. Launch titles were:</p><ul><li><a href="/battle-arena-toshinden/3030-5238/" data-ref-id="3030-5238">Battle Arena Toshinden</a></li><li><a href="/espn-extreme-games/3030-1870/" data-ref-id="3030-1870">ESPN Extreme Games</a></li><li><a href="/kileak-the-dna-imperative/3030-14799/" data-ref-id="3030-14799">Kileak: The DNA Imperative</a></li><li><a href="/nba-jam-tournament-edition/3030-14792/" data-ref-id="3030-14792">NBA JAM Tournament Edition</a></li><li><a href="/power-serve-3d-tennis/3030-18631/" data-ref-id="3030-18631">Power Serve 3D Tennis</a></li><li><a href="/the-raiden-project/3030-15131/" data-ref-id="3030-15131">The Raiden Project</a></li><li><a href="/rayman/3030-18473/" data-ref-id="3030-18473">Rayman</a></li><li><a href="/ridge-racer/3030-2158/" data-ref-id="3030-2158">Ridge Racer</a></li><li><a href="/street-fighter-the-movie/3030-3760/" data-ref-id="3030-3760">Street Fighter: The Movie</a></li><li><a href="/total-eclipse-turbo/3030-22915/" data-ref-id="3030-22915">Total Eclipse Turbo</a></li></ul><p>The European release followed soon after, on September 29th, by which time more games were already available for purchase, including the enigmatic <a href="/wipeout/3030-9707/" data-ref-id="3030-9707">Wipeout</a>. A platform exclusive title, that would take center stage in securing the 'cool' image of the Playstation brand.</p><p>The PlayStation had an interesting pre-order plan; people who reserved the console before its release would receive a compact disc which could be played in any standard Audio-CD player. When the consumer received their PlayStation, putting the compact disc in the console would reveal it was a demo disc; the first in a very long line. While the launch line-up for the PlayStation wasnt the strongest ever, the following month would prove to be very important with the release of big titles, such as <a href="/warhawk/3030-17169/" data-ref-id="3030-17169">Warhawk</a>, <a href="/twisted-metal/3030-12318/" data-ref-id="3030-12318">Twisted Metal</a>, and of course, <a href="/tekken/3030-10081/" data-ref-id="3030-10081">Tekken</a>.</p><h2>History</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1228002-bernie.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1228002" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.3017543859649" data-width="285" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 285px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:130.2%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1228002-bernie.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1228002"><img alt="Bernie Stolar" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 285px) 100vw, 285px" data-width="285" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1228002-bernie.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1228002-bernie.jpg 285w"><noscript><img alt="Bernie Stolar" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1228002-bernie.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/16944/1228002-bernie.jpg 285w" sizes="(max-width: 285px) 100vw, 285px" data-width="285"></noscript></a><figcaption>Bernie Stolar</figcaption></figure><p>Before the system was launched, <a href="/bernard-stolar/3040-33719/" data-ref-id="3040-33719">Bernard Bernie Stolar</a> was made the first executive vice-president of <a href="/sony-computer-entertainment-america/3010-313/" data-ref-id="3010-313">SCEA</a>. With him, he installed policies for games that were eligible release to the PlayStation in North America: 2D games and RPGs were not allowed. Stolar felt that 2-dimensonal games should be excluded because the console was made especially for 3D gaming, and because of the 16-bit was near its end and 2D gaming was heavily associated with it. Stolar felt that RPGs should not be released in the US because the US audience did not enjoy that type of game, and sales in North America proved this. Stolar would leave SCEA after the first holiday of the system's existence. He moved to <a href="/sega/3010-62/" data-ref-id="3010-62">Sega</a> and began work on its new-console, the <a href="/saturn/3045-42/" data-ref-id="3045-42">Saturn</a>, with those same policies. Stolar's departure from SCEA opened the door to one of the most important games of the PlayStation era: <a href="/final-fantasy-vii/3030-13053/" data-ref-id="3030-13053">Final Fantasy VII</a>.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1204996" data-size="small" data-ratio="1" data-width="640" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 640px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:100.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1204996"><img alt="Final Fantasy VII" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 640px) 100vw, 640px" data-width="640" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg 480w"><noscript><img alt="Final Fantasy VII" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9253/1204996-ffvii_us_ps_front.jpg 480w" sizes="(max-width: 640px) 100vw, 640px" data-width="640"></noscript></a><figcaption>Final Fantasy VII</figcaption></figure><p>Before Final Fantasy VII, <a href="/square-enix/3010-104/" data-ref-id="3010-104">Square</a> developed every game of the <a href="/final-fantasy/3025-194/" data-ref-id="3025-194">Final Fantasy series</a> for the Nintendo consoles. When Nintendo released the <a href="/nintendo-64/3045-43/" data-ref-id="3045-43">Nintendo 64</a>, Square was skeptical of its cartridge system; cartridges cost more to produce and had less storage space than a CD-Rom. Considering the costs of their new project, Square had to make a hard decision and announced on January 12th, 1996 that they would only develop for Sony consoles from now on. This policy remained in place until the release of <a href="/final-fantasy-crystal-chronicles/3030-15672/" data-ref-id="3030-15672">Final Fantasy: Crystal Chronicles</a>, in 2003. Final Fantasy VII was released in Japan January 31st 1997, and helped the PlayStation achieve mainstream popularity (serving as a killer app), it would impact pop culture in both the <a href="/the-united-states-of-america/3035-396/" data-ref-id="3035-396">United States</a> and Japan. The game also helped RPG's gain mainstream popularity, and inspired many role-playing games released during the following years.</p><h2>PSone</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426395" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.71282798833819" data-width="686" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 686px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:71.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426395"><img alt="PSone Packaging / Screen" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg 686w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="PSone Packaging / Screen" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg 686w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1426395-system_psone.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>PSone Packaging / Screen</figcaption></figure><p>On July 7, 2000, Sony released a new version of its console under the name PSone. The console was 50% smaller than the original PlayStation, and went on to outsell every other system on the market from throughout the rest of the year, including the <a href="/playstation-2/3045-19/" data-ref-id="3045-19">PlayStation 2</a>, released on October 26th. In fact, the PlayStation became the first video game console to ship 100 million units worldwide. The system was finally discontinued on March 23rd 2006, more than ten years after its launch. In the end, the PlayStation sold 102 million units, with 28 million being from the PSone redesign; thus, judging by sales and units sold, it clearly won the fifth generation of video game consoles.</p><p>Eventually the PSone received a LCD screen add-on, which made the system semi-portable.</p><h2>Controller</h2><h3>Original Controller</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8620/281217-playstation_controller.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-281217" data-size="small" data-ratio="0.69666666666667" data-width="300" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 300px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:69.7%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8620/281217-playstation_controller.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-281217"><img alt="Original Controller" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8620/281217-playstation_controller.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8620/281217-playstation_controller.jpg 300w"><noscript><img alt="Original Controller" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8620/281217-playstation_controller.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/8620/281217-playstation_controller.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300"></noscript></a><figcaption>Original Controller</figcaption></figure><p>The PlayStation controller was the most symmetrical of the game controllers at the time of its release and it still is in the current generation of game consoles. The use of shapes labels is unique to the PlayStation controller with a square, triangle, cross, and circle on the right side of the controller. The left side of the controller has a standard D-pad and on top of the controller is two buttons on the right and left side (L1, L2, R1, R2). After the release of the Dual Analog controller, there were two analog sticks, one for each of the gamer's thumbs.</p><h3>History</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426388-acc_ds.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426388" data-size="small" data-ratio="1.2785923753666" data-width="341" data-embed-type="image" style="width: 341px"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:127.9%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426388-acc_ds.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426388"><img alt="PS DualShock Controller" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 500px) 100vw, 500px" data-width="500" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426388-acc_ds.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426388-acc_ds.jpg 341w"><noscript><img alt="PS DualShock Controller" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426388-acc_ds.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426388-acc_ds.jpg 341w" sizes="(max-width: 500px) 100vw, 500px" data-width="500"></noscript></a><figcaption>PS DualShock Controller</figcaption></figure><p>The core of the original PlayStation controller design is still used today by the PlayStation 2 and PlayStation 3. The changes to the controller over the 13 years since the original PlayStation launched have been relatively minor. Part way through the life cycle of the PlayStation, Sony introduced the Dual Analog controller, then the <a href="/dualshock-controller/3000-1/" data-ref-id="3000-1">DualShock controller</a>. The DualShock controller worked on both PlayStation and PlayStation 2. When the PlayStation 3 launched, the controller became wireless and gained the use of the <a href="/sixaxis-controller/3000-9/" data-ref-id="3000-9">Sixaxis</a> but lost the vibration function that gamers had become familiar with. This was a result of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immersion_v._Sony" rel="nofollow">litigation that Sony faced over the vibration technology</a>. Later, with the legal troubles settled, Sony released the <a href="/dualshock-3/3000-29/" data-ref-id="3000-29">DualShock 3</a> that reintroduced the vibration function.</p><h2>PocketStation</h2><p>A few years after the PlayStation was released, <a href="/sony-computer-entertainment-incorporated/3010-995/" data-ref-id="3010-995">Sony</a> released the <a href="/pocketstation-support/3015-7661/" data-ref-id="3015-7661">PocketStation</a>, a portable accessory that had an infared port to transfer game saves and info to other PocketStations. It could also download features for PS1 games that weren't featured on the console version.</p><h2>Hardware Specifications</h2><h3>CPU</h3><p><b>Processor</b></p><ul><li>R3000A 32-bit RISC at 33.8MHz</li><li>Operation Cycle: 30 MIPS</li><li>Bandwidth Bus: 123MB</li><li>Instructional Cache: 4KB</li><li>SRAM Data Cache: 1KB</li></ul><p><b>Co Processors</b></p><p>Geometry Transformation Engine</p><ul><li>Operation Cycle: 66 MIPS</li><li>180,000 Texture Mapped Polygons Per-Second</li><li>360,000 Flat-Shaded Polygons Per-Second</li></ul><p>Data Decompression Engine</p><ul><li>Operation Cycle 80 MIPS</li><li>MJEPG and H. 261 File Decoding</li><li>Direct CPU Bus Connection</li></ul><p><b>Note</b>: The Data Decompression Engine is mainly used to cut back on game loading times.</p><p><b>Graphics</b></p><ul><li>Maximum Colors: 16.7 Million (Tru-Color)</li><li>Maximum Sprites: 4000</li><li>2D Scaling and Rotation</li><li>3D Texture Mapping and Shading</li><li>Transparency and Fading</li><li>Screen Resolution: 256 x 224 - 640 x 480</li><li>Possible AV Outputs: Composite, S-Video, Component (RGB)</li></ul><p><b>Sound</b></p><ul><li>Custom Sound Processing Unit</li><li>24 Channel ADPCM</li><li>Sample Rate 44.1KHz</li></ul><p><b>Memory</b></p><ul><li>Main Memory: 2MB</li><li>Video Memory: 1MB</li><li>Sound Memory: 512K</li><li>Operating System Memory: 512K</li><li>CD Rom Cache Memory: 32K</li></ul><p><b>CD-Rom</b></p><ul><li>XA Mode 2 Capable</li><li>CD-DA Capable (allows Audio CD Playback)</li><li>2x Functioning Speed</li></ul><h2>Best Selling Games</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/13158/1150989-gb.png" data-ref-id="1300-1150989" data-size="small" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>Gran Turismo</figcaption></figure><p>The following are the top 10 best selling games for the Playstation that have sold or shipped at least one million <h2>Overview</h2><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426256-logo.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426256" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0"><a  href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426256-logo.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1426256"><img alt="The Nintendo GameCube and its logo" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426256-logo.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1426256-logo.jpg 0w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" data-width="300"></a><figcaption>The Nintendo GameCube and its logo</figcaption></figure><p>The Nintendo GameCube is <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a>'s successor to the <a href="/nintendo-64/3045-43/" data-ref-id="3045-43">Nintendo 64</a> and predecessor to the <a href="/wii/3045-36/" data-ref-id="3045-36">Nintendo Wii</a>. The GameCube was released in Japan on September 14, 2001, in North America on November 18, 2001, in Europe on May 3, 2002, and in Australia on May 17, 2002.</p><p>The GameCube sold a total of 21.74 million units worldwide, coming in third place in the so-called "console war" slightly behind <a href="/microsoft-game-studios/3010-340/" data-ref-id="3010-340">Microsoft</a>'s <a href="/xbox/3045-32/" data-ref-id="3045-32">Xbox</a>, but far behind <a href="/sony-corporation/3010-3223/" data-ref-id="3010-3223">Sony</a>'s <a href="/playstation-2/3045-19/" data-ref-id="3045-19">PlayStation 2</a>. The console is officially abbreviated to the acronym "GCN" ("<b>G</b>ame<b>C</b>ube by <b>N</b>intendo").</p><p>A version of the GameCube with DVD playback capability was released by <a href="/panasonic-interactive-media/3010-1546/" data-ref-id="3010-1546">Panasonic</a>, but the "Panasonic Q Edition" GameCubes weren't available outside of Japan.</p><figure data-align="left" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/12813/856056-panasonic_q.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-856056" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.75"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/12813/856056-panasonic_q.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-856056"><img alt="The Panasonic Q Gamecube was released only in Japan." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/12813/856056-panasonic_q.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/1/12813/856056-panasonic_q.jpg 500w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/1/12813/856056-panasonic_q.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/1/12813/856056-panasonic_q.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></a><figcaption>The Panasonic Q Gamecube was released only in Japan.</figcaption></figure><p>The GameCube also saw a fair share of games, although its most popular games were first-party; <a href="/super-mario-sunshine/3030-6649/" data-ref-id="3030-6649">Super Mario Sunshine</a>, <a href="/the-legend-of-zelda-the-wind-waker/3030-18508/" data-ref-id="3030-18508">The Legend of Zelda: The Wind Waker</a>, <a href="/metroid-prime/3030-15473/" data-ref-id="3030-15473">Metroid Prime</a>, <a href="/f-zero-gx/3030-20448/" data-ref-id="3030-20448">F-Zero GX</a>, and <a href="/animal-crossing/3030-12893/" data-ref-id="3030-12893">Animal Crossing</a>, to name a few. Games released only on the GameCube at the time of release have a triangle in the upper left corner that says "Only For," implicating that the game was only available on the GameCube. The final "major release" on the GameCube was also first-party, with <a href="/the-legend-of-zelda-twilight-princess/3030-19467/" data-ref-id="3030-19467">The Legend of Zelda: Twilight Princess</a> being one of the most highly-anticipated titles in the console's run, though it is more well known for the version that was ported to the Wii.</p><p>As games were no longer on cartridges Nintendo released a series of memory cards where players would save their progress. Memory cards are available in 59 block (of which several were packaged with games including Animal Crossing), a 256 block, and a 1024 block versions.</p><h3>Marketing</h3><p>The first advertising campaign for the Nintendo GameCube was simply a rotating cube that would morph into the GameCube logo. A voice would then whisper, "GameCube." This was usually shown at the end of a commercial for a GameCube game.</p><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1844595-download.jpeg" data-ref-id="1300-1844595" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.75"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1844595-download.jpeg" data-ref-id="1300-1844595"><img alt="A wallpaper for the "Who Are You?" campaign." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1844595-download.jpeg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/7465/1844595-download.jpeg 1024w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_super/0/7465/1844595-download.jpeg 960w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/7465/1844595-download.jpeg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/7465/1844595-download.jpeg 320w"><noscript><img alt="A wallpaper for the "></noscript></a><figcaption>A wallpaper for the "Who Are You?" campaign.</figcaption></figure><p>Another well known advertising campaign was the "Who Are You?" advertising campaign. This was used to market a wide variety of Nintendo games. The "Who Are You?" logo was graffiti lettering. Most of the commercials and print ads were games developed or published by Nintendo, but some third-party developers were able to use the campaign as well. The idea behind the campaign was that players were the character that they played, with print ads showing pieces of the main character taped onto a photograph of a person (e.g. the helmet of Samus Aran taped onto a ballerina to promote Metroid Prime).</p><p>There was a distinct difference in the way Nintendo of Japan marketed the GameCube and how it was marketed in western markets. Although in western markets, the GameCube was advertised as a console for experienced gamers; in Japan, it was marketed to appeal to younger and newer players, as a way of gaining back the child audience they lost to their competitors in the previous console cycle. However, it failed, and the GameCube was a commercial flop in Japan. However, due to being marketed to a different audience outside Japan, the GameCube became a very successful console in other countries.</p><h2>Hardware</h2><p>Due to pressures from developers, the GameCube was <a href="/nintendo/3010-90/" data-ref-id="3010-90">Nintendo</a>'s first console to introduce a disc medium instead of cartridges, the games were recorded on mini DVDs. The disc can hold 1.5 gigabytes of data - 190 times more than what an <a href="/nintendo-64/3045-43/" data-ref-id="3045-43">N64</a> game cartridge can hold. Technologically speaking, the GameCube was more powerful than the <a href="/playstation-2/3045-19/" data-ref-id="3045-19">PS2</a> at about half the size of the <a href="/xbox/3045-32/" data-ref-id="3045-32">Xbox</a>.</p><h3>Colour Schemes</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393160-gamecube_1_1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-393160" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="1.0327022375215"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:103.3%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393160-gamecube_1_1.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-393160"><img alt="The GameCube's hardware used techniques to remain small and powerful." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 619px) 100vw, 619px" data-width="619" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393160-gamecube_1_1.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393160-gamecube_1_1.jpg 581w"><noscript><img alt="The GameCube's hardware used techniques to remain small and powerful." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393160-gamecube_1_1.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393160-gamecube_1_1.jpg 581w" sizes="(max-width: 619px) 100vw, 619px" data-width="619"></noscript></a><figcaption>The GameCube's hardware used techniques to remain small and powerful.</figcaption></figure><p>Like Nintendo's previous console, the Nintendo 64, and many of the company's handheld consoles, the GameCube was released in a variety of colours. The two most common colours were Indigo and Jet Black (Indigo being the default color). Later, a Platinum color scheme was released and marketed as limited edition. "Orange Spice" GameCubes were also made available, but were primarily available in Japan. The GameCube's model numbers (DOL-001 and DOL-101) were a reference to Dolphin, the GameCube's codename. Official accessories and peripherals also had model numbers beginning with DOL.</p><h3>Peripherals</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1072721" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.75"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-1072721"><img alt="The GameCube has four controller ports." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="The GameCube has four controller ports." src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/1992/1072721-nintendo01.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>The GameCube has four controller ports.</figcaption></figure><p>The GameCube allows direct connectivity with Nintendos handheld, the <a href="/game-boy-advance/3045-4/" data-ref-id="3045-4">Game Boy Advance</a>, via a link cable. Later on, Nintendo released the Game Boy Player which allowed backward compatibility with <a href="/game-boy/3045-3/" data-ref-id="3045-3">Game Boy</a>, <a href="/game-boy-color/3045-57/" data-ref-id="3045-57">Game Boy Color</a> and Game Boy Advance games. There were also some games that supported <a href="/e-reader/3015-3782/" data-ref-id="3015-3782">e-Reader</a> connectivity, allowing players to scan in extra game features. Supported games include Animal Crossing and <a href="/pokemon-channel/3030-19224/" data-ref-id="3030-19224">Pokmon Channel</a>.</p><h3>Online</h3><p>Although only a few games have online play for the GameCube (such as the <a href="/phantasy-star-online-episode-i-ii-plus/3030-11701/" data-ref-id="3030-11701">Phantasy Star Online</a> franchise), the GameCube offers LAN support that allows eight GameCubes to be connected together to play a multiplayer game. Using this, fans created unofficial online services for the GameCube, such as Warp Pipe, that used LAN technology for online play.</p><h3><a href="/gamecube-controller/3000-47/" data-ref-id="3000-47">Controller</a></h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-811125" data-size="medium" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.61356466876972"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:61.4%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-811125"><img alt="A standard GameCube controller" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg 634w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg 480w"><noscript><img alt="A standard GameCube controller" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg 634w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/3715/811125-gamecube_controller.jpg 480w" sizes="(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px" data-width="480"></noscript></a><figcaption>A standard GameCube controller</figcaption></figure><p>The inputs on a GameCube controller consist of a large, green A-button, a small, red B-button, two gray X- and Y- buttons, two analog sticks (with the larger being gray and simply called "the analogue stick" and the smaller being yellow and called "the C-stick", in reference to the C-buttons on the N64 controller), a D-pad, two shoulder buttons, and a purple Z-button on top of the controller next to the right shoulder button. Some Wii games <a href="/gamecube-controller-support-on-wii/3015-2523/" data-ref-id="3015-2523">support the use of the GameCube controller</a> (this is taken advantage of in Virtual Console and WiiWare games as well).</p><p>The wireless version of the controller was called the Wavebird. It allowed players to play from up to 20 feet away. It had the same button layout than the original controller except for an on and off button and a bulkier feel. The controller came with a wireless receiver. To play, the player had to tune his controller to one of the 16 radio channels that the little wheel at the bottom of the controller offered. Then, he had to put the same channel number on the receiver, plug the receiver into the GameCube's controller socket, turn on his controller and a light on the receiver would tell the player that it was receiving a signal. The controller worked with two AA batteries and in order to conserve battery life, <a href="/contextual-controller-rumble/3015-3502/" data-ref-id="3015-3502">rumble</a> is not included. It was released in summer of 2002 for the price of 34.95 dollars.</p><h2>Technical Specifications</h2><h3>Central Processing Unit</h3><figure data-align="right" data-img-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-393161" data-size="small" data-embed-type="image" data-ratio="0.75"><a class="fluid-height" style="padding-bottom:75.0%" href="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg" data-ref-id="1300-393161"><img alt="The GameCube unmasked" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320" class="js-lazy-load-image" data-src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg" data-srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg 320w"><noscript><img alt="The GameCube unmasked" src="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg" srcset="http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/original/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg 640w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_medium/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg 480w, http://static.giantbomb.com/uploads/scale_small/0/9566/393161-http_www_ichdertom_de_grafik_gamecube_dsc00125_jpg_640_480_80.jpg 320w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" data-width="320"></noscript></a><figcaption>The GameCube unmasked</figcaption></figure><ul><li>435 MHz IBM Gekko PowerPC CPU</li><li>PowerPC 750CXe based core</li><li>180 nm IBM copper-wire process</li><li>50 new vector instructions</li><li>32-bit ALU 64-bit FPU, usable as 2x32-bit SIMD</li><li>FPU:1.9Gflops</li><li>64-bit enhanced PowerPC 60x front side bus to GPU/chipset, 162 MHz clock, 1.3 GB/s peak bandwidth</li><li>64 KiB L1 cache (32 KiB I/32 KiB D), 8-way associative, 256 KiB on-die L2 cache, 2-way associative</li><li>1125 DMIPS (dhrystone 2.1)</li></ul><h3>System Memory</h3><ul><li>43 MB total non-unified RAM</li><li>24 MB MoSys 1T-SRAM main system RAM, 324 MHz, 64-bit bus, 2.7 GB/s bandwidth</li><li>3 MB embedded 1T-SRAM</li><li>Split into 1 MB texture buffer and 2 MB frame buffer</li><li>10.4 GB/s texture bandwidth (peak), 7.6 GB/s frame buffer bandwidth (peak), about 6.2 ns latency</li><li>16 MB DRAM used as buffer for DVD drive and audio, 81 MHz, 8-bit bus, 81 MB/s bandwidth</li></ul><h3>Connectivity</h3><ul><li>4 controller ports, 2 memory card slots</li><li>MultiAV analog audiovisual port: interlaced YPbPr (composite, Y/C() and RGB video, stereophonic analog audio</li><li>Digital audiovisual port: digital interlaced or progressive scan YCBCR video, stereophonic IS sound</li><li>Resolutions: 480i, 576i, 480p</li><li>High-speed Serial Ports: 2</li><li>High-speed Parallel Ports: 1</li><li>Power supply output: DC12 volts x 3.25 amperes</li><li>Physical measurements: 110 mm (H) x 150 mm (W) x 161 mm (D); [4.3"(h) x 5.9"(W) x 6.3"(D)]</li></ul><h3>Graphics Processing Unit</h3><ul><li>162 MHz "Flipper" LSI, 180 nm NEC eDRAM-compatible process, Co-developed by Nintendo and ArtX</li><li>8Gflops</li><li>4 pixel pipelines with 1 texture unit each</li><li>TEV "Texture EnVironment" engine</li><li>Fixed-function hardware transform and lighting (T&amp;L), 12 million polygons/s in-game</li><li>648 megapixels/s (162 MHz x 4 pipelines), 648 megatexels/s (648 MPx 1 texture units) (peak)</li><li>Peak triangle performance: 20,250,000 32pixel triangles/s raw and with 1 texture and lit</li><li>337,500 triangles a frame at 60fps</li><li>675,000 triangles a frame at 30fps</li><li>8 texture layers per pass, texture compression, full scene anti-aliasing</li><li>8 simultaneous hardware light</li><li>Bilinear, trilinear, and anisotrophic texture filtering</li><li>Multi-texturing, bump mapping, reflection mapping, 24-bit z-buffer</li><li>24-bit RGB / 32-bit RGBA color depth</li><li>720 x 480 interlaced or progressive scan</li><li>Integrated audio processor: Custom 81 MHz Macronix DSP</li><li>Instruction Memory: 8 KiB RAM, 8 KiB ROM</li><li>Data Memory: 8 KiB RAM, 4 KiB ROM</li><li>64 channels 16-bit 48 kHz ADPCM</li><li>Dolby Pro Logic II encoded within stereophonic output</li></ul><h3>Storage Media</h3><ul><li>Matsushita (2.000 MB/s-3.125 MB/s) CAV mini-DVD-like 8 cm optical disk, average access time: 128 ms; capacity: 1.5 GB</li><li>Memory cards of 59 blocks (4Mbit), 256 blocks (16 Mbit), 1024 blocks (64 Mbit), and 2043 (128Mbit) blocks.</li></ul><h2>Launch Titles</h2><h3>Japan - September 14, 2001</h3><ul><li><a href="/luigis-mansion/3030-10996/" data-ref-id="3030-10996">Luigi's Mansion</a></li><li><a href="/super-monkey-ball/3030-7247/" data-ref-id="3030-7247">Super Monkey Ball</a></li><li><a href="/wave-race-blue-storm/3030-11793/" data-ref-id="3030-11793">Wave Race: Blue Storm</a></li></ul><h3>North America - November 18, 2001</h3><ul><li><a Racing</a></li></ul><h2>Easter Eggs</h2><ul><li>Starting the console with no disc in the tray plays <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aFpw2kHnSuM" rel="nofollow">the Gamecube menu music</a>. It's a looping string of high, ambient notes. This piece of music sped up to 16 times its original tempo is the <a href="/famicom-disk-system/3045-91/" data-ref-id="3045-91">Famicom Disk System</a> startup tune.</li><li>Holding down the Z button while starting up the system will replace the typical noises with the noise of giggling children and squeaking rubber.</li><li>Holding down the Z button on all four controllers while starting up the system will replace the typical noises with noises such as a woodblock, drums, and a "hwaaa!"</li></ul>
